Memory and Continual Learning: Engram's Dan Biderman and Jessy Lin

Sequoia Capital(226.0K)·44:52·7.0K views·2026-06-24
Dan BidermanCo-founder, Engram Jessy LinCo-founder, Engram
TL;DREngram is a neolab founded by Dan Biderman and Jessy Lin that is shifting AI memory away from bloated context windows and RAG toward "always training" model weights.
By baking a team’s private context directly into white-box model weights, Engram enables personalized,...

TL;DR

Engram is a neolab founded by Dan Biderman and Jessy Lin that is shifting AI memory away from bloated context windows and RAG toward "always training" model weights. By baking a team’s private context directly into white-box model weights, Engram enables personalized, continuously learning models that can match frontier performance while using up to 100x fewer tokens.

The Core Premise: Memory and "Always Training" Weights

  • Continual Learning over Context Engineering: Rather than relying on massive context windows, complex prompting, or external databases, Engram focuses on "continual learning"—constantly training new, evolving contexts directly into model weights.
      • *The Employee Analogy:* The goal is for a model to deeply understand a company's internal priorities, culture, and workflows the way a human employee of several years does, rather than reading files from scratch at test time.
  • Targeting the True AI Bottleneck: Co-founder Jessy Lin argues that the primary bottleneck for AI utility today is not raw intelligence, but rather a model's ability to digest, adapt to, and act on bespoke, evolving organizational context.
  • Workspace-Level Training: Engram is focusing its initial efforts on training per-team models within collaborative workspaces. They are currently partnering with platforms like Microsoft, Notion, and Harvey to capture deep, multi-turn team interactions.

Technical Implementation & the 100x Efficiency Payoff

  • Adapter-Based Architecture: Engram performs fine-tuning on open-source or white-box transformer models using a variety of lightweight techniques, including Low-Rank Adaptations (LoRAs), prefix-tuning, and sparse architectures.
  • Dramatic Token Reduction: By training contextual knowledge directly into the weights, users avoid sending massive files and lengthy system prompts during inference.
      • *The 100x Metric:* A fine-tuned model can answer in 100 tokens what a frontier model would require 100,000 tokens of context to process, driving up to a 100x reduction in token consumption.
  • Bypassing the KV Cache Bottleneck:
      • *The Bottleneck:* Keeping massive contexts in a key-value (KV) cache is highly memory-inefficient. For a 70B parameter Llama model, storing a KV cache for a single long article can consume up to 80 GB of High Bandwidth Memory (HBM)—nearly as much as the entire 100 GB model itself.
      • *The Solution:* Engram uses offline compute to compress this contextual data into model weights via gradient descent, making the representation up to 1,000x smaller and significantly faster to load than a standard KV cache.

Internalization vs. Externalization (The "RAG Killer")

  • A Hybrid Future: While the founders describe their approach as a "RAG killer" for repetitive tasks, they view internalized memory (weights) and externalized memory (RAG) as complementary tools.
      • *Internalized (Weights):* Ideal for implicit knowledge, organizational priorities, bespoke command-line tools, and brand styles.
      • *Externalized (RAG/Search):* Ideal for highly volatile facts that change constantly and do not require conceptual understanding (e.g., a temporary room number).
  • Unlocking Associative Intuition: A model relying solely on RAG cannot make proactive, abstract connections. Weight-based training allows the model to form conceptual associations and surface relevant insights across a team's history without being explicitly prompted to search.

A Contrarian Vision: A Model for Everyone

  • Divergence from Frontier Labs: While frontier labs (like OpenAI or Anthropic) dedicate their primary resources (P0) to building a single, massive, AGI-capable model, Engram envisions a decentralized world where every team and individual runs their own privately trained, specialized model.
  • The Research-Product Loop: Traditional setups isolate research teams (who train models) from product teams (who build prompts on top). Engram integrates the two, ensuring user interactions immediately and safely feed back into the model's ongoing training pipeline.
  • The "ChatGPT Moment" for Memory: The founders believe the next major breakthrough will be a model that functions like a true intern—consistently and safely getting smarter day-by-day based on local feedback, without suffering from catastrophic forgetting.
"The bottleneck for making these models more useful these days is not really raw intelligence but understanding like new and evolving context." — Jessy Lin
"These kinds of things, the model can kind of implicitly learn by training on some data and answer, within 100 tokens, what the best frontier models would consume 100,000 tokens doing." — Dan Biderman
"If you are always doing RAG, you can't make associations... these kinds of associations can only happen in weights." — Jessy Lin
"We form these memories that happen to be neural memories, with models that happen to be personalized... It's not representing the file system as it is, it's representing a brain state of that file system." — Dan Biderman

Yeah, so other experiments, there have been many previous experiments of different architectures that we contributed to, like the state-space family and others to try and handle very, very long contexts more efficiently. The thing with all these methods, it ends up being a trade-off, usually a trade-off between memory and accuracy. And memory not in the behavioral, cognitive sense, memory in the computer sense, right? Instead of having, you know, the memory footprint of the transformer attention, which is quadratic in the sequence length, these models have... Some are claiming, you know, the sub-quadratic. Yeah, some are claiming, and some do have it, right, and some of the best Chinese models have layers that are, you know, inspired by those state-space architectures and are, you know, not quadratic in cost. Thing is, is that in our hands, we find that you always compromise accuracy for this memory. There's no free lunch. And what we're saying is like, look, if you really bitter-lesson pilled, what do you want to do is you want to think, how can I burn more compute, and how can I burn it on, you know, new contexts that I have not seen before? So, we're as bitter-lesson pilled as anyone else, and we are not betting that the overall direction of AGI is going to end anywhere soon. We just think there's more compute to scale, and if I really want to understand Sean and Sean's work and Sean's context, just like re-reading files is not going to make it, especially for a special person like you. It's special, derogatory. We got to train a 100-trillion parameter model for this guy. Cosine, cosine. What are you finding that people care most about their models learning? Like is it memorizing facts about the organization? Is it remembering like, "No, we do see it this way"? Is it like, what are people actually hoping to, and then maybe this feeds into how you do the ranking of memory slots and all that? Yeah. Well, I think if you look at what people are spending their time in the app layer doing these days, it's a lot of just trying to make the model work well for your use case. Like, "Oh, I want the model to, let's say, design my website with my brand style." Like, that's a very common example these days, but there's many kinds of different tasks that people do with agents, like learning how to run a workflow, or, you know, kind of your particular way of writing, let's say. So there's many, many kinds of things, and honestly, like I think when we think about these methods, kind of going back to this distinction between like facts and skills, there really is none. I think the methods are kind of agnostic to that. Yeah, to me, it's like, the natural thing. Almost all the app layers are basically, you know, a frontier model wrapped in a loop with search tools and stuff. And what they're all interested in doing with us is finding ways to kind of interface with their data in a way that's, you know, faster, more efficient, and also is more contextual. So, almost all of them, it's like, "We want to have our firm knowledge be encoded in something that's more efficient, that I don't have to research. We want to have the model know in a targeted way, who's the person I should triage a thing to." And we're just showing them that with pretty lightweight training, these things can be instinctual to the models. They don't have to have these very involved, long REPL loops to solve them. So, it's, in a sense, it's like, you know, it's a RAG killer kind of thing. Again, we can always do RAG and we can always retrieve, but that's the thing that people are interested in: interfacing with very large data planes and automating very repetitive things this way. And I want to double-click on this RAG killer thing, and I'm sorry to beat a dead horse, I just don't fully grok it yet. Is the premise that there's some trade-off between doing RAG versus updating your model weights? Is it the idea that you should be doing both? Like what types of things should be done in the weights versus what types of things should be externalized to RAG? I think it's, it's an unsolved problem. I don't think anyone has an answer to it; we're all working on it. It's also the fundamental question of like, in biological memory, what should be internalized versus what not. I do think that things that are like, you know, do you need to internalize the room number in a hotel that you were in like, a year ago? Probably no, not in your neural tissue—probably that's good to write down. But do you need to internalize the path-word to your home right now? Probably it's useful for the next few years to have that imprinted somewhere. So, yeah. How does this translate into like knowledge work and products? This is still something we figure out and we try to take the approach that we try to use as few heuristics as possible. It's easy to run filters on the data and say like, "I'm going to keep this, discard that, train on this, train on that," but as humans, you know, we watch TikTok and we get exposed to a lot of garbage, and still the brain is able to learn and not completely go off the rails, and we think models should be the same as well. Yeah. Maybe concretely in the short term, I think a lot of what people are worried about these days is the huge inference costs of running these agents like, for days on end. High inference cost is a good thing. I mean, consuming tokens for what? Sonia works with Fireworks. She really loves high inference. We love inference, too. Yeah. So, I think like, in the short term, I think that's the immediate pain point. Like, why are you reading the same files over and over again, you know, even in the same query? But definitely, you know, across people in the same company, they're running the same queries on the same documents over and over again, and that should be something the model just knows—like in the same way you ask an employee, they don't, you know, type into the search box like, "What was I working on yesterday?" They just know. But does caching kind of solve that? I think to some extent, yes, but I think going back to this question of what should be internalized versus what's, like, something you retrieve at test time, I think again, a lot of it is about building on your knowledge. So, if you are always doing RAG, you can't make associations like, "Oh, you know, I see somebody on the team is doing this kind of research, and I kind of recall at an abstract level, oh, there's this related thing that you might want to know about." You didn't even ask about it, right, but I think these kinds of associations can only happen in weights because they're not really about, "You asked me to search for this, I'm going to search for this." Yeah, and also, I think the main limitation with retrieval systems in general, and in AI specifically, is like: the problem is not so much what to store and where to put it; the problem is like, how to address it, like how to query the thing. Do you know what to look for, even? And this involves some sort of intuition that sometimes the models don't have, interestingly enough. They don't know where to look, and especially if you're, you know, limited to the current way of doing things, which is keyword search, that is just easier to scale in RL and least involved in terms of like infra for embeddings and stuff. So, yeah. Knowing what to search is something that's intuitive and can happen in the weights, and also about caching and inference. Like, much of this company started with us taking like a deep dive into like KV caches and caching. And this is a fascinating thing, right? KV cache is a monstrosity of the current way of doing things. Think of it: a KV cache for a single Wikipedia article, for some, you know, Taylor Swift or something like this, it will be like 80 gigabytes of HBM memory on the GPU, and, and entire Llama, it's for, say, a 70B Llama model. And the entire weights of the model would be about 100 gigabytes, and, you know, with some distortion, they remember the entire internet. And how come this thing is so... one thing is so bit-efficient, and this, we have this proof of existence that gradient descent can pack a lot of information in very few numbers, whereas this KV cache thing, you take a few tens of kilobytes of article and it becomes those 80 gigabytes of brain state. So, sure, you can cache this, you can load this, you will have issues with disk to HBM stuff, people are working on it, it's pretty interesting, but what if we can take those 80 gigabytes, spend some compute offline, maybe also in Fireworks, and file, but then compress it and make it really, really small so that the thing we load and cache is like 1,000x smaller? That would have tremendous implications for how we load things, how fast we can do things, and what the fidelity of the representation is. Super interesting. What are some of the things that could happen in the next year or two that would be like the ChatGPT moment of memory? Or do you think that that's not how things will play out? It's a good question. I don't know, I think like, the first proof of concept of the thing that people keep talking about with continual learning, which is you have an intern that you can teach things over time and actually gets better, I think everybody's waiting to see that. No matter how sophisticated the context engineering approaches are these days, they're not getting there. So, I think you need all these tools at your disposal to make that happen. Yeah, and it's important to say that the ChatGPT model was not anticipated. We just, you know, read about all the different product directions that certain people had before ChatGPT was different. I feel like to me, the example is like: look, if you resigned from your job today and your sole mission was to make a model that's better for you, and you would use OpenAI, Anthropic, and all these frontier models, and you would 24/7 engineer the context, your way to move the needle is very limited as an individual. You will just be better off waiting for the next version of the model, and you'll take it from there. The Latest on Industrial Clusters

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·35:07·310 views·2026-06-24
TL;DRIndustrial clusters serve as critical, co-located ecosystems for accelerating green and digital transitions through cross-industry collaboration and systemic planning.
The successful integration of technology, infrastructure, and policy—as demonstrated by China's pilot...

TL;DR

Industrial clusters serve as critical, co-located ecosystems for accelerating green and digital transitions through cross-industry collaboration and systemic planning. The successful integration of technology, infrastructure, and policy—as demonstrated by China's pilot initiatives—offers a highly scalable model for global industrial decarbonization and sustainable economic growth.

The Strategic Value of Industrial Clusters

  • Cross-Sectoral Convergence: Industrial clusters offer a unique spatial co-location where multi-industry and cross-sectoral players can collaborate. Solutions to complex energy and industrial transformations cannot be solved by single companies in isolation.
  • The WEF Global Network: The World Economic Forum (WEF) manages a network of 40 global industrial clusters—recently joined by Dalian—focused on accelerating the net-zero transition in an integrated, holistic manner.
  • Systemic Planning vs. Retrofitting:
    • Systemic Planning: Strategic planning ahead of time followed by precise execution minimizes friction and accelerates deployment.
    • Retrofitting: Implementing sustainable systems into existing legacy clusters is far more complex and requires adaptive, post-hoc design adjustments.

Dalian's Jinpu New Area: A Blueprint for Hydrogen Transition

  • Decoupling Growth and Emissions: Jinpu New Area, a national-level economic high-tech development zone, successfully maintained an economic growth rate of 6.2% while slashing carbon emissions by 11% in 2025.
  • Key Pillars of Success:
    • Master Planning: Launched a comprehensive master program for hydrogen development in 2020 to establish clear policy and operational guidelines.
    • Academia-Industry Collaboration: Partnered with local universities and the Dalian Chemical Research Institute to build national-level platforms for translating scientific research into industrial applications.
    • Supply Chain Capacity: Developed an annual production capacity of 5,000 tons of fuel cells and established testing and verification facilities.
    • Application Scenarios: Deployed hydrogen fuel cells across diverse public transport sectors, port logistics, municipal sanitation, and drones.
    • Port Capabilities: Dalian Port stands as the only port globally equipped with green methanol, LNG, and hydrogen injection capacities.

China Southern Power Grid's Strategy for Green and Smart Energy

  • Three-Fold Transition Approach: China Southern Power Grid (CSG) is upgrading the power sector by focusing on three key vectors:
    • Intelligent Development: Building smart grids to support massive terminal-device connectivity. This has bolstered grid stability to 99.999% in major computing hubs like Gui'an, directly supporting heavy AI workloads.
    • Green Power Integration: Over the past six consecutive years, CSG’s installed capacity of green energy exceeded 67%, maintaining a renewable utilization rate above 98%. This replaced 240 billion kilowatt-hours of traditional energy supply.
    • Integrated Platform Fostering: Established 58 zero-carbon pilot programs (with 25 accredited for carbon neutrality) and built an innovation network that has attracted over 600 industry partners.
  • Global Footprint (Belt and Road Initiative):
    • Interconnection: Operated 16 cross-border transmission lines routing green electricity across borders.
    • International Projects: Partnered on long-distance high-voltage transmission lines in Chile, smart grid distribution upgrades in Peru, blackout reduction initiatives in Cairo, Egypt, and a wind power investment in Kazakhstan that reduced carbon emissions by 1.6 million tons.

Overcoming Scalability Bottlenecks with Whole-Systems Thinking

  • The Real Bottleneck: Technology is rarely the primary barrier to decarbonization; rather, the bottleneck is the large-scale adoption and implementation of these technologies.
  • The Policy-Technology Gap: Technology often advances faster than public policy and commercial business models. Bridging this gap requires a "whole-systems model" where technology, regulatory frameworks, and financial business models are co-developed concurrently.
  • Broadening the Scope of Sustainability: While carbon emissions remain a primary focus, true sustainability must address:
    • Circularity and resource material efficiency.
    • Supply chain resilience and biodiversity preservation.
    • Balancing standard-of-living improvements with green transition trade-offs.

Notable Quotes

"We will not find a solution to industrial and energy transformation just in one company or one industry. It's really the cross-industry, the cross-sectoral, and co-location of those that will help." — Roberto Bocca, Head of the Centre for Energy and Materials, WEF
"Perfection is the enemy of doing good things... despite all these trade-offs, we need to remain focused on the long-term opportunities. Sustainability, the transition of the industrial clusters, is without any doubt the growth opportunity of this century." — Professor Mercedes Maroto-Valer, Heriot-Watt University
"The bottleneck tends to be more how do we do the large-scale adoption and implementation of that particular technology. And generally what happens here is technology tends to run ahead of policy and business models, and then we try to play catch-up." — Professor Mercedes Maroto-Valer, Heriot-Watt University

We should say that each place is different with different resources and development experience. Now, I'd like to share with you some of our takeaways from our experience. First, we need to really look at the resource features that we have. For us, there are three things. First, research capabilities: we have lots of excellent universities and research institutes. Second, we also have the petrochemical industrial park setting up platforms for the transformation. Third, we have ports and good logistic systems. The second aspect is service alignment. The government, experts, and businesses have been bridged by a communicative mechanism. For example, the Dalian Chemical Research Institute has helped us to build the hydrogen base, as well as the new materials base. These two bases are national-level platforms on which they facilitate industry translation. Third, the government provides supportive measures. Apart from building scenarios and use cases, we have also fostered full-chain hydrogen application cases. The municipal and provincial governments have adopted a plan to give favorable policies for the use of hydrogen-powered vehicles to bring down the cost of business. Thank you for your sharing. My next question goes to Vice-President Liu Wei from CSG. In your previous intervention, you mentioned the intelligent, green, and integrated transformation of CSG. Can you give us more specific cases and experiences from the China Southern Grid? In intelligent transformation, we have been proactive in supporting new business models. This includes, in particular, power stations and computing center development. In Gui'an and other major computing hubs, the stability of power supply reached 99.999%. Intelligent industries have been supported by CSG with an even higher quality of services. We have also taken the lead to promote consistent IoT services for distributed power suppliers. For heavy-consuming vehicles like off-road vehicles, they are also supported by our strong grid. Green electricity has been abundant, and installed capacity with green energy has exceeded 67% in the past six consecutive years. The utilization rate of renewable energy has exceeded 98%, and that has replaced 240 billion kilowatt-hours of traditional energy supply with green electricity. We've also set up 58 pilot programs of zero carbon, among which 25 have been accredited with carbon neutrality. The pilot parks we've established have attracted over 600 industry partners, promoting high-quality transformation of smart electricity equipment. Additionally, 125 upstream and downstream businesses have been provided favorable prices for their computing power needs. A new energy storage center has also been established. Recently, we launched full-scenario application bases for the entire society so that people can see the demos of these new applications. From an innovation standpoint, we find that industrial clusters cannot grow independently; they have to get comprehensive support from the market, policy, or institutional factors. Among them, I think industry-university and research collaboration is quite important. Professor, you are from the university, and you are a leading expert. My question for you is: how do clusters translate innovative achievements into large-scale application, and what are the biggest bottlenecks? Yes, thank you very much for the question. In terms of the work that we have done over the last five or six years working with clusters in the UK, this is actually true outside of clusters in general for all industrial transformations. The bottlenecks very rarely tend to be that we don't have the technology. The bottleneck tends to be more about how we do the large-scale adoption and implementation of that particular technology. Generally, what happens here is that technology tends to run ahead of policy and business models, and then we try to play catch-up. But this is really where the solution actually lies. What we have been implementing in the UK is the whole-systems model, where technologies co-develop alongside an understanding of policy, regulatory frameworks, and business models. What that means is that you can do the implementation at a much faster pace and scale than you would otherwise. Really pertinent to today's discussion as well is that clusters actually offer you the perfect ecosystem for this to happen. This is because you already have a number of industries, local universities, talent, and the workforce. You already have all the ecosystem that you need to implement a whole-systems, whole-thinking model. I think in terms of going forward as well, we still have many things that we have to address. But what is important to keep in mind is that this is not just about inventing solutions or new technologies as such. It's really about how we co-create the conditions together that are going to allow us to flourish in terms of the deployment of those technologies, and do this at a global scale so that there is prosperity for all. Thank you, Professor. And Mr. Roberto, I just mentioned that the World Economic Forum recently released a paper about industrial clusters. What are the most important takeaways you can share with us today? Yeah, at a very high level, first of all, I want to say that this work has been enabled by CSG. We want to be really thankful because this collaboration has allowed us to bring international knowledge and Chinese knowledge together. Thank you for that. We have analyzed five cases, of which one is indeed Dalian. We have seen that some of the elements Mercedes was talking about absolutely exist and are done in the best way. What we have seen is that the complexity of this ecosystem is somehow simple on paper, but when you try to make it happen across so many industries and logistical spaces, the complexity of the ecosystem really proves that planning ahead is key. So that's one really strong element: strong planning followed by ruthless execution. This is one of the elements that is so complex. In China, we have seen this happening in a sequential way. We see in other parts of the world where this is happening as a sort of retrofit. When you do a retrofit, it's much more complex because you then have to adapt. The design of it is a successful factor, but the implementation also takes a lot of effort and integration. Thank you. Thank you, Mr. Roberto. Okay, next we'll open the floor for the live Q&A part. If you have any questions regarding the industrial clusters, please feel free to raise your hands. Okay, first, this gentleman in row two. My question goes to Secretary Leng. You mentioned the achievements of hydrogen industry park development. Going ahead in Jinpu New Area, what are the forward-looking plans mapped out by the Working Committee for low-carbon development? Thank you for your question. In the 15th five-year plan, China has stipulated hydrogen as one of the future promising industries, making a stronger commitment to low-carbon industrial development, including hydrogen. Going ahead, we have the following key areas to focus on. First, promoting the acceleration of the realization of dual-carbon goals. Currently, 151 factories have been upgraded with green energy, including Anshan Iron and Steel Company, along with another 38 national-level green factories. We will intensify our efforts to accelerate the green transformation of factories and establish zero-carbon industrial parks. Second, we will accelerate green hydrogen material development. Dalian port is the only port in the world that has green methanol, LNG, and hydrogen injection capacity. We will continue to intensify our efforts in developing hydrogen technology, leveraging the advantage of the free trade zone and port advantages, and promote the further development of the green hydrogen pumping station. Third, we will continue to deepen our cooperation by using great platforms like the WEF. We will continue to be the Chinese champion by setting up the first pilot park of its kind. We will align closely with the most advanced international industry clusters, enhance our cooperation and communication on advanced technologies, and make our contribution to the further development of global industry clusters. Gentleman on the right. Thank you, Mr. Moderator, from the 21st Century Economic Herald News. My first question goes to Vice-President Liu from CSG. We know that the world is facing many challenges, such as US President Donald Trump's policies, and the global energy transition faces some setbacks. I was wondering how we can strengthen cooperation globally in such a challenging environment? Also, with China's leading role in energy transition and green energy, how would that help the world? Thank you. So you have two questions: first is for Mr. Liu, and second is for the Professor. Okay. Thank you for your question. China Southern Power Grid has made great advancement in international business by upholding the principle of mutual benefits and leveraging our expertise in management. In the BRI, we have adopted a lot of practices. For example, we have 16 cross-border transmission lines so that green electricity can reach wider. We have also been using smart, intelligent ways to enhance the stability of the grid in the Lao People's Democratic Republic. We've also set up a joint venture for promoting the high-voltage transmission line in Chile so that transmission can happen over long distances. We have helped to shorten the blackout time in Cairo, Egypt. We have also improved the management efficiency of the local grid, winning high acclamation from the local Cairo energy authority. In green electricity, we have invested in a wind power plant in Kazakhstan, reducing carbon emissions by 1.6 million tons. In electricity storage, we have also accelerated our international cooperation on that front. Going ahead, high-voltage DC smart grid configuration and smart storage technology will be the anchor points for us to serve the country's upgrading needs. For the reporter, I think due to time limits, we don't have time to take your second question. So Professor, I strongly recommend that you answer the question after the session when it's closed. Okay? The timer reminds us we have only one minute left, so let's wrap up the conversation with three key takeaways from our discussion. First, industrial clusters play a key role in industrial transformation and green transition. We have seen clear proof of this from Dalian Hydrogen Energy Industrial Park and also the rapid growth of China's national green electricity market. Second, China's cluster models provide two layers of value. Most of the practices can be adopted worldwide, while others are specially designed to fit China's unique local conditions. Third, almost all countries are facing the same set of opportunities and hurdles in the next stage of industrial shift. We need to roll out new breakthrough technologies, we need closer cross-border cooperation, and we also need to keep our industries competitive long-term. Inside China’s Energy System

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·76:10·384 views·2026-06-24
Michal MeidanDirector, China Energy Programme, Oxford Institute for Energy Studies
TL;DRChina's massive clean energy expansion has transformed its green sector into a vital economic engine driving 10% of its GDP, but the nation now faces critical hurdles in balancing grid stability, resolving fierce domestic price wars, and navigating mounting geopolitical trade barriers.

TL;DR

China's massive clean energy expansion has transformed its green sector into a vital economic engine driving 10% of its GDP, but the nation now faces critical hurdles in balancing grid stability, resolving fierce domestic price wars, and navigating mounting geopolitical trade barriers.

The Economic Engine of China’s Green Transition

  • Green Economy as a Growth Driver: Green industries have transitioned from environmental imperatives to primary drivers of China's economic growth.
      • According to Mr. Liu Shijin (Chinese Chief Advisor, CCICED), the green economy accounts for approximately 10% of China’s overall GDP.
      • It represents 20% of China's incremental GDP growth and a massive 50% of its incremental investment.
      • At least 10 trillion RMB has been invested in green sectors to date.
  • Evolution of Transition Drivers: Professor Kelly Sims Gallagher (Dean, Fletcher School at Tufts University) identified the historical sequence that shaped China's trajectory:
      • Energy Security: The initial push was driven by a desire to avoid heavy import dependence on oil and gas due to limited domestic reserves.
      • Economic Opportunity: In 2010, China officially designated clean energy as a "strategic emerging industry," recognizing its potential for job creation and industrial dominance.
      • Air Quality and Climate: Domestic air pollution crises around 2010 forced immediate policy actions, which aligned with global climate mitigation responsibilities.

Grid Infrastructure and the Challenge of Scale

  • Unprecedented Capacity Expansion: Zhang Wen-feng (President, State Grid Corporation of China) highlighted the sheer scale of China's grid integration efforts.
      • By the end of 2025, China's installed new energy capacity is projected to reach 1.2 billion kilowatts.
      • China holds 25% of the total installed new energy capacity worldwide.
  • State Grid’s Infrastructure Investment: To support this scale without risking blackouts, State Grid implemented targeted strategies during the 14th Five-Year Plan:
      • Financial Allocation: Invested 2.8 trillion RMB overall into the power grid.
      • West-to-East Transmission: Constructed ultra-high-voltage (UHV) power corridors spanning over 2,000 km to transmit 350 million kilowatts of clean power from western resource zones to eastern coastal demand centers.
      • R&D and Grid Control: Allocated 40 billion RMB annually (200 billion RMB over five years) to resolve technological bottlenecks in power control and generation.
      • Absorption Capacity: Increased system flexibility and absorption capacity five-fold to 120 million kilowatts to manage wind and solar intermittency.
  • The "Bottom-Up" Grid Disruption: Liu Shijin noted that the rise of distributed energy (e.g., rooftop solar, localized EV battery storage) is flipping the grid's traditional top-down flow upside down. This requires entirely new, bottom-up operational models and market-based trading of "green attributes."

Geopolitics, Monopolies, and Corporate Constraints

  • The Monopoly Challenge: China controls 80% of the global solar panel supply chain and provides 80% of solar and 70% of wind equipment worldwide.
      • Gallagher warned that other nations are increasingly unwilling to accept a Chinese monopoly. This is triggering a period of intense contestation, resulting in more tariffs, trade barriers, and local content requirements.
  • Corporate Adaptation Strategies: Namita Shah (President of Asia and OneTech, TotalEnergies) explained how global energy developers must navigate localized restrictions:
      • Geopolitical Hedging: TotalEnergies adapts its sourcing based on local laws; for example, they cannot import Chinese solar panels for projects built in the United States, whereas other regions remain open.
      • Grid Stabilization: Global developers are shifting toward combining renewables with battery storage, localized electrolyzers for hydrogen production, and using efficient coal- or gas-fired plants to maintain the grid's base load.

Domestic Market Pressures and "Vicious" Price Wars

  • The Paradox of Overcapacity: Despite a massive long-term runway for clean energy—which currently accounts for only 6–7% of China's primary energy consumption—domestic manufacturers are locked in a damaging price war.
  • Causes of Severe Price Competition: Liu Shijin attributed the chaotic domestic market to:
      • Hyper-Fast Tech Iteration: Technologies evolve so rapidly that companies fear investing in current-generation facilities, knowing they may become obsolete within a year.
      • Local Protectionism: Local government interventions keep unproductive "zombie companies" afloat, preventing natural market consolidation.
      • Export Pressures: Unable to turn a profit domestically, Chinese firms dump excess supply into international markets, further aggravating global trade tensions.

Policy Frameworks and the 15th Five-Year Plan

  • The 15th Five-Year Plan Horizon: Mr. Liang (National Energy Administration) confirmed that China will target an annual addition of 200 million kilowatts of new energy capacity during the upcoming planning period.
  • Energy Self-Dependence: China has established an 80% self-dependence level for its energy supply, providing a buffer against global geopolitical disruptions (such as energy transit crises in the Strait of Hormuz).
  • Consolidating the Energy Mix: The state will continue to transition thermal coal-fired power plants from primary energy suppliers to backup base-load facilities, coordinating them with nuclear, wind, solar, and hydro resources.

Notable Quotes

"In 2024, the green economy in China accounts for about 10% of the GDP... Actually, you can see that China's economy is driven by the green economy." — Mr. Liu Shijin, Chinese Chief Advisor, CCICED
"A risk that China faces is that while today it almost holds a monopolistic position in many of the clean energy technologies, I think a lot of countries are not going to be willing to let China continue to hold this monopolistic position, and so we could go into a period of a lot of contestation, more tariffs, [and] more trade barriers." — Professor Kelly Sims Gallagher, Dean, Fletcher School at Tufts University
"The renewable energy or new energy account higher and higher percentage... higher percentage of renewable energy in the system in the past several years led to blackouts in South America... This revealed one big challenge: that a higher new energy system will lead to the vulnerability and complexity of the grid." — Mr. Zhang Wen-feng, President, State Grid Corporation of China
Well, good evening, ladies and gentlemen. Welcome to our stakeholder discussion, "Inside China's Energy Systems". My name is Chen Yi. I'm from Shanghai Media Group, SMG International, and I'm really glad to be moderating this session. We all understand China sits at the center of the global energy transition. On the one hand, it's the largest investor in clean energy, and on the other hand, coal still accounts for 60% of our electricity generation. So, it's somewhere at a crossroads. You know, we need to balance a lot of things. One, we need to achieve energy security; we also have to achieve high-quality development, and we need to do the transition toward a low-carbon future, and it's all happening at the same time. Today we are very honored to have four panelists here just to talk about China's energy transition and its trajectory. First, I'm going to introduce our panelists starting from my left-hand side. There is Mr. Liu Shijin, Chinese chief advisor of the China Council for International Cooperation on Environment and Development. Next up, we have Mrs. Namita Shah, President of Asia and President of OneTech, TotalEnergies. Next, we have Mr. Zhang Wen-feng, President of State Grid Corporation of China. And also, we have Professor Kelly Sims Gallagher, Dean of the Fletcher School at Tufts University. Welcome to all of you. Now, just to set the stage for the discussion, I want to do a very quick round of icebreaking discussion. What do you see as the most important or significant challenge or opportunity for China's energy transition at this moment? We'll start with Mr. Liu. If in one sentence: the new energy developed so fast and, with the existing power grid and mechanism, it's not matching. So, the conflict between these two is creating a lot of challenges. The fast development of renewable energies is not matching the existing mechanism. I'd say that the first thing is geopolitics. I think it is actually a big opportunity in the sense that, in a world where everybody feels that they need to be completely energy resilient, it enables a huge push into the renewable energy sector because it just makes sense to invest more in it and go further in it. I think one of the challenges is probably the speed. There is probably more urgency to it today than there was before the geopolitical situation in the past six months. And also, I think a question mark is, whether we like it or not, what is the impact of the huge development in AI and data centers going to do in terms of impact on that energy need? It's interesting when we talk about geopolitics, everybody sees it as a conflict or pressure, but you see it as an opportunity, and we can elaborate on that maybe later in the session. Thank you. So, what are the biggest opportunities or challenges, Mr. Zhang? The transformation of energy is developing very quickly. The renewable energy or new energy account for a higher and higher percentage. And we have to see that this high percentage of renewable energy in the system in the past several years led to blackouts in South America, which had a very big impact on society. This revealed one big challenge: that a higher new energy system, with a higher percentage, will lead to the vulnerability and complexity of the system. We have to pay attention to this. So, we think on the one hand, we have to understand the necessity of the trend of renewable energy. On the other hand, we have to be adaptive and have some further solutions for the vulnerability and complexity of the grid. We have to guarantee the supply, the safety of the supply, while at the same time promoting the orderly development of the renewable energy. I think this is a big challenge; it's a balance we have to achieve. In summary, clean energy technologies—there's actually risk and instability that comes along with these new technologies. So maybe that's another topic that can be further elaborated during the session. Kelly? I would say energy security is at the heart of the challenge and opportunity. Because energy security challenges continue to loom large, it is making it difficult for China to more rapidly move away from coal. And yet, coal is at the heart of the climate challenge. So, these intersect, and I think they are creating a very acute challenge between those two issues right now. So, three out of the four focus more on challenges and risks, and one is more optimistic. Let's see how the dynamics play. I want to start with Mr. Liu. Mr. Liu, we're focusing on China's energy, which is the topic for today's discussion. From the policy process, in the 14th Five-Year Plan, we made a lot of achievements. When we look into the 15th Five-Year Plan, we have a lot of goals to achieve. We have to balance things like the security of the energy supply. Secondly, we have to guarantee high-quality development of the economy. Then, we have to guarantee the low-carbon or green transformation. How can we balance these three goals at the same time? How can we achieve these three goals at the same time? What are the policies? The question you have raised, we can review together simply or briefly, especially after the double-carbon target was raised. What has been done by the Chinese government correctly? It has been six years, or in the past ten years. In terms of green transformation, the most important change we have achieved is that very rapid development of low-carbon green industries has developed because of technological breakthroughs, exports, and also a higher capacity of consumption in China. For instance, in the production of those renewable energy facilities and equipment, China is a very big producer. So, how can we comment on this kind of transformation? Number one, green transformation is extremely important, and it is a very important policy from the Chinese government. Low-carbon technology or zero-carbon technology, even negative-carbon technology, are replacing the old technologies. This fundamentally changes the issues. So, technology innovation is very important. Number two, as we say, we should focus on this new technology. We need a higher level of investment. So, investment is the second keyword. I don't have the correct comprehensive number here—the correct number here maybe—but at least 10 trillion RMB has been invested in this regard. So, investment will translate into GDP and into economic growth. When we are talking about environmental protection or low carbon, people are concerned that the GDP growth will be compromised. But actually, it is not. It is one of the most important drivers for China's economic growth when we try to achieve these technological breakthroughs of green transformation. Another piece of statistics to share with you: we have a lab focusing on low carbon. In 2024, the green economy in China accounted for about 10% of the GDP. The green economy is 10% of the GDP. In the incremental GDP, it's 20% of the GDP incremental. In terms of investment or incremental investment, it's 50% of the incremental investment. So actually, you can see that the China economy is driven by the green economy. Let me come to the objectives. We have been developing, and actually, this green investment is not preventing us from developing. This morning we have been talking about AI, which consumes a lot of power, but that turned out to be an advantage for China because of the huge development of green development. So, it's the green power supply which is our advantage for China. As for high-quality development, as President Xi said, our development is being supported by this green development. So, this green transformation has been a big driver for our development. That's why we have been having this well-coordinated development on three fronts. In summary, we have been very successful in developing this way, and we will continue to do so. More specifically, we should keep developing as fast as we did in the past. We should not be slower. So, we should stick to that direction. Thank you. Investment in clean energy, in new energy technologies, actually has become a driver for economic growth in China. According to him, the green economy accounts for more than 10% of the total GDP and also accounts for 50% of the increase in investment in China. So, this is how we balance new technology in green energy and the goal of high-quality development in China. But I also want to touch upon one of the goals that China is aiming for in this new period: the energy security that we all talked about. So, Mr. Zhang, you mentioned a few specific cases about new energy development. One focus should be placed on energy security. For your company, you are putting in place this new system, and many of the installed capacities are based on new energy. But in the past, how do you make sure that this new energy is being transmitted and supplied to the rest of the country in a very secure way? What are the big challenges in terms of new energy security? With the deep transformation of this new energy in China, the scale and proportion of new energy is becoming increasingly bigger. Two statistics: by the end of 2025, the installed capacity for new energy in China is 1.2 billion kilowatts. And also in 2025, we have 434 million kilowatts, 25% of the total installed capacity of new energy worldwide. So, we are talking about the big-scale development of new energy in China. How do we make sure that they are well connected to the grid and consumed by the consumers? In terms of the direction, we have to implement the instructions from President Xi in terms of security, putting in place a new energy system and new power system. More specifically, there are four areas. First of all, we have to lay down a very strong foundation for the material foundation of this state grid. We invested 2.8 trillion in the 14th Five-Year Plan. And we put in place this power corridor, supplying power from the west to the east, which means increasing 350 million kilowatts of power supply so that our system can support this 500 million kilowatts of power supply across the country. We also have to rely on technological innovation. In the course of putting in place this new energy system, it's fair to say that we are kind of charting unknown territories in terms of new power generation, and also the power control system as we said. How do we make sure that our system can be detected and controlled properly? Every year, we invest about 40 billion. So, in five years' time, we invested a total of 200 billion RMB for technological innovation so as to address all these technological bottlenecks, including the power grid control system and new power generation technology. The third area is to improve the system adjustment capacity. Because of the intermittent and volatile nature of new power, you need to place a very powerful control system for power consumption. In the past five years, this absorption capacity has increased by five times. We have 120 million kilowatts of this capacity which was primarily constructed in the past 14th Five-Year Plan, which laid down a very good foundation for this new energy power system. Fourthly, we try to bring into full play the market mechanism. We have established this nationwide power market covering the whole country with spot and medium- or long-term markets. This means abundant power supply has been allocated from the west to the east. And also, many of these energy powers have been part of the trading mechanism, in the market mechanism, to reflect their green value. Thanks to these measures in the above four areas, as I said, we have to make sure that we have this energy security, and also we have to address these absorption capacity issues. We are doing a great job because of these measures we take in these four areas. Thank you so much for summarizing these measures. Namita, as head of a company that has operations in different regions and different parts of the world, what are some of your reflections on what Mr. Zhang said? He basically points out how China is doing its energy transition. He points out that we are building a strong infrastructure, we are using a lot of technological innovation, we're putting in a lot of money like 200 billion RMB in investment and system flexibility, and also this invisible hand of the market. What are some of your observations in other markets? Are they similar or are they different? Are there unique observations? Well, what I would say is that it's very clear that the challenges in any of the markets are the same. I mean, renewable energy does come with this issue of flexibility, and it does come with this idea of being able to manage the grid and the peak of when the sun produces energy at a certain time and demand may not be exactly aligned with when the energy is actually being produced. People have been working on developing different technologies to deal with this kind of problem, not just having to worry about grids, in which China has clearly taken a lead and anticipated some of the issues, but also using other things like more batteries. The development of batteries and how they can be used to be able to stock energy when there's a lot of energy that's being produced but cannot be brought onto the grid is key. How do you put electrolyzers next to the batteries to be able to use excess energy to maybe produce hydrogen when the energy is not being used directly to go on the grid? And then, of course, what do you do to help maintain the baseload? Traditionally, a lot of people thought that maintaining the baseload in renewables meant having a lot more gas-fired power plants because they can ramp up and ramp down very quickly. What we see recently is that countries like China are thinking about how they can do that better with coal-fired plants, and to be able to use that more as a baseload and get them to be more efficient, and to be able to ramp up and ramp down those plants to provide this offset to the grid. So, I think the challenges are very clear. What I must say is that it is very clear also that China has been at the forefront of putting a lot of investment and organizing the innovation and the technology drive to be able to deal with each of the different components of renewable energy. So, whether it's been the development of solar panels, of batteries, of investment in the grid, or of thinking about what the next generation of coal-fired power plants should be like, it is China that has done this in a very organized fashion and has tackled this problem. I don't think other countries have been that organized, for many different reasons. They haven't thought of putting that kind of money into innovation, or in some countries, green energy hasn't been the priority. So, I think that's where we need to see what other countries are going to be doing. And that's why I come back to this idea that geopolitics can be an opportunity. I think a lot of countries that were not thinking like China was thinking are going to have to start thinking that way if they want to have energy security. Renewable energy is one of the ways of achieving that. Kelly, talking about geopolitics, Namita said she sees great potential under current circumstances. What are your thoughts? We understand there's great tension around the world today, for example, the tension over the Strait of Hormuz, which has reshaped the energy market in the world. Under these circumstances, do you think some of the issues that we talked about or some of the things that China is doing, especially this huge investment in new technology, can be something that other regions and countries adopt at this point of time during these geopolitical circumstances, while still respecting their priorities and their unique aspects? It's funny, I feel like we've come full circle. As I think back to the beginning of China's trajectory towards the clean energy economy, I think it was originally, as an outside observer, driven by energy security concerns. China was always aware that it had limited oil and gas economically recoverable reserves and was very conscious about wanting to avoid a strong import dependence on oil and gas. Then, I think China was the first country to really see economic opportunity in clean energy. Whereas, in other countries, including the United States, I think that's been a very contested idea. Was the clean energy economy going to take away from the fossil fuel economy, the traditional economy, or was it going to be a source of growth and innovation? China really looked at it as an opportunity. It saw new energy as the industry of the future. It named clean energy as a strategic emerging industry in 2010. Then, I think sequentially, the last driver for China was the environmental imperative. Actually, I think urban air pollution was the initial stronger driver because, of course, urban air pollution was a very severe challenge in China, also kind of dating back to around 2010. But I think there was also a recognition that climate change was a real risk that had to be addressed, and that was never disputed in the Chinese context. I think China understood it had a responsibility, and now, as the largest emitter, continues to have a responsibility to manage the climate challenge. Now we come back to an energy crisis where we are today, and the whole integrated strategy that I think China arrived at as a result of these different factors has placed China in a stronger position than it would have been otherwise, if it hadn't done everything that it has done to invest in the development of a clean energy economy. I think China is more energy secure. To come to your question, I think many other countries are looking at what China has done and thinking that this would be a good strategy for them too. Renewables plus storage plus a strong grid all can create more resilience in an economy, and there's economic opportunity in a clean energy economy—new jobs, sources of economic growth. But the challenge for other countries now is how to contend with China. China is so dominant in the marketplace. Is there room for anyone else? I think a lot of other countries don't just want to be consumers of Chinese technology; they also want to be producers. That's going to be a challenge. I think that's really going to be a challenging path forward as we try to figure out, well, is there room for, you know, Brazil to become a producer? Is there room for Indonesia? Is there room for the United States to come back? I think these are all open questions. Well, I think there's always room for collaboration, right, Namita? Well, I think, you know, collaboration and cooperation are sort of very big words that people use all the time, and clearly, we use them often because it makes sense, especially in the world that we used to be in—to be sort of very connected and to be able to collaborate to try to solve these collective problems together. I mean, energy independence and climate change are all collective problems that we should be working together to solve. I think one of the challenges for a lot of other countries that did not do the work that China did today is, even if they wanted to invest, to step back and say, "Well, the technological advances are there." I mean, we're not going to now start trying to find another way of building solar panels. Everybody knows how to do it, and we know how to do it sort of best in China. So, I do agree that the dilemma for a lot of countries is going to be: how do I collaborate so that I can also push forward in this geopolitical context my energy independence, but not find myself in a position where then I become dependent not on fossil fuel providers, but on renewable energy technology providers? And how, at the same time, do I do that and make sure that it is also bringing economic growth to the country? Yes, I do believe that collaboration is the key to that. If you think about the '80s, there was a lot of Western investment in China when companies like Apple, etc., were coming up. There was a lot of investment, there was a lot of sharing of technology, and to the credit, of course, of all the Chinese companies that worked in that ecosystem, they learned from that. They took lessons, and they got new ideas because they were able to build on ideas that had come from outside. Maybe that model in the reverse way is something that is possible. I think the demand is so big that even if there is a small percentage of that demand that is, by collaboration, put in other countries—with collaborations between Chinese companies and local companies—there's so much demand that the rest of it can be met by exports from China. But I think there is something interesting that can be done, and you know, I know I've said it like five times, but I think that's why the geopolitical context, I find, is interesting. It puts a lot of countries in this position where they need to be more open about this. At the same time, I think it puts China in a position where they also need to think about how they can leverage off of that, be a partner, and give that reassurance of being a partner and collaborating. So basically, now it's a good time window, and a lot of countries are thinking about collaborating with China; they just don't know how. So, let's hear from our Chinese speakers. Mr. Liu, we talked about—given the geopolitical pressure, a lot of countries urgently need to cooperate with China. However, for China, according to the 15th Five-Year Plan, there are a lot of drivers to achieve our goals. One of them is the innovation of technology. What about international exchange and cooperation? How do you respond to the demand where many countries are eager to cooperate with China? How do you respond to that? I believe in the past several years or one decade, China has had our own way with Chinese characteristics. The feature of this path is innovation, paying a lot of attention to investment and transformation in the process of development to fundamentally achieve a green and renewable transformation. So, in some renewable energy areas, we have our own advantages, including our exports to other countries. Certain partnerships, especially when we see the Strait of Hormuz, some are hesitating, but the Strait of Hormuz crisis will help them make some decisions very quickly. Even though we are at a very initial stage, there is an opportunity for each country. The question is whether you have changed your attitude and you're ready to make investment and boost development. Each country has its own advantage. China has its advantage, and other countries also have their own advantages. But the precondition is cooperation, the mindset of cooperation, and no protectionism. Another point I would like to say is that our technology is expanding into new areas in terms of innovation. Mr. Zhang mentioned one very important issue: renewable energy. There is one advantage—actually, they have five advantages. One is it's abundant, then it's stable, it's green, number four, it's convenient, and number five, it's very economic, it's cost-effective. So, if you give scores to these five areas, wind and solar are green; they have an advantage. In terms of abundance, they are abundant, with sufficient sources of solar and wind. So, when we talk about security, when we talk about the big picture, wind and solar have their advantages. In the past, the cost was very high, but with innovation, the cost has been significantly reduced in the past several years. One Chinese company generating power in Saudi Arabia has a cost for 1 kWh of only 1 cent of US dollars. But how can we achieve the problem of stability? We have to be stable, right? When we talk about renewable energy not being secure, it is because it is not stable. But it's a minor instability, a minor risk. Sometimes we have wind, sometimes we don't have wind. But the whole power system has to coordinate, and the physical foundation and flexibility are very important. In the past, we had some solutions, but now we have chemical energy storage solutions. Chemical storage is also seeing a cost reduction very quickly. And also, we have a load-side potential. Today we have a meeting here, so you can pick the time of 10 AM, or you can pick the time of 10 PM. If at 10 PM we have a much lower cost of tariff, then it's more cost-effective to start our discussion around 10 PM, right? Because the power tariff is much cheaper at that time. So, there are a lot of solutions on the load side. So, you can offset the cycle. Then, we can solve all these five problems: green, stability, cheap, etc., right? Of course, we have challenges. Number one is that energy storage and the cost are still very high for now. Another, which is a very important question—Mr. Zhang is here. I'm learning from Mr. Zhang; you are the expert. I sometimes have a question in my mind. When we have new energy or renewable energy, we bring in a very big change, which is distributed power generation. In the past, we only had one big power generator, but now on each rooftop, we can have a power generator. So, it's distributed. They are consumers, and at the same time, they are power generators. And then they have a car—they have a Tesla or whatever—and renewable energy can be stored in their cars. So, "distributed" is the keyword. Now we have hundreds or even thousands of power generators in a small town or village. How can we coordinate with all these power generators while at the same time they are consumers of power? How can we balance supply and demand in this much more sophisticated and distributed situation? So, we have to reorganize our grid. We have to establish a bottom-up grid. In the past, it was top-down, right? So now it's kind of upside down. We have to establish a bottom-up grid. So, you have higher responsibilities to design a totally new, balanced power grid. The innovation we are talking about is technological innovation, but market or business model innovation is also very important, like trading, the market exchange to trade green attributes. It's a very, very important innovation. You can sell your green attributes with the renewable energy. If it's green power, it's very different from brown power or black power, right? So, a lot of innovation is in the management or in the policy side. We can design some new exchanges to trade green attributes. So, there are a lot of opportunities for each country. Who Pays for Inflation?

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·37:22·354 views·2026-06-24
Faisal AlibrahimMinister of Economy and Planning, Saudi Arabia Beata JavorcikChief Economist, European Bank for Reconstruction and Development Mario CentenoGovernor, Banco de Portugal
TL;DRWhile global inflation is driven by geopolitical triggers like Middle East tensions and energy volatility, its impacts vary widely between high-demand economies like the US and low-inflation states like China, requiring a mix of targeted fiscal…
policies, renewable energy...

TL;DR

While global inflation is driven by geopolitical triggers like Middle East tensions and energy volatility, its impacts vary widely between high-demand economies like the US and low-inflation states like China, requiring a mix of targeted fiscal policies, renewable energy investments, and innovative institutional frameworks to protect vulnerable populations.

Divergent Global Drivers: US vs. China Dynamics

  • Geopolitical Inflation Triggers: Global consumer price index (CPI) numbers, which had stabilized in late 2023, experienced renewed upward pressure starting in February due to the Middle East conflict.
      • US CPI rose to 4.2%, the Eurozone hit 3.2%, and China experienced a mild increase to 1.2%.
  • US Demand Pressures: The US is experiencing strong domestic demand, particularly driven by capital expenditure on data centers and equipment.
      • Fed Policy Delay: The Federal Reserve's slow initial response to rising inflation in 2021 and 2022 allowed the absolute level of prices to remain stubbornly high, even as the rate of inflation cooled.
  • China’s Low-Inflation Challenge: In contrast to the US, China struggles with weak domestic demand, keeping its core inflation (excluding energy and food) at just 0.6% against a 2.0% target.
      • The Deflation Trap: Dean Yiping Huang pointed out that prolonged low inflation can trap economies in contractionary cycles of consumer and investor expectations, as previously seen in Japan.

Energy Volatility and the Fiscal Burden on Governments

  • Geographic Price Disparities: The impact of high energy prices is highly uneven, depending largely on a nation's geography and access to refined products.
      • Despite rising global volatility, the US remained in the top five globally for the highest gasoline price increases.
  • Strategic Reserves and Demand Destruction: Global oil prices did not spike to $150 or $200 per barrel because of strategic stockpiles—particularly in China—and "demand destruction" (decreased energy demand) in key markets.
  • The High Cost of Energy Subsidies: Governments in wealthier developing nations use subsidies to shield citizens from price spikes.
      • Fiscal Space Squeeze: Karen Young emphasized that while countries like India have minimized inflation through high subsidies, doing so dramatically shrinks their fiscal space, limiting long-term government spending in education, healthcare, and infrastructure.
  • Persistent Geopolitical Risks: Energy diversification is essential to insulate economies from conflicts in transit zones like the Strait of Hormuz, but creating these redundancies will introduce long-term structural costs into the energy market.

The Renewable Transition: An Inflation Shield and Inequality Driver

  • Insulation Through Renewables: Investing heavily in renewable energy acts as a strong buffer against imported fossil-fuel price shocks.
      • Zero-Cost Energy: Elizabeth Thurbon highlighted China and Australia as leaders in this transition. In Australia, high rooftop solar panel penetration allows many households to pay virtually nothing for energy once capital costs are recovered.
  • The "Two-Track" Inequality Gap: Rapid renewable adoption risks widening socioeconomic divisions.
      • Homeowner vs. Renter Divide: Wealthier homeowners who can afford the high capital costs of solar panels reap the benefits of cheap electricity, while renters, apartment dwellers, and low-income families remain trapped paying volatile fossil-fuel utility rates.
  • Manufacturing Energy Security: Policymakers must transition from reactively managing external price shocks to proactively "manufacturing" sovereign energy security through localized clean systems.

Rethinking Institutional Frameworks and Policy Levers

  • The Case for an Energy Reserve Bank: Elizabeth Thurbon proposed establishing an independent "Energy Reserve Bank" to manage energy prices similarly to how central banks manage money.
      • Lowering Capital Costs: Because a 1% decrease in the weighted average cost of capital leads to a 10% decrease in final energy costs, an independent body could issue government-backed clean energy offtake contracts to secure ultra-cheap finance for green projects.
  • Limits of Monetary Policy: Central bank interest rate adjustments are blunt macroeconomic tools designed to manage average CPI. They cannot solve targeted pain points.
      • Fiscal Intervention Required: Sector-specific pressures (such as rising food or fuel costs) must be managed by finance ministries through targeted subsidies and income redistribution to protect low-income households.
  • Vulnerabilities of Small Island States: Small, import-dependent economies like Jamaica lack the monetary sovereignty and floating currencies required to utilize standard inflation toolkits.
      • Infrastructure and Capital Solutions: Protecting these regions requires cross-border infrastructure cooperation (e.g., regional grid interconnectivity lines) and innovative capital solutions rather than theoretical interest rate adjustments.

Notable Quotes

"When inflation rate rises, the impact on consumer groups or on industries differ... the fiscal policy should trying to take action to protect the low-income household, especially when inflation rate becomes high." — Yiping Huang, Dean of the National School of Development at Peking University
"In India for example, we see really high subsidies, and so you don't see the same inflationary pressure. But it reveals itself in a different way in the economy. It essentially shrinks fiscal space for governments." — Karen Young, Senior Fellow at the Middle East Institute
"Energy is absolutely foundational to the whole economy. The price of energy determines your manufacturing competitiveness, it determines households' ability to heat and cool... But we don't treat it the same way that we treat money. So maybe it would be helpful to think about having an energy reserve bank." — Elizabeth Thurbon, Professor of Political Economy at the University of New South Wales
Hello everybody, welcome to our session about inflation. As I was thinking about the session and about the things I wanted to ask about, I remembered something that happened to me. I live in New York and I was at the grocery store, and as I was checking out, I had this feeling that I've had a lot of times over the last few years, which is when the cashier gave me the total, I thought there has been a terrible mistake. But, I know enough not to say anything at this point; I also know enough not to hold up a line in New York, so I stood outside of the supermarket going through the receipt trying to figure out why the price was so much higher than I thought it would be. I think versions of this have happened all over the world, obviously to much more serious degrees than mine. Inflation, though, has become a global issue since the pandemic—and more generally than just prices rising, price stability. But luckily we have a really brilliant panel to help us talk through inflation, where it's headed, and what some of the causes and effects might be. So I will start with Karen Young. But you mentioned about the pandemic, and especially during and immediately after the pandemic, we saw inflation rising in different places. However, if you look at the CPI numbers in most economies—CPI index, consumer price index—at the beginning of the year or late last year, they were pretty stable, mild. And that's why people were thinking about either holding their monetary policy unchanged or some policymakers thinking about cutting the interest rate. However, things changed after February. So I think that Middle East conflict was probably a trigger of rising inflation rate across the board during this time. So you see in the US, the inflation rate rose to 4.2 percent; in the Eurozone, 3.2 percent; even in China, it's 1.2 percent, that's actually higher than what we we had. But I guess, so that's a common theme, and I think energy was one driver. But there are some different stories. In the US, you mentioned things becoming more expensive. I suspect that partly because the Fed was a bit slow in fighting against the high inflation in 2021, 2022. So even though the inflation rate came down, but I guess the absolute level of prices stayed relatively high. But in the US, we are also seeing relatively strong domestic demand, especially this time related to the equipment cost related to data center and so on. For China, that's a slightly different story. We are also seeing the inflation rate picking up, but if you look at core inflation excluding energy and so on, it's still 0.6 percent. You look at the US, China and the Eurozone, we all had a CPI target at 2 percent. Yeah. But for them, the rate is higher now, so the question is: should we cut, and should we cut more? The question, the challenge in China is it's still below 2 percent. How do we achieve a modest increase in inflation is something I think we should work on. Yeah, I remember finding that a strange thing when I was first learning economics, that we want to see a little inflation, we want to see prices rising a little bit. True. And when they're not, that is actually its own set of problems. Karen, I'd love to move to you. Would you mind talking about the energy aspect of this? What have we seen happen with energy prices sort of since the pandemic? It's been quite a ride. It has been a ride, and you know it's interesting to compare what's happened since February 28th with our prior kind of energy and political crisis, which meaning the the Russian invasion of Ukraine. In that period, in 2022, we actually saw a higher price spike, certainly in natural gas, than what we've seen since February 28th. And this, you know, the lesson that we're learning is that, you know, it really it really sort of depends on where the impact lands. In the latest, you know, three months and a half or so, the geographic impact of higher energy prices has been really geographically disparate. So we saw it very heavy first in in Asia, in South Asia, in Southeast Asia, and then, you know, slowly depending on access to refined products, coming to Europe and and also to the United States. But where we saw the highest increase in refined fuel gasoline prices, the US was actually top five of of highest price increase. And some of the the disparity in like where people feel that inflationary pressure in the things they use that come from oil really depends on how governments have behaved, even in their ability to stockpile fuels and their ability to provide subsidies. So in many developing economies, the wealthier ones were able to have access to some reserves or to provide subsidies to citizens. And in in India, for example, we see really high subsidies, and so you don't see the same inflationary pressure. But it reveals itself in a different way in the economy. It essentially shrinks fiscal space for governments, right? So if they're paying out more to reduce the the price impact to citizens, that money comes from somewhere, and it limits the government's ability to spend in other places. And so we'll continue to feel that, you know, even as oil prices are going down, right? Because the governments will have to account for what they spent in the beginning of the year. So, you know, I would say in terms of where we are now, oil prices are going down, that's a good thing. We never reached—you know, this was this enormous supply shock to oil and gas in our global markets—why weren't prices 150, 200 dollars a barrel? It's a question everyone's asking right now. And the reason for that is that we had these strategic reserves, especially here in China, and a reduction in demand, especially in China, right?
