Holcim CEO: Concrete, AI and Europe's Housing Crisis | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(124.0K)·33:59·3.0K views·2026-06-24
Nicolai TangenCEO, Norges Bank Investment Management Miljan GutovicCEO, Holcim
TL;DRUnder the leadership of CEO Miljan Gutovic, global building materials giant Holcim is transforming its traditional industry by leveraging AI to capture CHF 200 million in efficiency gains, spinning off its North American division to maximize value,…
and proving that...

TL;DR

Under the leadership of CEO Miljan Gutovic, global building materials giant Holcim is transforming its traditional industry by leveraging AI to capture CHF 200 million in efficiency gains, spinning off its North American division to maximize value, and proving that decarbonization can drive profitable growth.

Global Growth Strategies & Regional Market Dynamics

  • The North American Spinoff: Holcim completed the spinoff of its North American business to unlock a new chapter of growth and capitalize on diversifying strategies between North America and Europe. The corporate separation helped position Holcim to close 2024 as the best-performing stock on the Swiss Market Index.
  • The European Housing Shortage: Europe is currently facing a massive deficit of nearly 10 million homes.
      • Market Recovery: While high interest rates and slow permitting have historically throttled construction, a recent uptick in permit approvals and the EU’s affordable housing plan point to a near-term market recovery.
      • Defense & Reconstruction Demands: Anticipated increases in European defense spending will require significant infrastructure upgrades (road and rail networks). Holcim is also structurally positioned in surrounding nations (Poland, Romania, Hungary, and Moldova) to assist in the eventual reconstruction of Ukraine.
  • Latin America's High-Margin Boom: Latin America yields Holcim's highest EBIT margins, driven by rapid urbanization and population growth.
      • The Retail Model: Unlike in Western markets, nearly 50% of customers in Latin America are "self-builders" who buy bagged cement for personal home expansions. Holcim serves this demographic through its franchise network of approximately 2,500 *Disensa* retail construction shops, supplying up to 85% of their building needs.
      • Mexican Expansion: Mexico remains Holcim's primary growth market in the region, fueled by President Claudia Sheinbaum's commitment to construct 1.8 million homes over the next five years.

Transforming Materials Science & Decarbonization

  • The Scale of Concrete Consumption: Concrete is the second most consumed material on Earth after water, with global consumption reaching approximately 30 billion tonnes annually.
  • Sustainability as a Profit Driver: Rather than treating green transitions as a cost center, Holcim uses sustainability to drive higher profit margins.
      • Formulation Innovation: Over the last five years, eco-friendly cement and concrete formulations have grown to represent more than one-third of Holcim's total sales. These products replace high-CO2 raw materials with cheaper, more sustainable alternatives.
      • The Circular Economy: Holcim acts as a waste-management partner, getting paid to accept waste materials, processing them into alternative fuel sources, and using them to replace traditional fossil fuels in cement kilns.
      • Financial Impact: These sustainable practices and green product lines helped boost Holcim's European profit margins by 430 basis points over the past five years.

Harnessing AI and Operational Decentralization

  • The CHF 200 Million AI Target: Holcim has committed to achieving CHF 200 million in net benefits through artificial intelligence by 2028.
      • Predictive Maintenance: Large cement plants (averaging 1,000 by 500 meters) must run 24/7. Holcim feeds massive internal plant data into proprietary AI algorithms to detect early warning signs of hardware failure, allowing management to schedule preemptive maintenance and avoid costly unplanned shutdowns.
      • Upskilling Over Layoffs: Rather than replacing human capital, AI implementation is being used to upskill existing personnel and maximize overall operational efficiency.
  • Decentralized Corporate Structure: Holcim maintains an extremely slim corporate headquarters, operating on a "local for local" business model where countries manage their own sourcing, production, and sales. This highly decentralized structure is a key driver of Holcim's industry-leading EBIT margins.

Leadership Philosophy & Corporate Culture

  • The "Holcim Spirit": Gutovic aligned the post-spinoff workforce of 45,000 employees under three core pillars: Purpose, People, and Performance. This includes the creation of *Holcim University*, which trained 3,000 future leaders in its first year.
  • Gutovic's Three Leadership Rules:

1. Control the Controllables: Avoid wasting corporate time trying to predict external macroeconomic or geopolitical shifts. Instead, focus entirely on internal execution, cost discipline, and operational efficiency.

2. Fight Complacency: High-performing teams are highly vulnerable to complacency. Leaders must actively prevent backsliding through constructive criticism, fresh targets, and peer comparison.

3. Benchmark Relentlessly: Constantly compare performance metrics both internally across different countries and externally against competitors to copy and scale best practices.

  • Encouraging Bold Decision-Making: Gutovic advises young professionals to work from physical offices to network and learn, and cautions against the modern corporate tendency to identify problems without offering solutions. He encourages managers to make timely, bold decisions even with imperfect information.

Notable Quotes

"After water, concrete is the second most consumed material on earth. We use approximately 30 billion tonnes of concrete every single year. So we can say it's actually a bedrock of modern civilization." — *Miljan Gutovic*
"For us, sustainability is a driver of profitable growth. We are doing it because first of all it's the right thing to do, and for us it makes perfect business sense." — *Miljan Gutovic*
"Complacency is our biggest enemy... when the company is doing well, people have a tendency to become complacent. [My job is] to eliminate complacency from the organization." — *Miljan Gutovic*
"Very often people are scared or reluctant to make a decision because they believe that decision might be wrong. We need to encourage decision-making... make a decision today even if it's half good, because in three months' time the world will change so fast that this decision will be relevant." — *Miljan Gutovic*

It's a region where there are global megatrends that are actually shaping the future of the construction industry. You see population is growing, urbanization is increasing, people want to live better, and governments are committed to spend on residential and infrastructure. And we do have an excellent footprint in Latin America. We just concluded a big acquisition in Peru, which is an amazing market that is booming. There are mining investments, there are agriculture investments, and with our presence now in Peru, we are happy to capture this growth. But the biggest country for us is definitely Mexico. Mexico is a country where we are seeing accelerated growth when it comes to the construction industry. The new government under President Claudia has made it very clear: she wants to see Mexico in the top 10 economies by 2030. She is committed to infra spending, she wants to build 1.8 million homes in the next five years, and some of this has already started. How... how different is it to do business in Latin America compared to Europe and the US? It is different. In Latin America, nearly half of our customers are self-builders. These are people that buy cement in bags, and they mix it on site to renovate their home or to increase the space of their house. So what we did, we actually changed the route to the market. We operate close to 2,500 retail construction shops called Disensa where we are servicing this very powerful customer base. Through our shops, most of which are franchises, we are able to supply up to 85% of their needs. Let's move topic a bit. You recently came out with a statement about AI and quantified the savings that you expect to get from the implementation here. How do you think about AI in Holcim? It is definitely changing the way we process and produce. It is something that we have been doing now for several years. And yes, this year we came out with the ambitious plan to hit 200 million Swiss francs in net benefits by 2028. Just imagine, Nicolai, we are running complex industrial operations. Our factories, on average, are 1,000 by 500 meters in size. They're big industrial complexes, and we need to operate 24/7. In such a situation, you wish you could have a crystal ball to be able to predict the future of when a section of the plant will stop. With AI, what we are doing internally is generating huge amounts of data. This data is sent to AI platforms where we have specialized algorithms which were developed just for Holcim. These AI platforms are giving us warning signals that something might go wrong in a certain section of the plant. So with this info, we are able to organize ourselves better. We are able to plan the shutdowns and also to better plan the maintenance in our production facilities. So this is not laying off people? Absolutely not. This is about enhancing operational efficiency. This is about exercising strict cost discipline. It's not about laying off people; in fact, it's actually about upskilling people. You mentioned previously your approach to decarbonization. What does it mean to be a leading partner for sustainable construction? Just what does it mean in practice, this decarbonization you talk about? We put sustainability at the core of our strategy. When we talk about sustainability, we talk about decarbonization and reducing our CO2 footprint. We talk about circularity, which means recycling old construction and demolition materials into the new ones. And we talk about reducing fresh water usage. When it comes to decarbonization, we are leveraging all the levers possible to reduce our CO2 footprint. As I mentioned, we are reformulating our products, we are changing our energy mix, and also we are working on the next-generation projects related to carbon capture. Now, cement accounts for, I think it is 8% of global CO2 emissions, even higher than aviation. Where do you think it could go to? Well, we were the first company in the industry that committed to net zero by 2050. So it is a long journey, but it's a journey that will bring many innovations and it will change the way we operate today. I'll give you one concrete example. For instance, during the last decade, we have been working on reducing fossil fuels in our operations, replacing them with alternative fuels. What we do, Nicolai, is basically we get paid to take the waste materials, we process this waste into an energy source, and we use it instead of the traditional fossil fuels. By doing this, we are reducing costs, but we are reducing CO2 as well. So for us, sustainability is a driver of profitable growth. We are doing it because, first of all, it's the right thing to do, and for us it makes perfect business sense. I mentioned to you some of the products that we launched. On these products we do have cost advantages because we are using our production know-how and our formulation know-how. We are able to reduce the cost in these products as well as reducing CO2. That's why we keep bringing this statement back over and over again: that at Holcim, sustainability is actually driving profitable growth. Okay, so greener products will not be more expensive? We do get a small price premium, but in fact, we do have a cost advantage. We are using this to reinvest into new initiatives and to invest into new formulations. Probably in four or five years, formulation will change as we will be developing the next generation of alternative raw materials. When you came out with your... your plans and your visions, I mean, we have to admit that it was pretty fashionable to... to bring out these kind of plans, and subsequently, we've had a political shift in the US. Has that changed the way you look at... the way you look at these things? Absolutely not. Still today, sustainability is at the core of our strategy. Sustainability and innovations are driving our growth and will continue to drive our growth in the years to come. I can give you an example for Europe, for instance. The last five years, one of the best performing regions in our portfolio was Europe, despite all economic challenges and market conditions. In the last five years, we have managed to increase our profit in Europe by 430 basis points. One of the key drivers behind this was investments in sustainable offerings, in decarbonization, and also in circular construction. Can we talk a bit about culture? How would you define the corporate culture at Holcim? If you walk into Holcim, you will see one slogan consistently everywhere. If you go into our office in Mexico City, in Zurich, or in Sydney, you will see "Holcim Spirit." When you are going through a spin-off and I took over as a CEO, my first goal was that we align the teams on the ground. So we launched this thing called Holcim Spirit, which is based on three Ps: Purpose, People, Performance. We all have the same purpose: innovation and sustainability at the core of everything we do. People: still the biggest asset of this company are people—45,000 of our employees. This is what I'm especially proud of. We launched, for instance, Holcim University. Last year we had 3,000 of our employees going through Holcim University. We are constantly investing in people, and we want Holcim to be a place where we reward performance, we nurture talent, and where we encourage innovation. What were the changes you put in place when you took over as CEO? One of the changes is this Holcim Spirit, where we started investing heavily in upskilling our next generation of leaders. And the last P, which is equally important, Nicolai, is performance. One of the key strengths of this company is our deeply embedded performance culture. This goes to the way we are organized. We are a fully decentralized business, and the reason why is that our business is local for local. We source everything locally, we produce everything locally, and we sell to local customers. Our corporate structure is very slim. It all goes down to the country level, and the countries are driving the performance of Holcim. That's why we are the company with industry-leading EBIT margins. We have the most advanced decarbonization roadmap in our industry. This all goes back to our deeply embedded performance culture and our fully decentralized business model. How do you look at the trade-off between decentralization and having one culture and one system? Look, at the end of the day, we do have a slim corporate organization. I see them as mentors to coach the local teams, to provide constructive criticism, or to share best demonstrated practices. Personally, one of the things that I like most about my job is to spend time on the ground. I travel a lot, nearly every week. I do enjoy spending time in our factories and on construction sites. This is where me, as part of the core corporate team, I am learning from the countries, and then these learnings we are copying across the rest of our footprint. What would people say about you as a leader? Oh look, they will probably say that I keep repeating three things over and over again. I have three very simple rules. The first rule is control the controllables. Very simple. I don't want to spend time having endless meetings talking about the geopolitical situation or predicting the future. Impossible. Our goal is to focus on the things that we can control, on our strategy, and on our initiatives. All my meetings are about internal discussions on what we can do better, exercising strict cost discipline, and improving operational efficiency. The second big statement that I keep repeating, and I strongly believe that this is the case, is that complacency is our biggest enemy. I see my job as eliminating complacency from the organization. Because, you know, when the company is doing well—I mean, look at our performance the last several years, look at the performance of some of our key geographies—people have a tendency to become complacent. How do you prevent that from happening? Well, from constructive criticism to benchmarking, in many different ways. The third pillar, the third point that I should mention, is this benchmarking. I like football, I like tennis, I like sports in general. I like to benchmark internally and externally. We do provide a lot of benchmarking to the countries to encourage them to do even better. "Why is this country doing better than you? Find a way to improve, copy, and replicate best demonstrated practices." These three rules are probably the ones I've repeated a thousand times, and I will keep repeating them in the years to come. I think you were born in Sarajevo and grew up in a place which wasn't always the most peaceful place. How do you think that has impacted you as a person and as a leader? Yes, growing up there, it wasn't... it was not an easy period in my life. But then moving to Australia, where I finished school, uni, got married, and had my kids there, then moving to Dubai, and now the last seven years in Switzerland—working from Australia to Europe, in different locations—I think it did really shape me. I learned a lot about new cultures, and I learned about different ways of conducting business. I believe this is helping me today and this will help me in the future, as long as I continue to evolve and I continue to learn. It's exciting. Now I'm spending more and more time in Latin America—a fascinating region where I personally can learn a lot on a personal level, to develop myself on a personal level, but as well on a professional level. What do you think is the key to cultural understanding? Look, I think what we are facing today is that the world is changing on a daily basis. We need to change as well. We cannot be stuck in the same time forever; we need to evolve as the world is evolving. What I see today when I talk to the young generation of my colleagues is these people are very smart. They have access to information in a second. But at the same time, I feel often they are excellent at identifying the problems, but not very often are they able to bring the solution to the problem. Very often people are scared or reluctant to make a decision because they believe that decision might be wrong. And this is something that we need to change. We need to encourage the decision-making process. We need to encourage the next generation to bring a problem together with the solution. This is a culture change that will take a while. I think it's quite interesting. Most companies, the more I speak to CEOs, the more I realize that most companies are struggling with the same things. You know, people generally are quite short-term thinking, don't particularly want to share a lot of information because it takes time and effort. They don't particularly want to take risks. And the job as a CEO is basically to extend people's thinking in terms of time, get them to share information, better feedback systems. It is... it is the same in so many places. So, very interesting. What is your biggest mistake? Oh look, I have made many mistakes, but it's not about the mistakes. I believe it's about learning. But I mean, tell us... tell us about something which went really pear-shaped. I think very often I was too slow to make changes on a personnel level. I was sometimes too flexible when it came to some organizational changes. And the list goes on and on. But when it comes to this discussion, I always go back to—and I love this quote, maybe I won't repeat exactly what he said—but Thomas Edison, he kept saying, "I have not failed. I have just found something like 10,000 ways that won't work." So it's not about mistakes. I keep telling my people: it's about learning, it's about developing. Make a mistake. And it's better to make a decision today, even if it's half good, because in three months' time the world will change so fast that this decision will be relevant. You said that you had been too slow in making personnel changes. Have you seen many cases where you've had a problem with people and then it's actually improved? Oh, it happened. It happened sometimes. People do need to be woken up. Sometimes people... I've seen, for instance, in the high-performing teams, when after a while, because they've been praised so many times, they do become complacent. And I go back to my second principle. I really believe that complacency can be one of the biggest enemies in the company. So how do... so somebody has been praised a lot of times and they go into some kind of, you know, hibernation or whatever, right? How do you wake them up? It could be an inspirational speech to motivate them, but at the end of the day, it's dealing with the facts. It's dealing with some benchmarking, presenting comparisons, and there are so many different ways. There is no one single point, but that's a part of the job. It is my job to ensure that teams are performing, and very often, even the best performing teams, they need to be woken up. You have a PhD. Does that mean that you are micromanaging? Oh look, I would not say that I am micromanaging, but I do like to know what's going on in my business deep down. I like to be able to talk to a plant manager in Egypt on the same level, to ask him about certain KPIs that usually a CEO would not know. So I would not call it micromanaging; I would call it more curiosity and comparing the performance of different organizations. What's the kind of leader you do not want to be? Puff. I don't want to be a micromanager, since you mentioned it. I like to empower my people, I like to give them free hands, I like to have open and frank constructive discussions, and at the end of the day, it's all about teamwork. They do not work for me; they work with me, and we are all in the same boat. On a... on a personal note, how do you relax? Oh, different ways. I like spending time with family and friends. Occasionally, I like watching sports, I like to play tennis, to do cycling, and reading books. So different, different... but family is on top of the list, and friends. What do you read? I have read many; I really went all over the place. In high school, I had an obsession with ancient Greece—especially Sparta. Then during university, I was obsessed with *War and Peace* and Russian literature. Then later on, the last few years, I am reading a lot of biographies. From Einstein to Nikola Tesla, all the way to Musk and different, different people. What kind of music do you play? The last few years, Chopin, Bach. I like to relax. Kids sometimes make fun of me when we are driving in a car. Because you have a very old person's music taste? No, no, no. They... or maybe. It's not good for... you know when you do this thing on Spotify and you get "what's your music age," you are like up at 100 years or something. I'm a... I'm a 27. I'm a 27, you are at 85. So who do you like these days, Nicolai? I'm not going to tell you. I'm... I'm into mood music, you know. Taylor Swift? No, no, no, no, no, no. Lastly, what is your advice to young people? Be bold, don't be scared to make mistakes, and be open-minded. I believe young people, when they enter an organization, need to spend more time on networking. I'm not a big fan of this working-from-home policy. I like to see them in the office talking to the older generation of workers so they can learn, they can absorb their expertise. So it's all about open thinking, evolving, learning, and also being brave to make a mistake, and being curious to ask questions. Miljan, this has been great talking to you. Big thanks, and please continue to construct—make apartments, make roads, make buildings, you know. We... we love it. Big thank you. Will do, Nicolai. Thanks for having me. All the very best. Thank you. Thank you.

Ageing as an Asset

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·52:39·348 views·2026-06-24
TL;DRTo unlock the multi-trillion-dollar "longevity dividend," global economies must transition from treating aging as a demographic crisis to capturing its immense potential through 80-year financial planning, systemic support for female caregivers, and ...
a shift from reactive...

TL;DR

To unlock the multi-trillion-dollar "longevity dividend," global economies must transition from treating aging as a demographic crisis to capturing its immense potential through 80-year financial planning, systemic support for female caregivers, and a shift from reactive medical care to proactive self-care.

Redefining Financial Security and the Career Lifecycle

  • The Breakdown of Traditional Retirement Models: Kevin Strain (President and CEO, Sun Life Financial) highlighted that the traditional financial planning model is broken. Historically, individuals worked for 40 years to fund a 10-to-15-year retirement. Today, retirement can easily last 40 years or more, requiring financial plans to cover an 80-year horizon.
      • Accumulation vs. De-accumulation: Financial institutions must shift their focus from merely "achieving" retirement security to "building" it dynamically, helping clients manage assets through both the accumulation and active spending (de-accumulation) phases of a longer life.
  • The $7 Trillion Silver Economy: David Jacob (CEO, Marsh Asia) emphasized that early retirement represents a massive loss of untapped economic productivity.
      • Extending Work Spans: Creating flexible, "gig-style" corporate platforms for workers aged 55 to 70 keeps older adults mentally engaged and physically healthier, directly lowering the rates of Alzheimer's, dementia, and cognitive decline.
      • Public and Private Savings: Prolonging the productivity of older workers keeps them wealthier (allowing them to invest more in their own health) and dramatically reduces the burden on public and private health spending.

Gender Economics, Caregiving, and the Fertility Crisis

  • The Caregiving Penalty: The financial burden of eldercare and childcare falls disproportionately on women. According to the joint World Economic Forum (WEF) and Marsh report, a single year of caregiving combined with the gender pay gap can reduce a woman's retirement savings by almost 25%.
  • The "Motherhood Penalty" vs. Direct Costs: Liu Qian (Founder and CEO, Wusawa Advisory) highlighted that the economic barriers to raising children are heavily misunderstood:
      • In China, the direct cost of raising a child from age 0 to 18 is approximately $68,000 USD (extremely high relative to GDP per capita).
      • However, the "motherhood penalty"—the opportunity cost of a mother’s lost career progression and lifetime income—is even higher at $78,000 USD.
  • Redistributing Domestic Labor to Boost Fertility: Global data shows that fertility rates drop fastest in countries experiencing rapid economic growth and rising female autonomy that still cling to traditional family values (e.g., Japan, South Korea, Italy, Spain).
      • Paternal Leave Policies: True economic sustainability requires policy mechanisms like Sweden’s, which offers 18 months of parental leave with at least 3 months reserved exclusively for fathers on a non-transferable basis.
      • Economic Recognition: Incorporating non-transactional care work into formal economic metrics via "care credits" would protect women's retirement funds and structurally grow the global GDP.
      • Male Health Factors: Public policy must also address biological fertility; global sperm counts have declined by over 60% in the last 45 years due to environmental pollution, plastics, and lifestyle choices.

Shifting from Reactive Medicine to Active Wellbeing

  • The Unsustainability of Current Medical Systems: Chawalit Frederick Tsao (Chairman, TPC Group) argued that neither government budgets nor private insurance models can sustain the current trajectory of rising medical care costs.
      • From Medical Care to Self-Care: Public health policy must transition from reactive, clinical "medical care" to proactive, lifestyle-based "self-care and self-love."
      • Mindset Shift: Chronic stress acts as a systemic "death sentence" for the human body. Health ministries globally must pivot toward education that empowers individuals to take personal ownership of their mental and physical wellbeing.
  • Gross National Happiness (GNH) Over GDP: As artificial intelligence and automation assume transactional production tasks, the primary metric of a successful society should shift from raw GDP to wellbeing, happiness, and environmental harmony.

The Dual Power of Technology and Low-Cost Intervention

  • AI as a Demographic Cushion: While Western observers often warn that China’s declining population will trigger economic disaster, Kevin Strain argued that AI and advanced automation turn this decline into an economic advantage by replacing lost labor and boosting efficiency without causing mass unemployment.
  • High-Tech Medicine Extending Lifespans: Cutting-edge medical innovations are fundamentally changing survival rates. Examples include breaking the blood-brain barrier to treat blood clots using non-invasive ultrasound, and performing major lung surgeries that allow 85-year-old patients to walk within 24 hours.
  • The $6 Trillion Low-Tech Opportunity: Despite high-tech medical breakthroughs, the WEF/Marsh report highlights that the largest economic savings are remarkably low-tech.
      • Implementing simple, low-cost preventative measures—such as encouraging regular exercise, using hearing aids, and preventing physical falls in the elderly—could unlock $6 trillion USD in healthcare savings globally by 2040.

Notable Quotes

"We're used to one-year plans, five-year strategies. This is 80 years of thinking through what does your plan look like to both accumulate wealth, protect it, and de-accumulate wealth." — Kevin Strain
"Economic research has found out that the best solution to change the dropping fertility rate is for the fathers to pick up more housework." — Liu Qian
"We need to change from a medical care mindset to a healthcare, self-care, self-love mindset. And where everybody is responsible for the individual health." — Chawalit Frederick Tsao

You know, we've been talking about ageing for years as a crisis, a challenge. But I'd like to put this to you. If living longer has actually been one of humanity's greatest achievements, why is it that our economy still seems so unprepared for it? So perhaps the question isn't so much whether or not longevity is an asset, but why are we failing to capitalize on its value? To introduce myself, my name is Olivia Siong. I'm an editor and correspondent with CNA. I'm very happy to be here and to be joined by a very esteemed panel next to me. I'd like to just introduce them. Mr. Kevin Strain is the president and CEO of Sun Life Financial. We also have Mr. Chawalit Frederick Tsao, chairman of TPC Group. So, I do encourage you to start thinking of those burning questions you have to ask our panelists. Many of the themes that we'll be discussing today are, you know, framed around very interesting findings in that report. We were on a panel about ageing as well last year, but we'd like to start with a quickfire round. So, just one thought, one sentence, no elaboration. All of you engage with communities, governments, and business leaders. What is one misconception about ageing? Kevin? I think that... well, one sentence, that's hard actually. No elaboration. No elaboration. It's... a misconception is that it's a straight-line process, and it's not a straight-line process. It's different for everybody. Okay. Fred? Oh, well, ageing is a natural process. But don't let the fear of death, when you get closer, get the better of you. Okay. Qian? On top of my mind, we think about ageing as typically women that support the men. So it's the wives, the daughters, and the daughters-in-law that support the old men. And we need to change that. Mm-hm. David? You can be optimal at any age. Okay. I have many questions following that round. But I'd like to start with Kevin. You mentioned it's not a straight line. And I know you've spoken before about how we cannot think of our lifespan as separated between work, health, and finances. And so, when Sun Life talks about ensuring a long time of financial security for its people, why does that model break down? Why does the separation break down? Why doesn't it work when it comes to, you know, now that we all live longer? Yeah, I think that if you think about it, as a company, we've been around for 160 years. Our purpose is to help clients achieve lifetime financial security and lead healthier lives. Our purpose of lifetime financial security has been with us most of that 160 years. We identified a long time ago that that was a combination of life insurance for protection reasons, health insurance for protection reasons, and wealth management for wealth creation. We realized that you needed long-term thinking and long-term plans that saw across each of those categories. In the last 10 years, we saw an opportunity to add to that this concept of living healthier lives. Work is healthy; work is how you accumulate your wealth, and it is healthy for you. We also found that we knew a lot about people through our benefits, and that we could help them lead healthier lives and live healthier lives. So, we added that. But even more recently, we've been thinking about this concept of achieving, because in fact, now you don't really "achieve" lifetime financial security. When your retirement was lasting 10 years, you may achieve it. You'd work 40 years, and you'd have a retirement that lasted 10 or 15 years. People could handle the math of what that looked like. Now, you might work 40 years and your savings have to last 40 years, or potentially in the future, even longer. And so, we're shifting our thinking from achieving lifetime financial security to building it. You have to think about that being both your accumulation phase, but also your de-accumulation phase. How do you think about your assets, your wealth, and your health in retirement differently, making them part of an interactive solution? One of the things that slows people down is that you have to think long-term. We're used to one-year plans and five-year strategies. But this is 80 years of thinking through what your plan looks like to both accumulate wealth, protect it, and de-accumulate wealth. What does your plan look like to live healthier through those combinations? And you're seeing that because people are living longer and living healthier, they're also enjoying their retirement differently, which is part of what I was saying with the straight line, right? In your early years of retirement, you want to use more of the wealth that you've created, but you don't want to use so much that you don't have enough to save for the future, right? I think that people struggle to understand long-term thinking. That is where advice comes in, and I know we have some advisors who are here today as well. That's where that advice of talking to people about what it looks like, how the combination of these things goes together, and what your retirement is going to look like and feel like comes into play. We're trying to do more. We had a session that we did with our senior leaders at MIT, and MIT has a longevity lab. We are trying to learn more about what your life looks like in retirement today so that we can best suit financial plans to that. David, I'd like to bring you in here. Kevin was talking about how maybe institutions today are not exactly geared up for a longer lifespan. I'd like to push that argument a little bit further. When it comes to retirement, there's a telling statistic: the number of people aged over 65 worldwide is expected to increase by more than 50 percent by 2040. So, does retirement still make sense if people are living longer, to 90, to 100, and beyond? I know you have some views about that. Yeah, I mean, I think it's important to understand that we are sitting on a huge economic opportunity with ageing. Think about the untapped productivity of people who leave the organization too early in their careers. Whether that is because they are being sort of forced out because of certain age-related rules, it contributes to this whole concept that lifespan is outgrowing health span. That puts an enormous burden on both private and public spending when it comes down to healthcare. So, I think it's not just companies that have a duty, but it's also governments really thinking about, first and foremost, how do we prolong productivity for the older workforce? Are there opportunities for those aged 55 to 70 to continue extending their work span in a company? Ultimately, if they are healthier, more productive, and have more purpose, then there is less burden on society going forward. As you said, it is not straightforward, but we are staring at quite a huge statistic in terms of the number of people who will be retiring soon. I'd just like to quickly follow up on that, though. You talk about encouraging workers to work for longer periods of time and having purpose, but another question is whether or not people want to do that. If you're dealing with societies that have a young population that also has unemployment issues, that would be a challenge, wouldn't it? Yeah, you're right. So, there has to be a balance. Certain geographies or certain demographics will have different opportunities, but it is going to be a balance. I do feel that there is a tremendous opportunity here. They talk about this "silver economy" as a 7-trillion-dollar opportunity. How do we capture that effectively going forward? I do feel—you know, I'm 58, right?—I want to continue working longer, but I want to make sure that I'm healthy. If you're healthy, you're more productive at work. If you're more productive at work, the wealthier you get. The wealthier you get, the more you can spend on health. So, those dynamics need to continue to work together. It is a changing mindset as well. Behaviour is key, but behaviour is hard. If you think about many corporations that have put together wellness programs for their employees, the take-up rates are still very low. So, how do we get behaviour change for employees to embrace the fact that they need to be healthy now to be able to be productive going forward? Qian, I'd like to bring you in there because we've been talking about the costs, perhaps, of ageing. A lot of the discussion when it comes to ageing is about healthcare costs and the healthcare burden, but you have a different perspective on that. Before we get to that, I mean, you are an economist. You focus a lot on geopolitics and trade, but you also do a lot of research on gender economics. There was a finding in the report that really struck me, which said that a single year of caregiving combined with the gender pay gap can reduce a woman's retirement savings by almost 25 percent. That is a staggering figure to me, as a woman myself. So, what is your research telling you about perhaps what businesses, governments, or industry leaders are missing here? Yeah, so usually when we think about ageing, we think about old people. But I want to talk about this from a sustainability perspective, which is about the young and the babies. Instead of talking about ageing per se, I want to come in from the angle of fertility issues that we have around the world—specifically, the decreasing level of fertility throughout the world. Ultimately, the young support the old, and the babies and the young become the future old. For one, we are seeing this drop in fertility rates, especially in the past several years, which is partly COVID-related. For instance, people substituted dating, having babies, and marriage with alcohol and computer games. They were not going out to pubs, and we saw rising depression rates. So, that's part of it. But Harvard Professor Claudia Goldin actually looked at this. In her study, she compared the US over the past couple of hundred years, and she also looked at more than 20 OECD countries. She found that while the global trend of fertility has been going down, there are two different groups of countries that really stand out. The ones that experienced the more pronounced fertility drops are countries that tend to have, on one hand, very rapid economic growth and rising women's empowerment and autonomy, but on the other hand, retain very traditional values. These are countries such as Italy, Spain, Japan, and South Korea. While these countries were growing extremely fast, traditional family values kicked in where women are the ones taking care of the babies and doing the majority of the housework. When she was comparing all these countries, she started to realize the conflict between female autonomy and traditional family values. Basically, countries where men share much less of the housework compared to women are the countries that experience a much faster drop in fertility rates. This is telling us that for us to have a more sustainable fertility rate and ageing population, men need to step in and do more housework. That is a very important conclusion we are seeing from studies covering the last 200 years across more than 20 countries around the world. I find that absolutely fascinating. So, that is one. On top of the willingness to have babies, there is also the problem of infertility. We usually think about why people no longer want to date, marry, and have babies, but the truth is, when we look at couples who do want to have babies, one-sixth of them are unable to. This is because of all kinds of biological and environmental problems. It is very important to know this because traditionally we feel like if a woman is unable to get pregnant, she needs to exercise, or she needs to reduce cigarettes or alcohol. But science tells us that is not the case; men account for at least 50 percent of the problem. One of the most fascinating studies related to this was by a group of professors led by Levine. They looked at sperm counts throughout the world over the last 45 years and realized that the total sperm count has reduced by more than 60 percent worldwide across both developed countries and emerging markets. This is caused by a combination of health, lifestyle, pollution, what we eat, and certain plastics that we use. So, what this is telling us is that in order to increase fertility—and hence the future opportunities of an ageing population—it is equally important to care about men's health and their sperm quality. I'll stop there for now. Food for thought. Before we move into a question for Fred, I'd like to get David and Kevin's perspective on this gender gap we are talking about. It's a long-term issue, starting even before people begin getting old. Is that gap starting to be reflected in businesses, with clients, in plans, or in the advice that you are giving? I think what you see is that it is somewhat different by country. If I talk about Canada, we've known for many years that in the families we serve, women make more of the financial decisions than men. Our best advisors are often the ones who know how to tap into that, how to speak to that, and how to understand that family dynamic. Again, if you're thinking about lifetime financial security and healthier lives, it takes a plan that you stick to over time. I'm not sure if it matters whether it's a man or a woman; it's really about figuring out a plan for the family that they can work at. Nobody achieves lifetime financial security in one year. There's nobody who buys life insurance, health insurance, a retirement product, and an education savings product all in one year. It never happens because nobody makes enough money to do all of that at once. So, it's really about finding how to talk to the family. It's about discussing what the costs are going to look like and what the program is to achieve this, because this is not an easy process. You have to decide that there is something you want to do this year that you are not going to be able to do because you are saving towards these future goals. I think that conversation—and teaching people how to have that conversation—is key. Often, women do think about this differently than men, and so we try to train our advisors and our distribution partners on how to approach those conversations with families in a way that is empowering as they make those decisions. Yeah, I was just going to say that it's true. The burden of caregiving falls almost squarely on women. In order for companies to be entirely successful, we need to make sure that our women are well protected if they have to leave the organization to provide caregiving to their elderly relatives or parents. We have to ensure that their position in the firm is secure so they are able to come back and not miss a beat. I think this is where governments need to help as well in terms of pensions. People who leave companies for five years lose out on that. So, there have to be these sorts of care credits, as discussed in the recent longevity report, that you can actually tap into to protect you. It's a new idea. Yeah, just to add to that: as a trained economist, I know GDP basically only counts economic activities that are financial transactions. If I care for the elderly, my husband, or my kids without being paid, that is not part of the official economy. By actually giving a proper and adequate amount of credits to women who do all this care work, the whole world economy can grow significantly. Fred, I'd like to bring you into the conversation here. A lot of the talk is about how we can make people productive and how we can show that they are contributing to the economy. If you were to advise a government or a business given your expertise, is there a different measure we should be looking at beyond an economic measure like GDP if we want to measure how well we are addressing longevity? I think that measurements about wellbeing and happiness can only be answered by individuals because it is inside us, not outside us. Currently, we have a system based on medical care, and government medical systems cannot hold. Insurance medical systems keep going up and will soon not be able to hold either. As individuals, we have to understand that healthcare is actually very cheap. But healthcare can only be achieved through education and a shift of mindsets—including mindsets around gender issues, faith issues, race issues, and age issues. This can only be done through a change in how we relate, and through education. Of course, education can be formal, or it can come from family, business, and social media. We have to think about a way of intervening to change our mindset. If you want healthcare, first you have to start with self-care. That means the work has to be done by yourself, and the cost is very low. If you're healthy, you have no medical bills. That is, of course, the right way for governments to go. But governments don't understand much about health. The health ministry, much like the education ministry, is the most difficult ministry to change. It is the most difficult ministry to change everywhere around the world, including in China. They work very hard to change, but the health ministry is based on Western medicine, and they cannot get out of that medical care mindset. Even in China, pushing Chinese medicine faces big problems because the entire administration is Western-trained with a medical care mindset. Similarly, our education is an industrial education system—embraced by parents, teachers, and the education minister. Even though China has used so many ways and so many rules to try to shift things, it still cannot succeed because until the mindset changes in parents, students, and the education ministry, the status quo remains. Consciousness is the foundation of shifting. If we are not even conscious, we will not want to change. If we are conscious, we want to change. Once you are conscious of how to change, you change. So, education on all fronts, in collaboration, is needed to change reality and shift that consciousness. There is no other way. We need to change from a medical care mindset to a healthcare, self-care, and self-love mindset, where everybody is responsible for their individual health. Of course, you cannot have isolated individual health because we live a whole life within a whole universe system, both visible and invisible. You might ask, what is invisible? Do you think your body decides, or your mind? What's happening in your mind works the body; the body is only a vehicle for your mind. The universe works like that; it is a whole life system. The Chinese got it. They said we have to be in oneness with the cosmos in the way we live, and then we will have wealth, health, and happiness—including caring for the Earth as well, because we live in it. I think that brings us to a great point. That sums it up for us: there is a mindset shift that needs to happen. We are talking about different aspects—gender, business, and financial security. But we don't want to just talk about the issues we are facing now. I'd also like to move on to discuss some of the solutions and opportunities that come with longevity. Qian, I'd like to bring in China as an example, as Fred mentioned. China has one of the fastest ageing populations in the world, and its population aged 65 and above is expected to grow by almost 70 percent by 2040. Many people see demographics as a challenge. But when it comes to China's case, is it a challenge, or is it an opportunity for it to be a laboratory for longevity? There is certainly opportunity. For instance, when we look at this in China itself, the estimate is that the silver economy is about 10 percent of the GDP, which is quite significant. Until last year, the official retirement age for men was 60, and for women it was 50 or 55. The gap between the official retirement age and their final days is so long that this will increase opportunities for savings, pensions, expenditures, services, and communities. That is the plus side. However, I am also very worried about the significant drop—the cliff that we are looking at with the fertility drops in China. Let me come back to that when we talk about solutions. When we look at the world, there is no magical solution to all of a sudden flip things over and stop fertility drops, but there are ways we can slow it down. At the moment, the mainstream policy in China is to give more subsidies to encourage couples by giving them more money to lessen the burden. But we look into this and argue that this is not really the right policy direction. A few years ago, some economists did a study on the fertility cost in China. They found that to raise a kid in China from age zero to 18 costs, on average, 68,000 US dollars. That ratio of cost versus GDP per head is several times higher compared to France, Germany, or the US. So, in China, it is actually very expensive to raise a kid. In response, the government has started to say, "Okay, we're going to give more subsidies to cover milk powder, education fees, housing, etc." But I work with Professor Zuo Yaohui and Zhang Xiaobo on this, and we find that there is actually another very important cost. In some of the leading countries, there is a concept called the "motherhood penalty." This is not a direct cost that you pay out of pocket for the baby, but rather the opportunity cost of the mother staying in her career. On average globally, every child is equal to about 20 to 30 percent of a woman's lifetime income. We borrowed that concept and did the calculation for China, and we realized that this number is 78,000 US dollars. This means that just the single cost of the motherhood penalty is much higher than all the milk powder, education, and housing facilities combined. This means that if there were only one kind of policy, the solution should emphasize helping women to lessen the burden of housework. And let me take back what I just said about "helping women," because it is not just the woman's responsibility; it is the household's and the entire society's responsibility to take care of the kids. So, my solution would be, rather than saying "let's empower women this way" or reminding women to behave more responsibly as mothers or wives, we should encourage dads to pick up more housework. We should glorify the dads and say, "You're a super husband and a super dad when you do more work." I think that is the solution. Actually, it is not just what I think; economic research has found that the best solution to change the dropping fertility rate is for fathers to pick up more housework. We see you have supporters here, Qian. So Kevin, no pressure. The next question is for you. How are you viewing China's changing demographics, particularly from the financial planning services point of view? Yeah, let me take a step back. Sun Life is a truly global company; we are in 28 countries around the world, and I've been to China probably four or five times in the last 12 months. When I meet other executives, there is a dialogue in the US where people like to say that China's declining population is going to spell economic disaster. I have been turning this around on them to say, "In an AI world where things are going to become more efficient, isn't this actually an advantage, not a disadvantage?" While we are trying to search for jobs for people who are losing them, AI is replacing that sort of trend. I think a lot of the talk, especially in the West, about China's economy and population is misplaced in a world where AI is changing that calculation. We are seeing that calculation change all over the place. People are just one factor now—a very important factor, the most important factor—but technology is changing things so that the number of people is no longer the only factor. I think that is something quite new to all of us. We are starting to think about that. I have Laura, our head of IT, in the front row here. We are thinking about when we have programmers: do we have a thousand programmers, or do we have a thousand programmers powered by a thousand agents, effectively giving us 2,000 programmers? The AI and technology changes that are happening are going to be major factors. It is also a major factor when you think about healthier lives. I am on the board of a hospital in Canada—the second-largest hospital board—and we are seeing unbelievable treatments that never existed before. We have broken the blood-brain barrier, which in essence means you can now treat blood clots in the brain using ultrasound. You don't have to do surgery or anything like that. There is so much technology changing that allows people to live longer, and that is super important as well. If you think about China, I'd go back and say there are 1.4 billion people living here. There are amazing technologies and amazing universities and education in Qingdao. You are seeing incredible things, and I think the best way for the world to take advantage of these is to have innovation happening everywhere, sharing it, and learning from it. That is going to add to the length of people's lives. But then it comes back to the fact that we all want to have money and a nice retirement. Thinking about that longevity over the long term is fundamental to our purpose. We need to understand what is happening to the economy, what is happening with the digital transformation and AI, and how that is impacting things like health. So, technology certainly has given us many opportunities, some of which we may not even know the full extent of just yet. But here is another provocative finding from the report. In fact, while many conversations we've been having are indeed about AI and how it will clearly help with longevity, the WEF report finds that the longevity opportunity has, quote, "nothing to do with artificial intelligence," and everything to do with how we organize health, work, and care. Indeed, it finds that low-tech, low-cost measures could unlock 6 trillion US dollars in healthcare savings by 2040. That comes from simple things, as the report finds, like exercise, having hearing aids, or preventing falls. Yeah, all of the basics and fundamentals are really important. But don't underestimate the impact of AI to analyze what's happening and build on top of that. Yeah. David, what are your thoughts on this? Yeah, I mean, there is no doubt that technology can play a significant part in the ageing discussion. I will say, though, that it is not the end-all, right? When he was 80 years old, having smoked his whole life—60 years, three packs a day—he got lung cancer. He was treated with chemotherapy, radiation, and surgery. During the surgery, the lump was so big they had to take his left lung out. They performed the surgery through his back, using new technology that didn't exist 20 years ago. He was walking within 24 hours and is perfectly healthy today. That is where technology is fundamentally changing how we survive illnesses and helping us prepare for the fact that we are going to live far longer. If my dad had had that surgery 20 years ago, he wouldn't be living five or six years later and being perfectly healthy. They would have cut him open from the front and taken the lung out, which would have been very complex. So, technology is fundamentally changing how we survive illnesses.
