How Startup Cyera Became A $12 Billion AI Security Juggernaut

Forbes(2.1M)·13:14·1.3K views·2026-06-24
Yotam SegevCofounder and CEO, Cyera Tamar Bar-IlanCofounder and CTO, Cyera
TL;DRCyera, an AI-native data security platform valued at $12 billion, is addressing massive enterprise data vulnerabilities caused by rapid AI and autonomous agent adoption by offering a unified visibility and control suite powered by strategic funding and aggressive acquisitions.

TL;DR

Cyera, an AI-native data security platform valued at $12 billion, is addressing massive enterprise data vulnerabilities caused by rapid AI and autonomous agent adoption by offering a unified visibility and control suite powered by strategic funding and aggressive acquisitions.

The Core Vulnerability: Shadow Data and Autonomous AI Risks

  • The Visibility Gap: Most major corporations have no accurate grasp of what sensitive data they possess or where it resides across thousands of cloud systems and databases.
      • Co-founders Yotam Segev (CEO) and Tamar Bar-Ilan (CTO)—both veterans of the Israeli Defense Force's elite Unit 8200—launched Cyera in 2021 to solve this decades-old enterprise visibility challenge using large and small language models.
  • The Danger of Autonomous Agents: Traditional security relied on "security by obscurity," assuming humans would never access or search through all the data they technically had permissions to view.
      • The Agent Factor: Unlike humans, autonomous AI agents search, read, and process infinite quantities of data at extreme speeds. They systematically uncover "buried sins" (unsecured, sensitive data left in unused corporate folders).
      • Lack of Guardrails: Agents are frequently deployed without proper guardrails, leaving them vulnerable to transmitting sensitive corporate, healthcare, or personal data to unauthorized entities.

Cyera's Integrated Security Platform

  • Platform Offerings: Cyera consolidates disparate security tools into a single platform integrated with major IT systems like Microsoft Sentinel, ServiceNow, and Okta:
      • Data Security Posture Management (DSPM): Gives CISOs instant, granular visibility into hundreds of thousands of data stores and individual files.
      • Omni DLP (Data Loss Prevention): An AI-driven orchestration and endpoint client that replaces legacy DLP systems to secure consumer AI tools (e.g., Claude, Microsoft Copilot) on employee endpoints.
      • Identity & Agentic Security: Monitors how human and non-human identities interact with data, implementing real-time guardrails to restrict agent behavior.
  • Real-Time Threat Detection: By analyzing live prompts, responses, and API calls, Cyera can identify and block malicious activity or compliance violations instantly.
      • Insider Trading Example: The platform can detect when an employee uploads embargoed earnings call transcripts to public tools like ChatGPT to solicit investment advice.

Funding and Aggressive Scaling Through M&A

  • War Chest Capitalization: In June 2024, Cyera announced a $600 million Series G funding round at a $12 billion valuation, raising its total funding to over $2 billion.
  • The Speed Advantage: According to CTO Tamar Bar-Ilan, Cyera is leveraging this capital to accelerate time-to-impact and match the blistering pace of enterprise AI adoption.
  • Inorganic Growth (M&A): Cyera actively acquires smaller, specialized startups with strong entrepreneurial teams to fold their tech directly into the core platform.
      • Key Acquisitions: Notable acquisitions include Trail Security, Drift, and Genie Security.
      • Platform Leverage: Once established as a trusted vendor to a corporate CISO, Cyera leverages its distribution network to seamlessly cross-sell these newly integrated technologies.

Hypergrowth vs. Profitability

  • The Valuation Debate: Critics have questioned Cyera’s heavy cash burn and premium valuation, which is estimated at an 80x ARR (Annual Recurring Revenue) multiple.
  • The Investor Defense: Logan Bartlett, Managing Director at Redpoint Ventures (who helped lead Cyera’s Series B), argues the valuation is highly reasonable when adjusted for the company's hypergrowth.
      • Platform Dominance Priority: Bartlett emphasizes that the opportunity cost of not scaling rapidly to secure dominant "platform vendor" status with major CISOs far outweighs the benefit of prioritizing short-term profitability.

Notable Quotes

"We buried a lot of sins in the access people had to data that they never used... A lot of the security at present state is just based on the fact that humans never look for it. They never dig all the way through... and the agents do." — Yotam Segev, CEO & Cofounder
"You do not want to take a car and drive it at 200 miles per hour without an airbag and a seatbelt. You want good brakes, you want an airbag, you want a seatbelt, and that's what makes you comfortable to drive really fast." — Yotam Segev, CEO & Cofounder
"The opportunity cost to not go fast and get into these accounts and establish yourself as a platform vendor that is number one, two, three important to these CISOs, that to me is far more important than prioritizing profitability." — Logan Bartlett, Managing Director at Redpoint Ventures
Every enterprise we met with said data's the new oil, and data's our most important asset. We were very lucky to have started building in a time where AI became top of mind for every enterprise. Silicon and Steel, presented by T-Mobile for Business. As AI models and autonomous agents are rapidly plugged into the corporate world, they are consuming data at a rate not previously seen. But beneath the hype lies a massive, multi-billion dollar vulnerability. Most major corporations have absolutely no idea what data they actually have or where it lives. Enter Cyera. In that world where data is moving from being in just, you know, one or two types of databases to being in hundreds and even thousands of types of systems—in the cloud, in AI systems—in that world, being able to understand what data we have, where that data resides, how that data is being used by whom, and for what purpose, it sounds so trivial. But to do it at enterprise scale and to do it at the speed of technology today is a huge challenge. And that was where the customers pointed Cyera initially. When we started out and started meeting with security teams, what we heard from pretty much everyone was, "I don't know what sensitive data my organization has, and I don't know where it is and what's happening with it." Pretty scary notion, right? We saw the opportunity to solve this problem with models—with large language models, with small language models. And this is the really transformational technology that enabled taking a problem that's been around for decades and hasn't really had a good solution, and making it something that's solvable, feasible, and really works at enterprise scale. Co-founders Yotam Segev and Tamar Bar-Ilan previously built and ran the cloud security division for the Israeli Defense Force's elite Unit 8200. Then, in 2021, they launched Cyera. Now, they are one of the world's fastest growing AI-native data security companies, with integrations across tools like Microsoft Sentinel, ServiceNow, and Okta. As Cyera grows, so do the company's product offerings. Cyera offers a DSPM product, Data Security Posture Management. It also offers products in the DLP space like Omni DLP, which is a DLP orchestration and enhancement product with AI, and an endpoint client, which is a replacement to the legacy endpoint DLP but also allows customers to secure AI on the endpoint like Claude, Copilot, and others. Cyera offers products in the identity space to protect how identities interact with data and which identities, human and non-human, exist in the IT ecosystem of the enterprise, as well as products in the agentic security space. As customers navigate this suite of offerings, the most exciting part for Cyera is watching users unlock their data in new ways. So, probably one of the first "aha" moments is logging into, like, just the data stores page and being able to see the hundreds, or in many cases, hundreds of thousands of data stores in your environment with kind of a granular breakdown of every type of data and every file that's contained within each one. Most CISOs and CTOs have never had that kind of visibility before, and so it's just kind of like, I won't say like a kid in a candy shop, but they want to just go and explore and see what's in there. But once companies open their data to autonomous AI agents, the risk to an enterprise's security grows. I think the most basic challenge enterprises are dealing with today with data security and AI is that we buried a lot of sins in the access people had to data that they never used. We all have access to millions of documents, countless data that we've never touched and we'll never touch as human beings. But agents are not like us. They move much faster, they read much faster, and they're able to go through endless quantities of data. Everything and anything they can put their hands on is going to rise to the surface. A lot of the security at present state is just based on the fact that humans never look for it. They never dig all the way through, they never rummage through every drawer and try to find the secrets, and the agents do. And that's a material change that everybody is experiencing. The beautiful and powerful thing about agents is that they have this independence and autonomy, and they can really do a lot more than a simple application. But that's also a potential risk that needs to be managed and curtailed. In that sense, you really need the automated visibility and control into how these agents are behaving and what they're doing. We also detect issues that are associated with your AI tools or your agents. An issue is kind of a violation, like a misconfiguration that might introduce risk. So in this case, we've identified that this agent is missing guardrails. Guardrails, you can think of them as controls around an agent to prevent it from doing something. So if someone asks a question about a topic that you don't want, you can block them from doing that. This agent touches healthcare data, sensitive data, and it doesn't have any guardrails in place, so what we might recommend is to put guardrails in place to prevent it from sending sensitive data to the wrong person. Cyera can also catch real-time threats from bad actors within a company. Over to the DLP alerts: alerts are when we are sitting in line, actually reading the prompts and responses and looking at what tool calls the agent is making, and we're actually able to determine, given kind of the context of the employee, the agent in use, and the types of data they're sending, does this make sense? Are they doing something malicious, or is this part of their day job? So an example of that here is Alice Smith, and she's using ChatGPT, and it looks like she might have done some insider trading solicitation. So we'll click into that and you get alert details. It tells you that an employee, Alice Smith, provided an embargoed Q2 earnings call transcript containing material non-public information to ChatGPT. And then she followed up and specifically solicited investment advice based on this embargoed earnings call script. The important thing is for leaders to be minded towards that and understand that, you know, you do not want to take a car and drive it at 200 miles per hour without an airbag and a seatbelt. You want good brakes, you want an airbag, you want a seatbelt, and that's what makes you comfortable to drive really fast. In June, the New York-based startup announced a $600 million Series G funding round at a $12 billion valuation, bringing its total funding to over $2 billion. Logan Bartlett, managing director at Redpoint Ventures, helped lead Cyera's Series B investment. I think when you meet the co-founders of the company, there's just such a charisma and energy in talking with them, and so I think that was readily apparent. It just sort of felt like these are the types of people that we wanted to be in business with. Then after that, it's about validating the product-market space and what the company is going after. When we sort of looked at the wedge that they had, which has been called DSPM as a space, it was clear that data was only going to become more important. The cloud was only going to grow in importance as a store for data, and then security was only going to be increasingly prevalent and something that was top of mind for businesses. This last fundraise is allowing us to accelerate the time to impact, to really invest and double down on our product and our platform, and to be able to continue to grow it at an extraordinary pace organically. It also gives us the opportunity to grow it with the right partners inorganically by making acquisitions—strategic acquisitions into spaces where we see our customers needing us to be and we're not there yet. And I think that the name of the game today is speed, and the reason we're choosing to take on this round and leverage it is because it accelerates us. It allows us to move even faster than we moved before and deliver the value that our customers need at the pace that their business is moving with AI, which is extremely fast. A key pillar of that aggressive growth has been acquiring smaller, specialized startups, including Trail Security, Drift, and Genie Security. As Cyera absorbs these teams, they thread their acquired technologies directly into the core platform. So far at Cyera, we've had immense success with very strategic and pointed acquisitions. We feel that bringing in a team that has that founder mentality and the ability to go and really strike a path in the market is incredibly valuable. It's not often that we meet a team that we really want to spend the next four years together with, but when we do get those opportunities, we act on them. When they did their first acquisition, I felt it was probably too early. Managing culture and fitting businesses together while you're growing as quickly as the company is, is a challenging thing to do, to be able to accomplish. But they showed that that acquisition was a screaming success for them. I think Cyera has established itself as a platform, as a strategic vendor of choice for some of the most important brands and enterprises in the world. So with that, when you kind of become a platform, you're now a trusted vendor to the CISO within that organization. And as a trusted vendor, you have an opportunity to sell a suite of products to them. You have distribution, you have relationships, and you're a choke point in the organization for this CISO who is ultimately looking to solve a multitude of problems. It gives you an opportunity to acquire these entrepreneurs, these entrepreneurial teams, these companies with early product-market fit, plug them into your broader organization, integrate the products, and sell additional solutions to these CISOs within these big enterprises. With a $12 billion valuation and an estimated 80 times ARR multiple, some critics have questioned Cyera's heavy cash burn. How does an investor like Bartlett balance that premium multiple against the company's hypergrowth over immediate profitability? The multiple, while high and feeling out of whack compared to what you might see from those other businesses, if you really adjust it for the growth, actually becomes very reasonable, if you believe that they're going to go accomplish what they say they're going to accomplish. The opportunity cost to not go fast and get into these accounts and establish yourself as a platform vendor that is number one, two, or three important to these CISOs, that to me is far more important than prioritizing profitability. With over 1,500 employees globally and a war chest of capital ready to deploy, there are no signs of Cyera slowing down in the near future. In two years, I hope Cyera becomes the standard for secure AI adoption across the enterprise. I hope that customers view us as their strategic partner for adopting AI and continuing to innovate with AI within their organization. I feel like safeguarding digital trust while we innovate and reinvent the rules of the game, the rules of the universe if you will, is not trivial. Trust is not guaranteed. It's something that can easily erode and deteriorate, and I hope that Cyera can play a meaningful role in really securing the trust of enterprises with their data and the trust of their customers with them.

DeepMind Chief Demis Hassabis Says Google’s Still Winning AI Talent | Semafor Tech

Semafor(28.9K)·29:05·46.7K views·2026-06-24
Demis HassabisCEO, Google DeepMind Reed AlbergottiTech Editor, Semafor
TL;DRDespite escalating industry competition and high-profile departures, Google DeepMind CEO Demis Hassabis remains confident in Google's leading research bench and its multi-pronged, simulation-based path toward Artificial General Intelligence (AGI).
). Industry Talent...

TL;DR

Despite escalating industry competition and high-profile departures, Google DeepMind CEO Demis Hassabis remains confident in Google's leading research bench and its multi-pronged, simulation-based path toward Artificial General Intelligence (AGI).

The AI Talent War and the Path to AGI

  • Industry Talent Dynamics: While rival labs are aggressively poaching top minds, Hassabis emphasizes that talent movement between leading entities is normal in a "ferociously competitive" market.
      • Google DeepMind still maintains the largest and deepest research bench in the world.
      • Historically, over 90% of the foundational breakthroughs powering the modern AI industry—including Transformers and reinforcement learning systems like AlphaGo—originated from Google Brain or DeepMind.
  • A Multi-Pronged AGI Strategy: Google is not relying solely on scaling text-based models to achieve AGI.
      • The company is betting on a diverse ecosystem of models, including Gemini (multimodal foundation), Omni, and Veo (generative media).
      • True AGI requires an understanding of the physical world of "atoms," which is essential for advanced robotics and smart-glass assistants.

AI's Impact on Creativity and the Creative Industries

  • Evolution of Creative Tools: Creative tools have evolved from simple generation to highly granular, iterative editing.
      • Live Editing: Users of Google's media models (like Imagen and Omni) can now edit specific sections of an output using natural language commands rather than regenerating an entire piece from scratch.
  • Democratization vs. Professional Leverage: AI lowers the barrier to entry, allowing new creators to break into industries with fewer gatekeepers.
      • For professionals, these tools allow creators to iterate "10x faster," enabling them to test complex, expensive ideas cheaply.
      • Hassabis warns that "lazy" usage of AI dilutes the creative process, but innovative usage elevates it.
  • The Need for New Economic Models: Just as Spotify and YouTube's Content ID created new financial models for the music industry, the tech and creative sectors must develop fresh business models to manage IP attribution and payouts in the AI era.

Deepfakes, Safety, and Global Governance

  • Standardizing Provenance with SynthID: To combat misinformation and deepfakes, Google DeepMind developed SynthID, an imperceptible, unhackable digital watermarking tool.
      • SynthID is embedded into all Google-generated music, images, and videos.
      • Google has open-sourced the technology, and major competitors like OpenAI and NVIDIA have adopted it. Hassabis advocates for provenance watermark detection to become a regulatory requirement.
  • Escalating Global Risks: As the world approaches "the foothills of the singularity," the threat landscape will expand beyond cybersecurity.
      • Hassabis warns of biological, nuclear, and systemic risks arising within the next two years.
      • He calls for an international standards body to evaluate and test the guardrails of frontier AI systems before they are deployed.

The Neuroscience of AGI: Memory, Imagination, and Simulation

  • Reconstructing Reality: Hassabis's landmark 2007 neuroscience research established that memory is not a recording, but a reconstructive process sharing the same brain mechanisms as imagination.
      • AI video models (like Veo) mirror this by reconstructing 3D physical worlds based on prompts, representing a system-level parallel to human cognitive processing.
  • The Einstein Test: To prove an AI is genuinely creative, it must pass the "Einstein Test"—formulating a completely novel, mathematically sound hypothesis about physical reality (such as the theory of relativity) from a set of limited, historical data.
  • Simulations as the Ultimate Goal: The core value of AGI lies in its ability to run thousands of complex simulations to find optimal pathways.
      • This methodology powered AlphaGo and is currently driving breakthroughs in scientific fields like drug discovery (via Isomorphic Labs) and protein folding (AlphaFold).
      • Hassabis envisions using learned simulations to model highly complex, historically unquantifiable systems like global economics and weather patterns.

Notable Quotes

"We have by far the biggest and broadest research bench of any of the labs out there... and we win our fair share of the top talent." — Demis Hassabis
"I've said sort of statements like we're in the foothills of the singularity... cyber's just a warning shot for humanity, and I hope we take it seriously. There'll be bio, nuclear, and other kinds of risks coming down the line maybe in the next couple of years." — Demis Hassabis
"If you use it in a lazy way, it kind of takes away from the creative process. But if you use it in an innovative way, it should add to the creative process. It's going to take a while for the creative industries to figure out the best way to use these things." — Demis Hassabis
It's great to see you. It seems like we always talk where it's very cold or very hot. Today it's very hot, so let's see if we can get through this without sweating. Demis, everyone right now is freaking out about AI. They're banning AI models in D.C. Well, there's a lot to unpack just in that first question. I mean, first of all, with the things that we're seeing with cyber and Mythos and... I've been pretty vocal for a long while that as we get closer to AGI, and I think we're on the cusp of that now—I've sort of said statements like we're in the foothills of the singularity—that we need a bit more systematic approach. Of course, there's amazing opportunities ahead. A lot of the things you talked about in the introduction: solving all disease, finding new energy sources. These are the reasons that I've worked on AI my whole career. But there are also risks. And cyber's one, but I think actually there are going to be even more serious things. That's just a kind of warning shot for humanity, and I hope we take it seriously. But there'll be bio, nuclear, other kinds of risks coming down the line, maybe in the next couple of years. And we've got to get ready for that. From Transformers that underpins all large language models to AlphaGo and all the reinforcement learning pioneering that we did back back back then. And you can see that with first first sort of five, six, seven years of DeepMind, we were working on games and AI being good at games, like like Go and and Atari games. Yeah, so the as you mentioned, the the through line between that was so I first of all used my own visual creativity to help design and program these video games very early in my career. And I I and then when I went into neuroscience to do my PhD, I was fascinated to try and uncover the mechanisms that in our brain that allows us to do that, that all of us use all the time. It's like at least the way I used to create for games was visualize the end goal, visualize really viscerally visualize a player, a kid playing the game and using the interface and thinking through, even before it was it was there programmed, like what would be the issues with it and how would they find it fun and these types of things. So we're using this sort of imaginative or future thinking it's sometimes called capability in the brain, all the time. And I had this suspicion when I first started my PhD, nominally I was supposed to be doing it on memory. But but and and memory had been studied for a long time and it's memory's dependent on the hippocampus. And there are these rare patients that have had a a disease that only just attacks the hippocampus, unfortunately for them but leaves the rest of the brain intact. And there's a few of them in the UK. We went to interview every single one of them. And I had this idea that reading all the memory literature which is what I did the sort of first month of my PhD, was that there were two schools of thought. One is that it's a videotape and you just record everything that happens to you. And another which obviously seemed wrong to me. And there was another school of thought which is that it's a reconstructive process, that actually memory when you remember something, you're actively reconstructing it from its parts. And that seemed much more obviously correct to me. But if that was true, then imagination should use the same brain mechanisms. So it's just the goals different. Instead of trying to recreate something that seems familiar to you, this time you're trying to create something from those component parts that looks novel, that feels novel to to your brain. And in in fact that's what we discovered. And we were the first people to, amazingly, to test any of the patients on their imaginative capabilities rather than just their memory. Do you see any similarities between what's happening under the hood with these video models, recreating the world from prompts and and and what's happening in the in the brain? I think definitely on a systems level. I don't know I don't think the implementations are similar one-to-one, and that was never the idea behind doing neuroscience. It wasn't to copy the brain, but it was to understand the principles and the algorithms the brain might be using and the representations the brain was using and and sort of lift that inspiration to then try and build that and into the direction of our AI models. So I think there are some for sure some similarities between the way our new Omni models and Veo models are generating the world, and there's definitely some things to be learned about I think how that system is working. And quite a few neuroscience professor friends of mine are actually indeed comparing the latest models about what they can do with a certain prompt with what a human might do in an fMRI machine and what images they might create. And they're doing all sorts of crazy amazing things like decoding what image the person's thinking about or dreaming about and then using one of these models to recreate actually the visuals and then asking the the subject in the scanner: 'Is that what you were imagining?' And it is. So it's kind of we're going to have these sort of amazing kind of sci-fi devices I think in the next few years. So interesting. Do you you've talked about the Einstein test. I love this idea that you could sort of give an AI all the data that Einstein had and and nothing more. In other words, like the cutoff date is whatever, January 1901. And and and then see if the AI model could do what Einstein did, just come up with the theory of relativity and and all these other, you know, physics breakthroughs that we use today. So when you hear that though, you could almost imagine like text models, but are you are you really kind of imagining a more visual process? Well, I think you first of all that's how I define true creativity. And that was my test for that is because people always ask, 'Well, how do you define it?' where you're not just extrapolating something that already is known, but you're actually coming up with a new hypothesis, a new scientific hypothesis about some part of reality that is genuinely novel, like Einstein most famously did in 1905 with his incredible set of experiments and papers. So it's interesting. You it could be that there's enough in language that that that's enough to come up with some new theory that was sort of hidden cross-connected with all of the text if you could read it all and hold it all in mind. But Einstein himself used to daydream while he was a patent clerk in Switzerland, and this is well-documented, and dream of these sort of thought experiments of, you know, being on trains and then if you were speed of travel- traveling at speed of light what would it look like and... So he was doing he was using his visual imaginative apparatus to come up with these new theories, and then he had to prove mathematically. But I think you're going to need to access and at least understand, and certainly if you're going to propose new experiments or have to do new experiments in order to test your hypothesis to develop it further so it's not just the- a theory, then I think you're going to need to have an understanding of the world of atoms, not just the world of bits or the world of logic. Yeah. You back in, you know, in your video game days, you you had this game, 'Republic: The Revolution' at Elixir, which is the company you founded. Game was actually kind of a failure, but it was this idea, it was it was too ambitious. You were trying to recreate this former Soviet republic and simulate the world in a sense, right? On a Pentium in 2003, it was probably a little bit too ambitious. Ten years ahead of my time, yeah. Right, right. Now now you have access to, you know, I tens hundreds of thousands of of GPUs at your fingertips and you're building these virtual worlds again. You're simu- you know, it's funny how it kind of comes full circle. But like do you imagine that this is where inside these virtual worlds that like, obviously they're going to be useful for robotics, right? Like training these things to to go upstairs or whatever. But like, you know, Einstein in this virtual world, a virtual Einstein wandering around at the patent office and daydreaming. Like is that where it's going to happen? Well, the reason is look, with with Republic we tried to simulate a whole country with 100,000 living, breathing people sort of going about their daily lives and all the politics of it and you were supposed to like create a revolution. So it was a pretty ambitious game that we had to write by hand. And what's amazing now and it took years, and but what's amazing now is that we might be close to the possibility of actually just sort of generating that with some of our systems. I don't think we're ready yet, but in two, three years' time that might be something close to that might be possible. And then we could realize maybe the full vision of what I was thinking about then. But the reason this is all important and why there's a why it's all connected is simulations and AI I think are fundamental and they're very closely related. So simulations are going to be useful basically that's what imagination is, it's a type of simulation. The reason simulations are useful is it allows you to try out many things in theory and then select the best path. Right, that's what AlphaGo did when it tries to select the best Go move in the current position. It simulates tens of thousands of moves using Monte Carlo tree search in this case, uses the model of Go to to kind of constrain that to only the useful paths, and then it evaluates at the end of those 20, 30 moves, which one of those end positions is the most promising. And then that's what guides its next move and allows it to beat the world champion. But there are many areas of the world, robotics and and assistants are just two things and science even, but there are many areas of the world where we would love to in my opinion kind of be able to have many reruns of that problem. I'll give you an example: economics. Right, so economics is, you know, it would be great if it was more like a natural science. So at the moment we we like put up and down interest rates by half a percent and then we see, did it cause a recession? Oh, oops, we should maybe we shouldn't have done that. But it would be much better if we could simulate hundreds of thousands of trajectories from here, the economy, what it would do if you adjusted these big levers and then get some kind of statistical aggreg- aggregate of all of those accurate simulations and then make an informed, much more rigorous scientific decision about what to do. But that's not possible in a lot of the social sciences because you can't rerun the experiment in a controlled way hundreds of times like you can with the natural sciences. So I think simulations which are learned and and then where AI is connected is you can you can hand-code a simulation if you understand the underlying system well enough, but most of the time the things we want to simulate, whether they're it's like weather models like we have the the world's best weather models, or it's economics models, we don't actually understand well enough how the system really works on a mathematical level. So then an AI system could learn it, that that simulation from the data. So that's sort of the bigger goal if you like of what I'm trying to do. I love how you've tied the creativity part of AI with the science, and I'm sure you've given this audience like a lot of inspiration in their in their work. So thank you so much, Demis. Great to talk with you. Thank you. Great to talk. Appreciate it.

Reid Hoffman on what's real and what's hype in AI | Pioneers of AI

Pioneers of AI(31.3K)·36:12·134 views·2026-06-24
Reid HoffmanAI Investor & Co-founder, LinkedIn Rana el KalioubyHost, Pioneers of AI
TL;DRRenowned tech investor Reid Hoffman argues that the AI landscape is not a winner-take-all "cage match," but a multi-trillion-dollar utility shift where both OpenAI and Anthropic will thrive.
To survive this shift, early-stage startups must move past "thin wrappers" to...

TL;DR

Renowned tech investor Reid Hoffman argues that the AI landscape is not a winner-take-all "cage match," but a multi-trillion-dollar utility shift where both OpenAI and Anthropic will thrive. To survive this shift, early-stage startups must move past "thin wrappers" to build AI-native moats, while individuals must adopt an "agency mindset" to leverage AI as a collaborative partner.

Hoffman’s Transition to "Founder Mode" and Manas AI

  • Stepping Down from Microsoft: Reid Hoffman (Co-founder of LinkedIn and longtime tech investor) announced he will not stand for re-election to the Microsoft board, concluding a decade of service to return to "founder mode."
      • Legacy Achievements: Hoffman highlights his work facilitating the LinkedIn acquisition, the strategic purchase of GitHub, and building early trust between Microsoft and OpenAI.
  • Manas AI Launch: Hoffman is shifting his primary focus to Manas AI, a computational chemistry and AI drug discovery startup he co-founded with partners Sid and Ujwal.
      • The Monopolistic Moat of Biotech: Hoffman notes that Manas AI already has computational chemists validating highly promising small-molecule proposals. Because drug discovery relies on patented IP, the startup's pitch deck explicitly aims to construct "monopolies" around newly discovered biological molecules.

The Trillion-Dollar Valuation and IPO Landscape

  • OpenAI vs. Anthropic Co-existence: Rather than a "cage match" where only one model provider survives, Hoffman predicts both OpenAI and Anthropic will become massive infrastructure providers.
      • Scale of Electricity: Hoffman compares the scale and impact of AI to applying intelligence at the cost and scale of electricity across all human endeavors.
      • Market Segmentation: Both companies are carving out distinct strengths—Anthropic is excelling in code, design, and legal applications, while OpenAI’s ChatGPT acts as a massive consumer front-end search equivalent alongside its highly capable Codex product.
  • Valuation Realities: Hoffman compares current AI valuations to early internet-era pricing. Some will inevitably collapse to zero, while others are priced far too low compared to their future terminal value.
      • The Amazon Parallel: Just as Amazon operated unprofitably for years to secure strategic dominance, leading AI companies are valued on expected future terminal value rather than immediate profitability.
  • Analyzing SpaceX's AI Narrative: SpaceX’s June 12th IPO was heavily driven by an AI narrative, culminating in its acquisition of the coding assistant Cursor.
      • Buying Relevance: Hoffman asserts that SpaceX is not natively an AI company. Instead, it is utilizing its high market cap to buy AI assets because Elon Musk's other venture, XAI, has struggled with founder departures and multiple restarts.

Shifting Defensibility and Moats for Startups

  • The "SaaS-pocalypse" Myth: The belief that software-as-a-service (SaaS) is dead is overstated. However, traditional moats built on the high cost of software development are shrinking.
      • AI-Native Imperative: Hoffman advises investors to short any SaaS company that is not aggressively transitioning to become AI-native, and to back those that are.
  • Avoiding the "Thin Wrapper" Trap: Startups that merely package foundation models into a vertical interface are highly vulnerable to first-party features from major model providers.
      • True Defensibility: Sustainable value lies in proprietary data integration, highly specialized physical AI, and world models that operate outside the standard web-scraped datasets used by OpenAI and Anthropic.

Geopolitics, AI Regulation, and the Human Element

  • Sovereign Wealth Funds: Hoffman supports the concept of a US sovereign wealth fund for AI to capture economic growth and address workforce disruption. However, he warns against hostile appropriation of private assets, favoring market-rate state investments instead.
  • Predictable AI Safety: Commenting on Anthropic being forced to pull its Fable and Mythos models over US security concerns, Hoffman cautions against unpredictable, "willy-nilly" regulatory actions. He advocates for a consistent, principled rule of law focused strictly on major threats like cybersecurity and bioterrorism.
  • High Tech, High Touch: As AI automation scales, human-centric experiences (e.g., hospitality, live entertainment, and sports) will command a massive premium. Hoffman believes the demand for authentic human connection will rise in tandem with technological progress.

Notable Quotes

"If you believe that AI has the impact of applying intelligence with the scale and price of electricity across everything, these [OpenAI and Anthropic] are going to be two of the major providers of that." — Reid Hoffman
"If you're like, 'all the valuations are crazy,' you're wrong. If you said, 'some of the valuations are crazy,' you're right. And then the trick is, which ones?" — Reid Hoffman
"You guys have the opportunity to be 'generation AI' where you come into the workforce saying, 'I know this a lot better than all of you, you should be hiring me in order to help you become AI-native organizations.' It should be an opportunity, not a threat." — Reid Hoffman (On advice to the younger generation)

Part of what has made capitalism so essential and so successful to humanity, to all societies, has been that the allocation of resource decisions tends to go to the things that drive, you know, quality up, price down, etc. And so, the answer of like how does work get allocated is like, well, where does the work done get done at a certain amount of quality at a low price? Follow the Money: Where Climate Capital Is Going

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·65:45·349 views·2026-06-24
Catherine McKennaChair, UN High-Level Expert Group on Net-Zero Steven van RijswijkCEO, ING Group Jesse JenkinsAssistant Professor, Princeton University
TL;DRGlobal green investment has surpassed $5 trillion annually, transitioning climate capital from a regulatory burden into a highly profitable, security-driven strategic advantage.
However, unlocking its full potential requires overcoming severe grid integration limits,...

TL;DR

Global green investment has surpassed $5 trillion annually, transitioning climate capital from a regulatory burden into a highly profitable, security-driven strategic advantage. However, unlocking its full potential requires overcoming severe grid integration limits, reducing trade protectionism, and de-risking investments in hard-to-abate non-electricity sectors.

The Shift from Regulatory Burden to Economic Advantage

  • Dynamic Market Growth: Global green investment now exceeds $5 trillion annually, making it the second most dynamic growth sector behind technology. Green revenues are currently expanding at twice the pace of traditional sectors.
  • Decoupling Transition from Net Cost:
      • 90% Solar Cost Reduction: Over the last 15 years, technological innovation—primarily driven by Chinese manufacturing scale—has reduced solar power generation costs by 90%. Solar has now become the cheapest source of new electricity in most global markets.
      • National Economic Savings: Renewable energy transition is increasingly driven by national economic and security interests rather than climate mandates alone. Zhong Baoshen (Chairman of LONGi Green Energy Technology) noted that in 2025, China's domestic clean energy deployment saved the nation $110 billion by reducing dependence on foreign oil and gas imports.
  • The GCC Clean Energy Premium:
      • Record-Low Tariffs: In the Gulf Cooperation Council (GCC) region, solar tariffs have reached historic lows of 1.24 to 1.6 cents per kilowatt-hour.
      • Targeted Private Capital: While massive GCC sovereign wealth funds (like PIF, Mubadala, and ADIA) focus on megaprojects exceeding $1 billion, private family offices and corporations are successfully targeting highly profitable mid-scale green infrastructure projects in the $50 million to $500 million range.

Overcoming Infrastructure Bottlenecks and Decarbonizing Molecules

  • The Intermittency and Grid Bottleneck: Clean energy has successfully crossed 15% to 20% of total electricity generation in major economic blocs. However, crossing this threshold introduces severe grid-management challenges.
      • The Storage Mandate: To ensure grid stability, the future of green power relies heavily on integrated "solar + storage" and "wind + storage" systems.
      • Falling Storage Costs: Battery and energy storage technology costs are dropping rapidly, enabling local microgrids to power entire industrial, mining, and residential zones with 100% renewable baseload electricity.
  • The Decarbonization Divide (Electricity vs. Molecules):
      • Mature Electricity Markets: Power generation solutions are mature and highly bankable. For example, Sumant Sinha (Chair and CEO of ReNew India) highlighted that India scaled its renewable installations to 50 GW last year without facing financing constraints because projects offered robust, risk-managed returns.
      • The 75% Non-Electricity Gap: 75% of global energy consumption occurs outside the electricity grid (e.g., heavy transport, shipping, and industrial manufacturing). Capital must pivot toward creating market demand for green molecules, such as green hydrogen and green ammonia, through smart subsidies and risk-mitigation structures.
      • Industrial Heat Opportunities: Industrial steam and heat generation account for 42% of total energy consumption in Saudi Arabia. Lamar Holdings has integrated renewable technologies into industrial heat and water desalination projects, successfully increasing public-private partnership (PPP) project returns by 40% to 45%.

Geopolitical Shocks, Energy Security, and the Cost of Protectionism

  • Clean Energy as National Security: Recent geopolitical conflicts—including wars in Ukraine and Gaza, and supply chain threats in the Strait of Hormuz (which handles 20% of global energy transit)—have forced nations to prioritize energy security. Governments increasingly recognize that energy independence can only be secured via domestic renewable energy.
  • The Extreme Cost of Trade Protectionism:
      • The $6 Trillion Penalty: Special Envoy Liu Zhenmin cited a McKinsey study warning that if Western economies persist in decoupling from Chinese supply chains, the global transition cost will surge by 20%—equivalent to a $6 trillion penalty.
      • Tariff-Driven Inflation: Recent trade barriers on clean tech components have doubled procurement prices in certain markets, resulting in final project costs for users that are 20% to 30% higher than they were a few years ago.

Closing the Financing Gap in Emerging Markets

  • Undelivered Multilateral Pledges: Under the UNFCCC, developed nations established a New Collective Quantified Goal to provide $300 billion annually to developing nations. However, these commitments remain largely unfulfilled.
  • The Africa Risk Premium: Over 600 million people in Africa lack access to electricity. Due to institutional instability, currency devaluation, and a lack of predictable legal frameworks, solar costs in Africa average 18 to 20 cents per kilowatt-hour, compared to under 2 cents in the Middle East.
  • G2G De-risking Mechanisms: To bridge this capital gap, GCC governments are employing Government-to-Government (G2G) diplomatic frameworks to secure private sector investments in Africa. Operating under these sovereign umbrellas mitigates currency and political risk, successfully driving African solar costs down to 3 to 5 cents per kilowatt-hour.

Notable Quotes

"If you continue to insist on your decoupling from Chinese products, globally we should run the risk of a 20% increase of global transition cost. 20% increase means around 6 trillion US dollars... The humanity, we cannot wait." — Liu Zhenmin, Special Envoy for Climate Change of the People's Republic of China
"To expect the Western world in this current context to be transferring huge amounts of money as grant money to developing countries—I don't think it's going to happen, frankly. We can keep asking for it, but it's not going to show up... The reality is that that’s not where this battle against climate is going to be fought. It’s going to be fought by large amounts of capital coming in for projects that make economic sense." — Sumant Sinha, Chair and CEO of ReNew India
"In our region... we have solar more than anybody in the world, and we need to use it. The solar tariff is around 1.24 to 1.6 [cents], which is the lowest in the world... Introducing renewables in water desalination and infrastructure increases the return on the project by 40% to 45%." — Lina Noureddin, President and Founder of Lamar Holdings
"Initially, we believed that this is a cost we have to pay for the renewable energy transition... But with time passing by, we find that no one talks about it anymore. People don't believe that the energy transition is a pure cost, or a liability we have to bear as a burden. Actually, now we find renewable energy is the cheapest among all." — Zhong Baoshen, Chairman of LONGi Green Energy Technology
Good afternoon, everyone. My name is Guan Xin, and I'm the anchor and chief business news editor of CGTN. A very warm welcome to all of you to join this panel: "Follow the money, where climate capital is going." Despite headlines often suggesting that the green transition is kind of losing steam, numbers actually tell a very different story. According to the WEF's annual report on growth in the green economy, global green investment exceeded five trillion US dollars annually, making it one of the world's most dynamic growth sectors, second only to technology. Green revenues are growing at twice the pace of traditional sectors. And I think there is a deeper shift happening here. Investing in a green economy is no longer seen as an obligation or a burden to carry for businesses; it has actually become a strategic advantage, a genuine source of growth and jobs, especially in the era of rising energy demand and concerns for energy security brought by the rapid development of AI and geopolitical tensions. So here is a critical question: is the capital flowing to the right place with the right scale and expected impact? We have an exceptional panel to unpack this. Allow me to introduce them. So, from government priorities to business decisions, let me turn to Mr. Sinha. From your experience leading one of the largest renewable platforms in India, which is not only a massive market but also a critical testbed to scale up the green economy in a large emerging economy, in your opinion, what would it actually take to scale up green energy and other sectors? Yeah, thank you. Thank you, Guan. It's great to be here and to share the dais here with my fellow colleagues and panelists. You know, I think there are many things that need to be done. First of all, let's acknowledge the fact that clean energy in general, or the energy transition in general, has progressed quite substantially over the last decade and a half. I think at COP28 in Dubai, there was a pretty fundamental decision taken to start phasing away from fossil fuels. And I think that was the first time that globally there was an acknowledgment that fossil fuels were, in the long run, not something that the world should be moving towards. The alternative, therefore, really was the clean energy area. And within clean energy, if you look at the progress that we've made, it's been again quite substantial. We've now got to a point where clean energy in most geographies, in most large countries and economic blocks in the world, has at least crossed 15 to 20 percent of total electricity generation. The cost of new energy from renewable energy sources has now become the cheapest when you compare just pure generation to any fossil fuel alternative in most geographies as well. And there is a pretty developed, I would say, ecosystem now of the supply chain, manufacturing, installation, etc., that is now already existing, and trained people as well. So we've made a lot of progress. But now we're coming up against some fundamental constraints. Those constraints are, number one, grid management has become pretty hard because, you know, crossing this 15 to 20 percent with intermittent renewables is no longer that straightforward. So we need to start looking at the whole systemic sort of solution and looking at how renewables can start giving baseload power. Storage is becoming incredibly important. Grid build-out has to happen much faster. At the same time, the easily available land in most geographies is getting exhausted, so we now have to start thinking a little bit more innovatively about land, access to land, and so on. And the scale of what is required now has suddenly increased tremendously. Therefore, all of this needs to now get scaled up at many X's, many multiples of what we've done so far. All of that, therefore, requires a very different approach now going forward. I think that is where we're sort of at this inflection point now: we've got to a certain point, and now we need to take it to a whole different level if we have to phase out fossil fuels. I would say the other good thing that has happened is that because of the Middle Eastern war, there is a very strong, I think, expectation and realization that now energy security can only be met—not only, of course, but very substantially met—through clean energy which is generated within your own borders. So that realization is now driving governments to really move forward on this energy transition much more substantially. This is really the point in time where we are. Now going forward, there are two or three things that need to happen. One is that government policymaking has to really, really become, I think, a lot more enlightened to drive this inflection point and pass that into the multiple-X installations that we require. The second thing is the corporate sector needs to scale up. The third thing is we need to diversify supply chains to some extent, because obviously everybody realizes that dependence is not great. So we need to figure out how to do that. This is really where we are. Now, in this whole equation, because the topic is on climate financing, financing obviously has an incredibly important role to play. My experience has been that capital will flow to areas where they can get good returns. So it's really incumbent upon the corporate sector, working with governments, to make sure that there are appropriate returns that can be provided in projects that are now being done in any geography—whether it's the US, whether it's China, whether it's India, or whether it's Africa—because capital will not flow to places where you don't get proper returns. So if we want that capital to flow to all of these areas, and there is sufficient liquidity available, then we need to ensure that we have investable projects, appropriately risk-managed and designed, which can then attract this capital. And I think the experience that we have in India has been that we've scaled up our clean energy installations from two or three years ago, from 18 to 20 gigawatts to 30 gigawatts, and last year we did 50 gigawatts. There has been no problem financing any of that. Now, as we look forward, you know, we'll do 60, 70 gigawatts a year, and we'll have newer areas like the bio sector coming into play as well. We'll have some of the downstream industries, whether it's mobility and so on, that will also start decarbonizing. So capital is easily available, but it just needs to give the right kind of returns through appropriately structured projects. I think that is going to be very important. But there is sufficient liquidity to fund a lot of this transition that needs to happen. Indeed, it's great to see that private capital is now playing a bigger role in the green transition in addition to governments. Let me turn to Ms. Noureddin. The private sector is also very important in this aspect because they have the execution capability. The sovereign fund has the wealth and the money, but at the end of the day, as Mr. Sinha said, the execution is on the ground. The execution on the ground is very important. Private companies have this, and they can make it happen. In our region, it is a very high temperature; we are maybe the highest temperature in the world. We are in summer, and we can feel it. What that means is we have more solar than anybody in the world, and we need to use it. What we have now is a solar tariff of around 1.24 to 1.6, which is the lowest in the world. That means we can use solar more, and it means, as my colleagues say, we have to look at renewable energy that can at least subsidize or replace a bit of the fossil fuel that we are using. In Lamar, we can say that in all our projects we are working on, we are introducing renewables into them. Because with renewables, you are not taking from fossil fuels; you are not using gas, even in water. Water is a very important subject in our region. It is the most scarce resource in the Middle East; we are the most water-scarce. So, in water desalination and all of these water facilities, you can see that we are using renewables in our projects, which increases the return on the project by 40%. Yes, we are looking at different proposals for how we can introduce more and more renewables, especially in the GCC, where we have the framework, the legal framework, the land allocation, the willingness of our governments, and their support. So we are more eager to go into that as private offices to increase our return. Thank you, Ms. Noureddin. And talking about making use of solar energy, I have to turn to Mr. Zhong. You lead LONGi, one of the world's largest solar manufacturers, and we know LONGi has been at the forefront of driving down the cost of solar energy and making it now the most affordable source of electricity in the world in many markets. So my question to you is: how do we continue this success across many new and emerging technologies, and if you were to invest in the future, which sector do you think we should be betting on? Thank you very much. Thank you for your question. Yes, for sustainable and renewable energy, it is closely related to technology innovation. We all know that sustainable and renewable energy development started from our exploration of how to deal with climate change. At that time, we believed that this was a cost we had to pay for the renewable energy transition. I remember that at the climate conference in Paris, I was in this meeting hall, and we had a discussion on who will bear this cost of the renewable energy transition. The consensus reached was that developed countries would need to pay 100 billion every year to support developing countries on the energy transition and to deal with climate change. But as time has passed, we find that no one talks about it anymore. People don't believe that the energy transition is a pure cost, some liability we have to bear as a burden. Actually, now we find that the cost of renewable energy is getting lower and lower. For example, as our two panelists have mentioned, in many places in the world, solar energy has become the cheapest energy of all. So it is not that we have to pay a cost for renewable energy; we can do it for local economic development and for energy independence and security. Because we know that solar power is more distributed, and wind power as well is more distributed compared with oil and gas, it can be inclusive. It can support the energy demand in many places in the world and help with strategic energy independence. Looking at the resilience, cost-effectiveness, and security of energy, developing renewable energy is good for the local economy. This has become a consensus. And how to achieve this? If you look at the past 15 years, I have been working in the solar industry, and we have seen that the power generation cost has decreased by 90 percent with the help of technological progress. We should say that Chinese companies have made great achievements and contributions to renewable energy, for PV, and for wind. We can also see that in the past several decades, Chinese companies' contribution has been a large part of all these achievements. These innovations and technology progress have made inclusive renewable energy possible. In recent years, we have seen that the voice on dealing with climate change is getting weaker. We are facing other challenges like geopolitical issues, trade conflicts, and the attention of society has been shifted to the security of energy supply, the safety of the whole society, trade, and economic progress. On dealing with climate change, the voice is getting weaker, but we still have to see that renewable energy is still developing. We are now facing some new issues, like connecting to the grid and the intermittent nature of this energy. This character is a bottleneck for the further expansion of solar energy. But we can also see some other wonderful solutions to help us with renewable energy development; in recent years, we have seen the cost of energy storage decrease significantly, and it has made it possible for us to build a real renewable energy system. We can have the integration of wind, solar, and storage, and we can use renewable energy to support the power supply system. We have also seen a lot of demonstration systems, and these demonstration projects have been carried out in a lot of major projects like mining zones and communities, showing they can be supported with 100 percent renewable energy. In the future, we need more innovations of this kind. In LONGi, we are now looking at the integration of solar and storage to further expand the borders of renewable energy. We will continue to reduce the cost of solar power generation, introducing it to more scenarios to be used in buildings, in transportation, and to support people's lives while reducing power generation costs. At the same time, we will need to integrate power generation with power storage to make its usage more flexible, and to make renewable energy more economical, convenient, and reliable. We need to remove the limitations of using green energy—meaning in all different scenarios, we can use renewable energy to support our work, our lives, and other demands. In this case, we need other technologies like the prediction of power generation, peak shaving, the management of energy distribution, and also scheduling, etc. All of this needs innovation. The good news is that in all these fields, globally, we have seen a lot of technology progress. Now, the large-scale deployment of clean energy to make it a driver or a major supplier to the grid has become possible. The technology is getting more and more mature, so we believe that clean energy will bring more opportunities to the world economy through technology innovation, helping with energy independence and giving inclusive benefits to the world. Thank you, LONGi, for very valuable lessons from your work. I really feel that capital is important, but it's the businesses, the entrepreneurs, and the scientists really doing the hard job of making cost reduction in renewables a reality nowadays. We're here to talk about scaling up innovation, innovating at scale, at this annual meeting. Mr. Liu, let me turn to you because in the world there are so many competing priorities. There are concerns about energy security and concerns about economic development. With so many things, national interests sometimes seem to be diverging. How much room do you see for genuine international cooperation going forward, especially when it comes to innovation in green development and the public good? Thank you. I think, first, let me add my confirmation that I fully agree with what my fellow panelists have shared. Let me add a few things. First, I think the urgency for responding to climate change is increasing, because we have been alerted by the World Meteorological Organization and the IPCC that the increase of the global temperature is very fast—fast. If humanity does not accelerate action in the forthcoming five or ten years, then we are running the risk that we will not achieve the goals set by the Paris Agreement. So we must enhance and accelerate our national actions. Second, I think we can see from the experience of many countries that the evolution, innovation, and development of new technology, especially solar and wind technology, is at a stage where they are affordable to all countries. The prices are becoming cheaper than even the use of fossil fuels. That is why, as Mr. Zhong has mentioned, the share of solar energy is equal for all countries. If all countries could really benefit from their share of solar and wind energy and start to develop that energy, then they will definitely, first, change the entire energy scenario of every country—even in the very remote areas—for access to energy. Because so far, we see that even in some regions, especially in Africa and some parts of Asia, still around 600 million people do not have electricity. That's why we can achieve both benefits: for humanity and for the climate. Third, I think climate financing remains a big issue. Even among the developing countries, not all countries can afford their own transition. However, so far this amount of money has not been delivered yet. The agreement took place, but so far nobody has delivered that amount. A 20% increase means around 6 trillion US dollars—6 trillion US dollars! You know, 6 trillion US dollars is a big amount, so that is why we, humanity, cannot wait. Indeed, Mr. Liu, the cost of inaction is huge, and we have seen that COP30 has been framed as the implementation COP. We really need to close the gap between pledge and delivery. Mr. Sinha, I want to have your take on this because India has also emphasized the longstanding obligation for developed countries to provide climate finance for developing countries, and it has expressed satisfaction with the establishment of the just transition mechanism. How do you think we can strengthen the implementation of these international negotiations and mobilize more private capital to invest in this transition? Yeah, look, I think that, you know, Mr. Zhong was right when he talked about the 100 billion dollars number in Paris, which now seems really tiny in terms of what is actually required, but even that never showed up. To expect the Western world in this current context to be transferring huge amounts of money as grant money or whatever to developing countries—I don't think it's going to happen, frankly. We can keep asking for it, but it's not going to show up. Of course, that doesn't mean we shouldn't ask for it, because obviously we may get something, and something is better than nothing. But the reality is that that's not where this battle against climate is going to be fought. It's going to be fought by large amounts of capital, as I said earlier, coming in for projects that make economic sense. The really good news for climate today is that—let's break down the solutions that we require into two broad buckets: one is electricity, and one is the rest of energy. In electricity, we all know what the solution is; it just has to be implemented at scale. Because of the efforts of Mr. Zhong in LONGi, all the developments that have happened in China, and all the work that is going on in India, companies like ours at ReNew are able to find baseload solutions by combining wind, solar, and storage together. We're able to give these baseload solutions to the grid at prices which are cheaper than coal-based power. So, to me, the issue is now one of implementation in the electricity sector. The solutions are well known, they are mature, and they just have to be implemented at scale. For anybody asking for money to solve that problem any longer, I don't think it makes a lot of sense. In Africa, for example, which is where—as Mr. Liu was saying—600 million people don't have access to affordable electricity, they need to put their grid together. They need to have a single grid in Africa, first of all. They need to build long transmission lines across the whole continent and create a single market, much like there is in the European Union. Which, by the way, isn't the case so much in the US yet, because the US still has seven different, almost isolated zones. But really, the more we combine together, the easier it will be. Policymakers have to really get up to speed on building out grids, figuring out what are the right incentive structures for storage, and then just creating the right returns for capital providers to come in and do the execution that needs to be done. So, on electricity, the solutions are well known and a lot of countries are implementing them. India has done quite a bit; we're fairly far advanced on electrifying our grid system, building out the grid as much as we need to, and now implementing huge amounts of storage as well. The models are already there. And it's not like India's per capita income is that much more than Africa's. Africa as a continent has a $2,500 per capita income; India's is $3,000. If we can find the solution, I'm sure they can as well, and so can a lot of other emerging market countries. The question is really looking at the rest of the energy spectrum, which is actually 75% of energy consumption—the non-electricity part. There, we don't yet have the solutions in place, and that is where this capital of 100 billion dollars or 300 billion dollars can really go into the right structures to create the right subsidy mechanisms and the right risk mechanisms to create market demand for things like green ammonia, green hydrogen, etc., which can then get the whole green fuels business off and running. A lot of that production can happen in places like India and China, and can get supplied to the Far East, the European Union, and so on, and eventually start replacing fossil fuels in some of the molecule parts of the energy value chain. That is an area where we really need to deploy capital very intelligently, but where—if you ask me honestly—the Western world, for all their promises, has not really kept pace. And when I say the Western world, I'm not including the US, because the US has, of course, pulled out of the Paris Climate Accords. But let's say even in Europe or in the Far East, we're not seeing demand creation happening fast enough. For example, the European Union had set a target of 20 million tons of green hydrogen by 2030, and so far there is virtually nothing. Those markets have to develop because once they do, there will be scale, which will bring down pricing quite substantially and develop a model for the non-electricity, non-fossil fuel part of the energy value chain. So, we need those kinds of developments to happen. If you ask me, I think capital needs to go into creating demand for the non-electricity part of the energy value chain—in things like green fuels through risk mitigation mechanisms, subsidy mechanisms, etc. That is where the capital is best spent, and for the Western world, that capital will then stay within their borders, so they don't even have an issue of it going away from their countries. That, I think, will develop these industries and start taking the climate change agenda forward much faster because then we'll get into a whole new area of carbon emissions reduction. I think those are the things that need to happen. Carbon markets also need to develop. Every country is now developing their own carbon markets, and they all need to get linked through these Article 6.2 bilateral agreements. That is something that has to happen really quickly so that we can start coming up with carbon emissions reduction projects wherever they are best deployed and are cheapest to do. In that case, capital will start going into the most efficient areas rather than in areas which are not so efficient, which leads to inefficient deployment of capital from a carbon reduction standpoint. I haven't seen any analysis, by the way, globally, that says capital invested in XYZ area has a certain level of efficiency in terms of carbon reduction. But if we have that kind of framework, then I think that will be very useful to start directing capital in the right directions for carbon emissions reductions. And I'm sure there is definitely more space for cooperation among the Global South. Now, I want to talk about public-private partnerships, and I want to turn to Ms. Noureddin. The power we generate during the daytime can be saved and used during the nighttime, and this can also increase the price of renewable energy. It was much higher than fossil fuels, but with technology development, the cost was optimized and it became superior to fossil fuels. We can refer to storage in the same way. With storage added, the green energy system cost is a little bit higher than fossil fuels, but if we look at the value of using green energy and give it priority, people investing in green energy will get a better return in the long run compared with fossil fuel investments, making green energy solutions more valuable. If we take China as an example, with renewable energy development in 2025, the purchase of oil and gas is reduced by 250 billion US dollars. In China alone, in 2025, we will have saved 110 billion US dollars because we buy less oil and gas. In the long run, we can see the great benefits of renewable energy. In the short run, we may see a conflict because the temporary cost is higher. So, I believe that it is beneficial to give green energy priority in deployment. In China, we have also seen that enterprises and local governments are required to take responsibility for reducing carbon emissions, giving priority to green energy. Inside Instacart's AI-Powered Smart Shopping Cart | NVIDIA AI Podcast Ep. 302

NVIDIA(2.2M)·39:55·4.3K views·2026-06-24
David McIntoshChief Connected Stores Officer, Instacart
TL;DRInstacart is bridging the gap between physical and digital retail using its NVIDIA Jetson-powered "Caper Carts," which employ edge AI and sensor fusion to provide real-time basket tracking and personalized recommendations.
This "Connected Store" ecosystem is driving...

TL;DR

Instacart is bridging the gap between physical and digital retail using its NVIDIA Jetson-powered "Caper Carts," which employ edge AI and sensor fusion to provide real-time basket tracking and personalized recommendations. This "Connected Store" ecosystem is driving double-digit sales lifts for retail partners and laying the foundation for a fully unified, software-optimized grocery experience.

## The Caper Cart: Edge AI & Sensor Fusion

  • Onboard Edge Computing: Caper Carts utilize onboard NVIDIA Jetson processors to run AI models locally inside the cart.
      • Latency Reduction: Edge processing enables response times in the hundreds of milliseconds, which is critical for real-time shopping feedback. Traditional cloud processing (taking several seconds) is too slow and fails in steel-constructed grocery stores with poor cellular and Wi-Fi connectivity.
  • Sensor Fusion Architecture: The carts combine multiple physical and visual inputs to build a highly accurate, real-time picture of the customer's basket.
      • Weights and Measures Scale: The entire basket serves as a highly sensitive scale (certified for weighing produce in the US) that acts as the "ground truth" for cart contents.
      • Camera Array: Multiple cameras look into the basket to identify items and mitigate edge-case issues like visual obstruction or rapid item entry/removal.
      • Visual SLAM and Location Tracking: Carts use location sensors and visual SLAM (Simultaneous Localization and Mapping) to pinpoint their position in store aisles within inches.

## Driving Retail Value and Personalization

  • Double-Digit Sales Lift: Grocers deploying Caper Carts—including Wakefern (ShopRite/Fairway) in the US, Coles in Australia, and Morrisons in the UK—consistently see double-digit percentage increases in overall sales.
  • Predictive Recommendations: Instacart applies its digital database of over 1.6 billion lifetime online orders and a 2-billion-item catalog to the physical grocery aisle.
      • Location-Aware Prompts: Carts use side-facing cameras to recognize exactly what is on the shelf next to the shopper, serving hyper-local ads and suggestions.
      • The "Did You Forget?" Feature: When heading to checkout, the cart prompts shoppers with highly personalized suggestions (e.g., reminding a customer to grab the yogurt they buy weekly). This single feature drove a 1% absolute increase in in-store sales.
  • Consumer Convenience: Shoppers highly value having a real-time, running budget total on screen, instant access to coupons, and the ability to bag items as they shop and bypass checkout lanes entirely.

## Optimizing Store Operations and CPG Collaboration

  • Proactive Inventory AI: By combining "Store View" technology (where Instacart gig-shoppers scan shelves with phones) with the Caper Cart's side-facing cameras, Instacart builds real-time 3D maps of the store's inventory.
      • Out-of-Stock Agents: Automated AI agents track shelf states and proactively alert store managers when popular items are out of stock, shifting operations from reactive to proactive.
  • Employee-First Hardware Design: Instacart designed the hardware to fit seamlessly into existing store behaviors. Carts feature "stackable charging," allowing them to charge automatically when pushed into one another in a cart corral, eliminating the operational burden of manually plugging in individual units.
  • CPG-Facing Spatial Analytics: For Consumer Packaged Goods (CPG) brands, Caper Cart data reveals exactly where items (e.g., bread placed in multiple promotional spots throughout a store) are actually grabbed by shoppers, enabling brands to optimize product placement.

## The 10-Year Horizon: Unifying Physical and Digital Grocery

  • Seamless Omnichannel Shopping: Instacart's long-term vision is that in 5 to 10 years, there will be no distinction between online and physical shopping. Users will operate in a single, unified mode where online meal planning, budgeting, and allergy lists sync instantly to in-store smart devices.
  • Hybrid Fulfillment: Future grocery trips may be hybrid; heavy or pantry items are pre-picked and waiting inside a personalized Caper Cart at the storefront, while the consumer uses the smart cart to hand-select their own fresh produce.
  • A Foundation for Robotics: The massive physical dataset being generated—including precise 3D store maps, accurate item weights, and multidimensional object imagery—creates a "grocery foundation model" that could ultimately unlock automated robotic workflows in stores.

And so, key to our approach is doing a lot of these calculations around what's in the basket and recommendations at the edge. And so, the way it works is that we have several sensors coming together. We have camera sensors, we have the weight sensor, we have the location sensor, and then we have that sensor fusion system. So, we have an edge encoder that processes a lot of those signals at the edge. We also, for longer sessions, longer analysis, will look at those sessions in the cloud with a separate decoder. And then, we'll put the two together in what we call an overall shopping decoder—shopping experience decoder—that builds the best understanding of the customer's basket. And you can imagine that in this environment, there's thousands and thousands of edge cases that could emerge in terms of the way that people are shopping with this product. And so, we've really found that it's important to have multiple signals all coming together. In fact, we published a blog post on this, announced it at GTC, and actually walked through this in a GTC talk. If you look at just camera alone, there's all kinds of ways that the camera can easily be tricked. And I don't use the word trick to imply necessarily an intent on the user. It's just in the natural way that people are shopping—pulling things out at different speeds, a camera getting blocked, the cart getting full—all those things, we've found you absolutely need those multiple sensors coming together. The weight of the cart, that basket, is almost sort of like an X-ray that builds that ground truth understanding of what's actually happening with this thing, with all of the camera inputs informing it.
