It's a, it's a, it's a software system. It's sort of like getting an update to an app, right, or your phone. We ship regular updates to our retailer partners, so the software is getting better all the time. And then, this is probably a little bit, you know, in the weeds, but from a, from a, from a technical perspective, some of these models about the store might refresh as frequently as 15 minutes. Okay. Right? And so, they're really designed to get a, as close as we can, up-to-the-minute view of what's happening. Because again, going back to the example of out-of-stock, the item might have been on the shelf 15 minutes ago, but then when someone comes to the shelf either to grab it and put it in their own cart or to pick the order for a customer online, it may not, it may not be there. And so, that, that real-time understanding of the store that we're building from the more than half a million Instacart shoppers that go into a store every day, and from these Caper Carts that go through the store and are continuously scanning the shelf with the side-facing cameras, that's unlocking a foundation for us to build new agentic experiences on top that start to automate the optimization of the store to then ultimately deliver a better experience to users, it makes the associates' job easier, and ultimately drives value for the retailers. Yeah. You, you said, you said the word agentic. What's your approach to using agentic AI right now? You mentioned, you know, the one agent kicking off and doing something in the background, but is Instacart, are you deploying agentic systems as, as part of what you're doing in stores? Are you looking at agents for sort of different expertise, kind of, you know, tasks and lines of thinking? What's your philosophy on using agents? Yeah, I think our, our vision is—I think consistent with, with NVIDIA's vision, which is we believe that there's going to be experts for different tasks, right? And so, the way that we're approaching it is that we're building the foundation model for grocery. And so, that foundation model is taking in a couple different sources. It's taking in the 1.6 billion lifetime plus online grocery delivery orders, the 2 billion item catalog that we have, and then it's taking all the in-store data, right? It's taking this understanding of not just the shelves but also the clickstream. Where is the user pausing in the store? Going back to your example, kid taking something out of the cart, what convinces you to take something out of your cart? What convinces you to put that thing in? Where are you in the store when you're adding something that you normally don't add to the store, right? And so, it's that triangulation of where you are, what you're doing, building the best understanding of what is the most personalized experience for you in-store and online, and really marry the two data sources together. That's the continuously learning system, right? Which you then can stack on top of agentic applications. Whether it's a cart assistant that lets you plan your trip to the store and then sync that plan to the cart when you're in the store, or shop it with in-store mode from an app, right? Or it's building associate-facing tools, like the example I described. Or even CPG-facing tools. So, another example, there, if you, if you look at a lot of the ShopRite Wakefern stores we're in, you will find bread from a particular vendor being in, let's say, half a dozen places throughout the store. That bread company wants to understand, "Where in the store is my bread being bought? Where are people actually taking it off the shelf?" And so, today, what we can do is give that CPG an understanding of, stack-ranked list, here's the top six places people buy that, that bread in the store, right? And so, then you can imagine the next step in that process is to use all the location data, the heat map, the 3D representation of the store, and expert agents on top to say, "You know what, brand, brand bread CPG, you should put a seventh location, and here's where you should put it in the store. You should put an eighth location." By the way, and it'll weigh that out against the cost of a person not putting those locations in another store because they're burdened by those additional two locations. Or it might say, "Look, in this store, you only need four. Take away those two locations and put that in another store." And so, again, those are all the types of things that can start to be unlocked as you build that really rich understanding of not only the experience in-store, but then online and really marry the two data sources together. That's the continuously learning system. Yeah, no, that's really interesting. John Cornyn Celebrates Efforts To Strengthen Critical Mineral Supply Chain, Reshore Chip Industry

Forbes Breaking News(5.6M)·17:47·731 views·2026-06-24
John CornynUS Senator (R-TX)
TL;DRSen.
John Cornyn (R-TX) highlights the urgent national security and economic risks of America's reliance on foreign nations—particularly China and Taiwan—for advanced semiconductors and critical minerals, championing the bipartisan CHIPS and Science Act as a crucial...

TL;DR

Sen. John Cornyn (R-TX) highlights the urgent national security and economic risks of America's reliance on foreign nations—particularly China and Taiwan—for advanced semiconductors and critical minerals, championing the bipartisan CHIPS and Science Act as a crucial legislative success to reshore manufacturing and secure US leadership in the global AI race.

The Geopolitical Threat and Semiconductor Vulnerability

  • China’s Long-Term Strategy: China has spent decades executing a "hide your strength and bide your time" strategy under the Chinese Communist Party, exploiting access to Western markets and sensitive technologies while defying international norms.
  • The 2027 Taiwan Threat: Chinese President Xi Jinping has ordered the People's Liberation Army to be prepared to reincorporate Taiwan by 2027.
      • Strategic Monopoly: Over 90% of the world's most advanced semiconductors are manufactured outside the US, primarily by Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company (TSMC) in Taiwan and Samsung in South Korea.
      • Defense Risk: A Chinese takeover of Taiwan would allow Beijing to cut off US access to advanced chips, instantly paralyzing US defense production (including avionics for fifth-generation fighters like the F-35, missile defense systems, and AI data centers).
  • The US Manufacturing Decline: The US share of global semiconductor manufacturing has steadily collapsed over the last three decades:
      • 1990: The US produced nearly 40% of the world's semiconductors.
      • 2021: Domestic production dropped to 12%.
      • 2024: The US share of advanced semiconductor manufacturing sits at just 8%.

Broader Supply Chain Dependencies: Critical Minerals and Pharmaceuticals

  • Rare Earth Elements and Magnet Monopolies: The US is highly vulnerable to China's near-monopoly on the mining and processing of rare earth elements (such as lithium), which are required for high-tech motors, drones, defense systems, and electric vehicles.
      • Coercive Labor Practices: China secured its supply chain dominance via the Belt and Road Initiative, purchasing mines in the Democratic Republic of the Congo and Tanzania to process raw materials under low humanitarian standards.
      • US Shortage: There are only two locations in the US that produce motors utilizing rare earth magnets, leaving the nation dependent on Chinese processing.
  • Active Pharmaceutical Ingredients (APIs): China has successfully cornered the global market on the raw materials that make up essential pharmaceutical ingredients, creating a highly dangerous scenario where the US is dependent on an adversary for everyday medical supplies.

The Bipartisan Legislative Remedy: The CHIPS and Science Act

  • Pandemic Wake-Up Call: Supply chain disruptions during the COVID-19 pandemic—ranging from shortages of basic medical PPE to automotive microchips—forced policymakers to realize the danger of offshore manufacturing. Early alarms had previously been raised by Trump administration officials Mike Pompeo and Wilbur Ross.
  • The Legislative Path:
      • In 2020, Sen. Cornyn and Sen. Mark Warner (D-VA) introduced the *CHIPS for America Act*, with crucial House support from Rep. Michael McCaul (R-TX).
      • The initiative was later fully funded through the bipartisan *CHIPS and Science Act*.
  • Federal Funding Breakdown:
      • $40 Billion: Allocated for domestic semiconductor manufacturing incentives to construct "fabs" (fabrication plants) on US soil.
      • $13 Billion: Allocated for cutting-edge R&D and workforce training programs to ensure American workers can fill these highly complex, well-paying technical roles.
  • AI Leadership: Securing local advanced chip production is vital for the US to win the AI competition against China, as large data centers require massive volumes of advanced chips to train AI models.

Texas as a National Semiconductor Leader

  • Federal and State Synergy: Federal CHIPS Act funding has directly catalyzed major private expansions in Texas, supporting new semiconductor fabs for Samsung, Texas Instruments, and other leading manufacturers.
  • State-Level Legislation: Following federal efforts, Texas Governor Greg Abbott signed the *Texas CHIPS Act*, establishing the Texas Semiconductor Innovation Consortium and the Texas Semiconductor Innovation Fund to further incentivize local production.
  • Economic Impact:
      • The semiconductor industry now contributes over $469 billion to the Texas economy.
      • Texas boasts the second-largest semiconductor workforce in the nation, supporting more than 42,000 high-paying jobs.

Notable Quotes

"If they are successful in their quest to pull off the so-called reunification with Taiwan, President Xi could cut the United States off from our ability to access the most advanced chips made in the world... and bring our defense production to a grinding halt at the snap of a finger." — Sen. John Cornyn
"We have been asleep at the switch and allowed China to hide their motives and bide their time until they put us in an incredibly vulnerable position." — Sen. John Cornyn
"In the 19th century, the key ingredient of military power was gunpowder. In the 20th century, it was petroleum. And now it's semiconductors." — Dr. Will Inboden (quoted by Sen. John Cornyn)
I ask unanimous consent that the quorum call be dispensed with. Without objection. Mr. President, there's an old Chinese saying attributed to Deng Xiaoping that goes: hide your strength and bide your time. Hide your strength, bide your time. China has, of course, been hiding its strength and biding its time for many years now. For too long, the West has turned a blind eye to what's been happening in the People's Republic of China, controlled, of course, by the Chinese Communist Party. We allowed them to join the World Trade Organization, naively hoping that by allowing them to participate in the global economy, they would somehow adopt Western principles of freedom and democracy. But as I said, that was a naive hope, and it didn't happen. To the contrary, China benefited from this access to global markets and became a major world economy. But they actively resisted Western standards, as we know now. China continues to refuse to follow international norms. They use their access to sensitive technologies to steal them. They have become a major global power that we, of course, cannot trust. We should take President Xi at his word when he said that China plans to reincorporate Taiwan by 2027, less than a year away. He's told the People's Liberation Army to be ready for that by 2027. Never in the history of the world has the United States' strategic competition with China mattered more. Perhaps the most critical nexus of this competition is in manufacturing in critical industries such as advanced semiconductors. We know semiconductors underpin all modern technology—everything from the phone in our pocket to the avionics in an F-35 Joint Strike Fighter, even in our washing machines and our refrigerators. Chips power far more than consumer electronics. They're integral to the energy grid, cell towers, water treatment plants, agriculture equipment, and other forms of critical infrastructure. They also play a huge role in our national defense. Missile defense systems, advanced fifth-generation fighters like the F-35, as I mentioned, artificial intelligence, data centers—all of these require an astounding number of the most advanced semiconductors. Now, with the global AI competition becoming a two-country race between the United States and China, it's clear that control of the future of the world economy will come down to which nation can produce the most advanced semiconductor chips and secure complex and highly vulnerable supply chains. Today, more than 90% of the world's most advanced semiconductors are made other than in the United States, mainly in Taiwan, but of course South Korea, where Samsung is located as well. Going back to 1990, the United States produced nearly 40% of the world's semiconductors. But over time, we lost our advantage in this critical industry. By 2021, this figure had decreased to 12%, and by 2024, our share represented only 8% of the advanced semiconductors made in the world. With China threatening the invasion of Taiwan and the possibility of disruption as a result of another pandemic or perhaps a natural disaster, this issue has never been more important. Should they be successful in their quest to pull off the so-called reunification with Taiwan, President Xi could cut the United States off from our ability to access the most advanced chips made in the world by Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company, bringing our defense production to a grinding halt at the snap of a finger. But don't just take my word for it. In a widely cited 2023 report from the US-Taiwan Business Council, it says: "A significant shock to the delivery of chips would have an impact as large as either of the two previous economic recessions in the United States: the global financial crisis of 2009 and the global pandemic of 2020." Well, we might ask, "How did we find ourselves in this place with this incredible vulnerability? How did the United States go from producing almost half of the world's chips to now less than 10%?" Well, Dan Wang, who wrote the book *Breakneck*, has maybe a suggestion. He says one difference is America's run by lawyers and China's run by engineers. Now, as a recovering lawyer myself, I don't think that's a complete explanation, but the point is this has been a point of focus by the Chinese Communist Party and the People's Republic of China for decades now, while they've hidden their motives and bided their time. And now we find ourselves in an increased position of vulnerability and in a competition with them for this most advanced technology to fuel the data centers that train the artificial intelligence that are going to determine how everything from our weapons systems to our industrial economy works in the future. But the difference is this: China has a willingness to violate international law and it has a centralized economy. It doesn't have to worry about permitting. It doesn't have to worry about the bureaucracy. It doesn't have to worry about the slow rate at which the Pentagon creates and produces new weapons systems to compete and to keep the country strong. Conversely, we have a market economy in the United States that does provide a lot of strength, particularly in terms of strategic capital. But we needed to identify the importance of this essential technology before we began to do something about it. And now we know we also have vulnerabilities when it comes to things like critical minerals and rare earth element producing. There's only two places in the United States that actually produce the motors that are made from magnets that are produced from rare earths, and 90% of it is done in China. But literally, this is another example of a component of the technology that goes into almost every piece of technology, certainly everything with a motor in it, and we are dependent still largely on China for access to those rare earth elements and the critical mineral processing. China built its empire of industrial inputs on the back of forced labor and diplomatic manipulation. That's another difference between the United States and China. What they can't produce at home, they coerce others to produce for them in places like Africa, Southeast Asia, and even Latin America, at prices that undercut world markets and with almost no humanitarian protections for their laborers. China has done this in a multitude of industries, as I said, not just semiconductors. Through its Belt and Road Initiative, China has become the primary buyer of rare earths from mines in the Democratic Republic of the Congo in the heart of Africa, in Tanzania, and other parts of Africa. This has allowed them to solidify their control of the supply chain for these rare earth elements, which I said are essential to literally every electric motor. And they've enforced strict export controls to maintain their position. They've used this strategy to create a near-global monopoly on the mining and processing of rare earths and critical minerals such as lithium, which is used to produce magnets and other essential components for vehicles, drones, defense systems, and much more. China has also cornered the market on raw materials that make up all of our pharmaceutical ingredients, so-called API—active pharmaceutical inputs. Can you imagine the United States and other countries around the world dependent on China to produce the medications that we take on a regular basis to keep us healthy and cure disease? But that's where we are right now. We have been asleep at the switch and allowed China to hide their motives and bide their time until they put us in an incredibly vulnerable position. Well, the United States finally started to wake up to this during the COVID pandemic, when we began to ask, "Okay, where can we get the masks and the gowns necessary for medical personnel to treat people with the COVID infections?" Lo and behold, we didn't make any of it in America. All of it was made in China. And that's just one simple example. With global supply chains thrown into disarray, we found ourselves with a shortage of many essential goods, particularly those that require semiconductors. The demand for electronic devices for students and new remote work policies helped America realize the urgent need to reshore—that is, to bring back to America some of the manufacturing capability which we had over the years allowed China to attract to our detriment. Even industries like auto manufacturing were severely disrupted by the lack of global chip supplies. The Trump administration had already contemplated the issue of chips and this vulnerability before COVID and asked Congress to act. And I still remember the Secretary of State particularly, Mike Pompeo, and the Secretary of Commerce, Wilbur Ross, were the first ones in the first Trump administration to bring this to the attention and to sound the alarm. But the pandemic made clear as day that we could not afford to kick the can down the road on something as essential as semiconductors. So back in 2020, I got together with our colleague Senator Mark Warner, a Democrat from Virginia, to introduce the CHIPS for America Act. The goal of the bill was pretty simple: to restore American leadership in the semiconductor manufacturing industry. It authorized the creation of a chips program at the Department of Commerce to support all chip-making activities on American soil, from R&D to manufacturing. Within six months, with the good help of our friends across the Capitol in the House of Representatives, particularly my friend Michael McCaul, Congressman from the Austin area, within six months the CHIPS Act became law. And about a year and a half later, it was fully funded by Congress through the bipartisan CHIPS and Science Act. This law provided nearly $40 billion in manufacturing incentives for semiconductors to bring that capacity back to American soil, and roughly $13 billion for research and workforce development. Because you need more than a—it pays more than a minimum wage to do these highly sophisticated manufacturing jobs of producing these advanced semiconductors. So training of the workforce was really very, very important, and continued investment in research and development so we can stay ahead of the Chinese competition. But obviously most important to me in the CHIPS and Science Act was the major investment that this produced in my state, the state of Texas. We used this funding to build new semiconductor fabs at Samsung, Texas Instruments, and other places, create new workforce pathways and quickly upskill Texans for these industries, and accelerate advanced chip manufacturing. In 2023, Governor Abbott signed the Texas CHIPS Act to build on our work at the federal level and further invest in the Texas chips industry. This state law established the Texas Semiconductor Innovation Consortium and the Texas Semiconductor Innovation Fund to further incentivize investment in this critical industry, which contributes more than $469 billion to the Texas economy. So today, Texas has the second largest semiconductor workforce in the nation, totaling more than 42,000 well-paying jobs thanks to these investments. And I'm proud to say that Texas has not only become a national but a global leader in the semiconductor industry. Now, there's certainly a lot more work that needs to be done to address America's supply chain vulnerabilities, not only for semiconductors but for mining and processing of rare earth elements and active pharmaceutical ingredients as well. But the CHIPS and Science Act was an important step toward reshoring our critical industries. My friend Will Inboden, former executive director of the Clements Center for National Security at the University of Texas, who now serves as executive vice president and provost at the University, summed it up perfectly. He said in the 19th century, the key ingredient of military power was gunpowder. In the 20th century, it was petroleum. And now, it's semiconductors. I'm proud this legislation has proven to be a contributor to reshoring American manufacturing in an industry that will determine who controls the future of the world. Were it not for the CHIPS and Science Act, we may not have been in a position to help build the artificial intelligence economy with the advent of these huge data centers necessary to train the AI models so that we can be a leader and indeed win the competition with China. If we didn't have access to these advanced semiconductors and continued to be dependent on those produced over in Asia, it's doubtful we would be able to be where we are in terms of leading in that competition. So thanks to the generational investment that was made in this legislation, if we have the fortitude and determination to do so, the US has the potential to both design and build here right at home the infrastructure we need in this century and the next. The truth is we live in very exciting times. Technology is rapidly advancing in life-changing ways, sometimes even faster than we know how to keep up with. But what is critical is that America continues its leadership and continues to lead this revolution. I want to make sure that future generations can live in a world where the US, not the Chinese Communist Party, remains dominant and in a world where we continue to lead from a framework of freedom, democracy, and the rule of law. Keeping up with domestic manufacturing in critical technologies like semiconductors is essential to that end. So as I look back, I'm very proud of the work we all did together through this CHIPS and Science Act, which transformed not only our national security but transformed Texas and put us in a posture to ensure that America leads the way for generations yet to come. Mr. President, I yield the floor and I would note the absence of a quorum. Clerk will call the roll. Ms. Alsbrooks.

How sunday Became A Restaurant Fintech Unicorn By Killing The Paper Check

Forbes(2.1M)·40:55·168 views·2026-06-24
Maggie McGrathHost, Forbes C-Suite Unscripted Christine de WendelCo-founder and CEO, sunday
TL;DRBy replacing traditional paper check-outs with immediate QR-code mobile payments integrated into point-of-sale (POS) systems, fintech unicorn **sunday** has scaled to over $5 billion in transaction volume across six countries, saving restaurants an average of 12 minutes per table turn.

TL;DR

By replacing traditional paper check-outs with immediate QR-code mobile payments integrated into point-of-sale (POS) systems, fintech unicorn sunday has scaled to over $5 billion in transaction volume across six countries, saving restaurants an average of 12 minutes per table turn.

***

The Product and the 12-Minute Table Turn

  • Frictionless Checkout: sunday utilizes table-placed QR codes linked directly to a restaurant’s POS system, allowing diners to split bills, calculate tips, and pay in under 10 seconds without waiting for a physical check.
  • 12-Minute Table Savings: sunday’s data confirms that digital checkout saves an average of 12 minutes per table turn. This acceleration helps high-volume restaurants seat extra walk-ins during peak shifts (e.g., weekend brunch and Friday/Saturday nights).
  • Empowering Servers: By eliminating the administrative burden of running credit cards and splitting checks, servers gain time back to upsell, interact with diners, and elevate the hospitality experience.
  • POS Integrations: The platform integrates natively with 28 major full-service POS platforms—including Toast, NCR, Micros, and Square—offering a specialized checkout experience that outperforms watered-down, built-in features from major POS competitors.

Scaling Through Post-Pandemic Adaptation

  • Pandemic Roots: Co-founded in April 2021 by US CEO Christine de Wendel and European restaurateur Victor Lugger, the product was originally beta-tested as a touchless safety solution for Lugger's Big Mamma restaurant group.
  • The Menu Pivot: While early adoption was driven by contact-free menus, 90% of sunday’s restaurant clients have returned to physical paper menus post-COVID, choosing to use sunday exclusively for the payment portion of the meal.
  • Adoption Metrics: Across 3,500+ restaurant groups, adoption rates range from 35% up to 80% in top-performing spots like Lettuce Entertain You's Chicago venues. High adoption is driven by proactive server onboarding and integration with restaurant loyalty programs.
  • Fine Dining & Discreet Payments: Despite early assumptions that QR codes might diminish luxury service, Michelin-starred and high-end venues (such as the Seven Reasons group in DC) use the app to facilitate fast, highly discreet payments for time-sensitive corporate lunches and private dinners.

The "Shift Work" Philosophy and Operational Legitimacy

  • Mandatory Restaurant Shifts: As part of onboarding, every sunday employee—including software engineers—is required to work shifts at a partner restaurant (such as Big Mamma's high-volume venues).
  • Building Empathy and Trust: Experiencing the high-stress reality of front-of-house operations on a busy Saturday night helps tech teams build empathy and ensures they understand the real-world impact of system downtime.
  • Product Feedback Loop: Real-world training shifts yield direct product iterations. sunday hosts a dedicated Slack channel where employees submit operational insights and immediate "light bulb" UI/UX improvements from their floor shifts.
  • Cross-Industry Application: De Wendel strongly advocates that corporate executives in any sector experience frontline customer realities (e.g., working in e-commerce warehouses or answering customer support calls) to foster humility and deep brand alignment.

Serendipity and De Wendel’s Executive Journey

  • An Accidental Co-Founding: The partnership with Victor Lugger was sparked by a seasonal holiday card sent by de Wendel in 2020 after she relocated from Europe back to Atlanta. Seeing she was in the US, Lugger called her to pitch spinning off his restaurant's checkout tech.
  • Career Pivots: De Wendel initially aimed to work in international diplomacy—failing the Foreign Service exam three times—before pivoting to business consulting at Bain & Company, where she built her foundational strategic toolkit.
  • Scaling Pedigree: Before sunday, de Wendel managed high-growth tech operations in Europe, serving as a key executive at fashion e-tailer Zalando (scaling the French division to IPO) and Chief Operating Officer at home-improvement marketplace ManoMano.
  • Growth Trajectory: After securing $125 million in seed and Series A funding in 2021 and growing from 0 to 400 employees in its first year, sunday aims to double its transaction volume to $10 billion in the next year while targeting market expansion in California, Texas, and Florida.

***

Notable Quotes

"On average we save our restaurants 12 minutes on the table turn... why? It's because when you pay with sunday you don't have to flag down your server, ask them for the check, have it brought out to you, and then have your card go back of house to run the check." — Christine de Wendel
"We over-index on a great operations team... and once [servers] can see the value, they're actually encouraging guests to pay with sunday." — Christine de Wendel
"Having everyone on the executive team answer inbound customer service calls created tremendous empathy, a lot of humility... I think there are ways to do it, either with partners or with just customer-facing initiatives. If you're working for a retail brand, you have to go work in the floor even if you're in the corporate office." — Christine de Wendel
What if a QR code could solve the agonizing issue of flagging down a server to get your check at the end of a great meal? Our next guest is betting that it can. Welcome to C-Suite Unscripted. I'm Maggie McGrath, your host, and today I sat down with Christine de Wendel. She is the co-founder and US CEO of sunday, an app that makes it possible to pay your restaurant bill immediately from your phone. Launched just five years ago, sunday processes more than $5 billion in transaction volume and counts as clients buzzy restaurants like Tao, Din Tai Fung, and Little Goat Diner. Christine and I talked about how a well-timed holiday card was the accidental start to her entrepreneurial journey, why she requires all employees to work at least one restaurant shift, and why she thought it would be easy to build a billion-dollar business. But for folks who've not had my same experience, can you give the 60-second overview as to what sunday is? Yes. sunday is a digital checkout solution. We make paying in restaurants very easy for the guest, and we bring operational efficiency, data, and a delightful experience to the restaurant. Our main product is exactly what you saw. It's a QR code that we put on the table that is connected to the point of sale system, and it allows the guest to pay in 10 seconds. You have $5 billion in transaction volume on this platform. You work with more than 3,500 restaurant groups. Are you in six countries right now? Yes, we are in six countries. And the founding date is just 2021, just five years ago. I've heard you say, or maybe I read somewhere, that there's a 12-minute savings in terms of paying with the QR code and with the sunday app versus the traditional wave down the server, get them to come sign a paper check. Is that true today? That's absolutely true. On average, we save our restaurants 12 minutes on the table turn. We actually look at the data every day: how fast does a table turn that doesn't pay with sunday versus, in the same restaurant, a table turn that does pay with sunday? And then we compare the data. That's across countries, it's across point of sale systems, across cities—it's pretty amazing. And why? It's because when you pay with sunday, you don't have to flag down your server, ask them for the check, have it brought out to you, and then have your card go back of house to run the check. Then you don't have to do the math on the tip and give it back to your server. And so, that experience on average saves the restaurant and the guest around 12 minutes. I actually love doing the math on the tip. I like being faster than my friends. You told me you were a data and a fact-checker. Yeah, I like data. But most people are happy to have it done for them. When I think—so, that's 12 minutes per table. Are you seeing at the restaurants that you work with additional reservations because of the time saved? Or is there a measurable increase in revenue, not necessarily from ordering extra food, but literally because all of that time saved adds up to more people coming in? Absolutely, in very high-volume restaurants that turn a lot of different tables. Usually what we'll see—because let's face it, most restaurants in America and Europe don't have a line out the door every day—is that where we really add a ton of value is during your busy shifts. So Saturday brunch, Friday night, Saturday night. For those shifts, you're going to be able to put in a couple more walk-ins because your tables are turning faster, and that adds revenue. This means they can actually spend more time upselling, chatting with you, asking you if you want a dessert or an extra glass of wine. An employee at sunday has, as part of their onboarding program, to spend time in a restaurant. And so I had this fantasy, frankly, of joining an early-stage startup, starting a new country for a successful startup, and having a ton of flexibility. The flexibility was there, but I worked like crazy and we grew that company. Three years into my tenure, the company went public. When I left after seven years, there were thousands of employees, the market cap was over 20 billion, and it was an incredible story. Flexibility, yes, but a super intense workload, also. Which I guess is good training ground to eventually become a CEO and co-founder. But from Zalando you went to ManoMano as Chief Operating Officer. Did I hear you say that was your favorite job of the prior 15 years? Yes. I joined ManoMano after seven years at Zalando, where we had scaled the company, took the company public, and I was running the French business for them. By that time, I had three small kids. I was traveling every week to Berlin—the headquarters of Zalando was in Berlin—and I was living in Paris. And so, Monday morning at 6 AM with my little day bag, I would fly to Berlin. My team was in Berlin, I was managing around 200 people in Berlin, and my three little kids were at home. And so when I came back from maternity leave with number three, I went back to work and then after a couple of months I was like, "Okay, this is nuts. I'm living on EasyJet and it's hard to balance everything." I think you can do everything, but you need to time it properly. And so I said I want to join a company that's got great values, is growing as quickly as Zalando is, but where I don't have to travel as much. ManoMano is the leading home improvement platform in Europe. It's a marketplace. I loved the founders; they had great energy and they had this beautiful vision that you could build a fast-growing company and still create tremendous balance for the teams. Now, it made it much easier that we were in the peak of e-commerce years, so there was a ton of growth and a ton of funding. Let's be honest, it's not as easy to run an e-commerce company today as it was between 2017 and 2020 when I was there. But it was amazing. The people we hired, the culture was outstanding, but we had major tailwinds, and so that made it much easier to have such a phenomenal culture. So what made you leave? So when I turned 40, I decided, one, I wanted to start my own company. Two, my three very Parisian kids were so French I needed to Americanize them a little bit. And so my husband and I had always said that when the kids were little, we would move to the US and give them an American life. Because the optimism, the enthusiasm, the opportunity that comes with growing up in America is unparalleled. My husband is very French but he loves the US, and so in 2020 we both quit our jobs and we moved back to my hometown, Atlanta. And that's exactly when I sent my Christmas card to Victor, and that's how the sunday story started. And the rest is almost history. I do have to ask you, though, I heard you say that you kind of had this unrealistic idea that if you can't build a billion-dollar business, it's not a success. At what point in your career did you feel that way, and do you still feel that way today? It's one of the challenges I had when I moved to Atlanta in 2020. I had been incredibly lucky at Zalando—the company went public, you know, turned into a $20 billion market cap company. Then I joined ManoMano; there were 100 employees when I joined, and when I left there were 800. We were doing almost 3 billion in transaction volume. And so, I had had, as an executive, two incredible rides. When I started looking for ideas to start a company in 2020 in Atlanta, the bar was so high, and it was a really tough moment for me. I was in my basement in Atlanta, the kids were at school, and I was looking at a white piece of paper, and I said, "Okay, I've had these two awesome rides. Good looks like a billion dollars to me. I'm not just going to start a cool company and build it over 10 years." My bar was so high. I really struggled with that. It was a little naive. But when I left ManoMano, I said, "If you get lucky, you have a good idea, you execute well, and you get good funding, it's not that hard to build a billion-dollar company." Boy, was I wrong. And totally unrealistic, but I got really lucky to have two such awesome rides. And so, one of the things I love about partnering with Victor, my co-founder today, is he dreams so big. He is so much of a visionary. And when he called me up, he said, "We're going to build this enormous company, and we're going to go get a ton of funding"—so back to the $145 million we've raised. The fact that my co-founder dreams so big has been a huge part of the excitement and fulfillment of this adventure because we're quite aligned that if we're going to do this and we're going to spend so much time and so much energy building this, we want to go big. And so I tell people now, the first step in building a tremendously successful company, which we're still trying to build, is that you have to dream big. So when do you think sunday will be a billion-dollar company? It's a really good question. I think we're absolutely on track. We mentioned earlier that we're doing, you know, 5 billion in transaction volume after five years, which is awesome. We have 250 employees who are stellar. I'm pretty optimistic about our trajectory. Normally I would ask for one forward year production, but I didn't ask at the beginning—where does the name come from? Why sunday? sunday. When we started the company, we were very bold, we were dreaming big, and we said, "Okay, it needs to be a company that's going to work all over the Western world." So we need a name that works in many languages. Victor's French, I was in the US, we have an office in London, and we're active in several European countries. So, sunday was something that resonates in all languages, first of all. The second one is we wanted something that was a little playful. Our branding is hot pink, we don't take ourselves too seriously, we know we have to have fun with this—we're in the hospitality space. And so, we liked the idea that sunday sometimes evokes ice cream, even though we spell it like the day of the week. There's something playful around it, and that takes it a little less seriously. And honestly, the night before we announced the first fundraise, we still had a couple of names that we were working around, and this one was available. We liked it, and we're thrilled five years later that that's the name we took. It's amazing how often availability is one of the levers that contributes to a company name. All right. So now I will ask you: if you were to sit down with me one year from now, what would you hope to report that you and sunday have accomplished in that year? We certainly plan to be doing over 10 billion in transaction volume. So we're on a trajectory where we're doubling the business every year in terms of net revenue and transaction volume. For me, a big focus is expansion across the US. We have a great presence in Chicago, Atlanta, and New York. We're growing in Dallas, Miami, and Los Angeles. For me, a big focus this year is California, and there's tremendous potential. The first discussions we're having with restaurant groups are amazing. And so, I hope we can sit down together a year from now and I can tell you, "Yes, we totally cleared the 10 billion mark." It's pretty symbolic, but also that we have established our market share and presence on the West Coast, in Texas, and in Florida. That would be huge. And I hope that our product suite continues to expand, because payments is the entry point, but there's so much data and so much we can bring to restaurants and guests that we continue expanding that product suite. Well, I look forward to hearing that update, but in the meantime, Christine, thank you so much for sitting down with us. Thanks, I was delighted to be here. Forbes

Opening Plenary

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·61:50·690 views·2026-06-24
Klaus SchwabFounder and Executive Chairman, World Economic Forum Li QiangPremier of the People's Republic of China
TL;DRThe 17th Annual Meeting of the New Champions ("Summer Davos") in Dalian, China, convened over 1,800 global leaders to focus on the theme of "Innovating at Scale.
" Chinese Premier Li Qiang highlighted China's resilient 5% Q1 economic growth and championed a transition...

TL;DR

The 17th Annual Meeting of the New Champions ("Summer Davos") in Dalian, China, convened over 1,800 global leaders to focus on the theme of "Innovating at Scale." Chinese Premier Li Qiang highlighted China's resilient 5% Q1 economic growth and championed a transition toward "China Opportunity 2.0," urging global stakeholders to reject protectionist trade barriers in favor of multilateral technological collaboration.

Launching Summer Davos: Theme and Global Context

  • Event Overview: The 17th Annual Meeting of the New Champions gathered more than 1,800 leaders, including 200 technology pioneers, representing over 90 geographies at the Dalian International Convention Center.
      • Co-hosted by the World Economic Forum (WEF) and the Chinese government, the meeting focuses on frontier technologies, emerging economies, and next-generation leadership.
  • The Imperative to "Innovate at Scale": Ngoh Gium-Hui, Managing Director and Chair of Greater China at the WEF, emphasized that scaling innovation requires a holistic systems lens that balances local realities with global ideals.
      • The forum aims to catalyze actionable solutions to build institutions, reskill workforces, and protect the environment while navigating geopolitical and economic realities.
  • Deepening Ties with China: Alois Zwinggi, President and CEO of the WEF, highlighted a 40-year relationship with China dating back to 1979, noting that China remains an essential engine of the global economy, clean energy, and digital infrastructure.

China's Economic Outlook: The Four Pillars of Growth

Premier Li Qiang outlined the state of China's economy at the launch of its 15th Five-Year Plan, defining its trajectory through four key keywords:

  • Economic Stability: China registered a steady 5% GDP growth in Q1, demonstrating strong resilience in its massive 140 trillion RMB economy despite global supply chain disruptions and energy shortages.
  • Technological Innovation: The domestic artificial intelligence (AI) sector has seen explosive growth, with daily AI token usage hitting hundreds of trillions by the end of May, ranking first globally. Embodied intelligence has also moved into large-scale commercial application.
  • Market Dynamism: Driven by green and niche consumption, China's domestic market remains highly active, handling over 550 million express deliveries daily—surpassing the combined total of the rest of the world.
  • Global Integration: Rejecting protectionism, China has extended zero-tariff policies to 63 countries and maintained its position as the world's second-largest import market for 17 consecutive years, recording a 20.5% import growth in the first five months of the year.

The Mechanics of China's Innovation Ecosystem

Premier Li rejected narratives of government subsidies driving Chinese market competitiveness, attributing success to structured, long-term national effort:

  • Relentless R&D Investment: During the 14th Five-Year Plan (2021–2025), China's total R&D expenditure grew by an average of 10% annually, making it the world's second-largest R&D spender.
      • R&D investment by enterprises accounts for nearly 80% of China's total research spending.
      • Telecommunications giant Huawei invested over 1 trillion RMB in research over nearly 10 years to bypass external technological blockades and achieve core breakthroughs.
  • Accelerated Industrial Application: China's robust manufacturing supply chain dramatically shortens the time from lab to market, bridging the "Darwinian sea" of commercialization.
      • Premier Li noted that physical blueprints can be turned into functional consumer prototypes in Shenzhen in just one week, compared to up to a year in other countries.
  • Talent and Infrastructure Development: China produces approximately 7 million university graduates in science, engineering, agriculture, and medicine every year.
      • China claims 24 of the world's top 100 innovation clusters, topping the Global Innovation Index rankings for three consecutive years.
      • Specialized hubs include a one-hour electronic innovation circle in the Yangtze River Delta and a 30-minute robot-supporting circle in Shenzhen's Robot Valley.

Transitioning to "China Opportunity 2.0" and Global Cooperation

Rather than viewing China's technological rise as a threat ("China Shock 2.0"), Premier Li advocated for viewing it as a global benefit ("China Opportunity 2.0") that offers innovation dividends and investment returns:

  • Transition to "Created in China": Global enterprises are increasingly setting up R&D centers in China, with 14,000 foreign-funded scientific and technological service firms established in 2023 alone (a 27.2% year-on-year increase).
  • Exponential Capital Returns: Rapid growth pathways exist for local tech startups; robotics firm Unitree grew in valuation from 10 million RMB in 2016 to 42 billion RMB today.
  • Commitment to Open Source: Much of China's technological advancement is designed to be shared. China's open-source AI models have surpassed 10 billion cumulative downloads globally, and its large-scale scientific facilities (including the Tiangong space station) are open to international scientists and astronauts.
  • Call for Collective Tech Governance: Recognizing the "Cambrian moment" of AI, Premier Li urged global cooperation to manage the ethical risks and control liabilities of fast-moving tech, calling on world leaders to "tear down walls and build bridges."

Notable Quotes

"Pathfinders, pathfinders, there is no path, the path is made by walking." — *Ngoh Gium-Hui, quoting Spanish poet Antonio Machado*
"For China, our integrated development with the world has never been an expediency, but a strategic decision made in line with the trend of history and based on our own development philosophy." — *Premier Li Qiang*
"Advanced technologies should serve as tools that benefit people across countries rather than sources of chaos that undermine peace and stability." — *Premier Li Qiang*

It is a great honor to open this plenary here in China, a country with which the World Economic Forum has shared a long and meaningful relationship. Since its launch in Dalian in 2007, this meeting, often called Summer Davos, has become a global platform for entrepreneurship, innovation, and collaboration. It brings together leaders from government, business, academia, civil society, and the next generation to address the challenges and opportunities shaping the future. Artificial intelligence, advanced manufacturing, clean energy, biotechnology, and digital systems are reshaping industries and societies. At the same time, the world's most urgent challenges, from climate change and energy security to supply chain resilience and inclusive growth, cannot be solved by one country, by one company, or by one institution alone. In this context, China's role is essential. China is a major engine of the global economy, a center of manufacturing and innovation, and an increasingly important contributor to technological progress and international cooperation. The scale of China's market, the strength of its industrial base, and the dynamism of its entrepreneurs matter not only for China but for the world. China's advances in areas such as green technologies, digital infrastructure, mobility, life sciences, and artificial intelligence are helping to shape the next chapter of global growth. These advances also create important opportunities for cooperation—cooperation that can help ensure technology serves people, supports productivity, advances sustainability, and contributes to shared prosperity. At the World Economic Forum, we believe that dialogue is not an end in itself. Dialogue leads to understanding, understanding leads to trust, and trust leads to collaboration and action. That is why this meeting is so important. It provides a space where leaders can look beyond immediate pressures and focus on what can be built together. Your Excellency Premier Li, on behalf of the World Economic Forum, I would like to express our sincere appreciation for your continued support of this meeting and the forum's engagement with China.
