What happens when the Federal Reserve goes quiet? | Econ World

Reuters(4.2M)·36:03·892 views·2026-06-24
Carmel CrimminsHost, Econ World Kevin WarshFormer Governor, Federal Reserve Board
TL;DRUnder new Federal Reserve Chair Kevin Warsh, the U.S.
central bank is scaling back forward guidance and shifting toward a strategy of silence. While proponents argue this allows markets to naturally discover prices based on economic data, critics warn that reducing...

TL;DR

Under new Federal Reserve Chair Kevin Warsh, the U.S. central bank is scaling back forward guidance and shifting toward a strategy of silence. While proponents argue this allows markets to naturally discover prices based on economic data, critics warn that reducing communication risks raising market volatility and fueling damaging misinterpretations.

***

Kevin Warsh’s "Silent Revolution" at the Fed

  • A Pivot Toward Less Talk: New Fed Chair Kevin Warsh is leading a "silent revolution" to shorten policy statements and eliminate forward guidance—the practice of telling the public what the central bank plans to do with interest rates.
  • The Communication Review: Warsh has launched a comprehensive review of the Fed’s communication policies, targeting everything from regular press conferences to the release of meeting transcripts.
      • Transcript Censorship: Under current rules, every word spoken during Fed meetings is transcribed and released five years later. Warsh argues this delayed release inhibits free debate and policy planning. He previously recommended that the Bank of England turn off tape recorders during select policy sessions.
      • Rethinking the "Dot Plot": The review targets the Summary of Economic Projections (the "dot plot"). While meant to show individual policymakers' optimal policy paths, the dot plot is routinely misinterpreted by the public as an official, locked-in interest rate forecast.
  • Ending the Era of Spoon-Feeding: Warsh believes long-term bond rates should be set by supply and demand conditions in the economy, rather than being artificially managed by Fed forward guidance.

The Power and Purpose of "Central Bank Speak"

  • Managing Expectations: Modern monetary policy relies heavily on public psychology. Under the New Keynesian Phillips Curve model, managing expectations about future inflation and interest rates directly shapes how consumers and businesses spend money today.
  • Influencing Long-Term Rates: Central banks directly control only short-term, overnight interest rates. They must rely on communication to influence the long end of the yield curve, which dictates the rates for mortgages and government debt.
  • The Announcement Effect: If a central bank has established credibility, it can stabilize markets through speech alone without ever deploying capital.
      • The Draghi Precedent: Former ECB President Mario Draghi famously saved the euro in 2012 by stating he would do "whatever it takes" to preserve the currency. The emergency facility designed to back up his promise was created later but never actually used; the verbal commitment alone calmed the markets.
      • The Lagarde Precedent: Current ECB President Christine Lagarde stabilized pandemic-era market spreads by verbally committing to interest rate differentials before an official tool was ever formally built or utilized.

From Greenspan to Warsh: The Fed's Stylistic Evolution

  • The Greenspan Era (Deliberate Obscurity): Former Chair Alan Greenspan famously prided himself on being opaque and confusing, once stating, "If I've made myself too clear, you must have misunderstood me."
  • The Bernanke & Yellen Era (Democratic Transparency): Former Chairs Ben Bernanke and Janet Yellen introduced post-meeting press conferences and published quarterly economic projections to increase democratic accountability and align market expectations.
  • The Powell Era (Simplicity): Former Chair Jerome "Jay" Powell spoke colloquially, keeping his language at an eighth-grade reading level (on the Flesch-Kincaid scale) to ensure average American households could comprehend Fed policy.
  • The Warsh Era (Technical Rigor): Warsh represents a return to a more technical and formal style of communication, preferring exact economic terminology over simplified, colloquial explanations.

The Risks of Silence vs. Over-Communication

  • The Danger of Broken Promises: When central banks offer explicit forward guidance and are forced to pivot due to unexpected events, they severely damage their credibility.
      • In late 2021, ECB President Christine Lagarde promised that the bank would "almost certainly not" raise interest rates in 2022. Unexpected inflation forced the ECB to break that promise and raise rates aggressively, drawing criticism for reacting too slowly.
  • Filling the Vacuum: In the absence of central bank guidance, markets will search for and amplify any available signal, which can lead to misguided bets.
      • During Warsh’s first press conference, his heavy emphasis on inflation led markets to quickly price in faster interest rate hikes, even though the Fed's official statement remained neutral.
  • The Value of Price Discovery: Proponents of Warsh’s approach argue that increased market volatility is not inherently negative; rather, it reflects a healthy process of price discovery based on macroeconomic reality rather than central bank hand-holding.

***

Notable Quotes

"If you think about the Draghi magic and how Draghi is credited with saving the euro, it was essentially all talk and no action... He told markets, 'Believe me, it will be enough.' And the market believed it." — Balazs Koranyi, Reuters Chief ECB Correspondent
"Worse than not guiding markets is to guide markets to something and then you have to change your mind. Because then that starts to weaken commitment, it starts to weaken credibility, it starts to raise a whole sort of other problems." — Howard Schneider, Reuters Fed Correspondent
"If markets are digesting data in the economy and applying that to our policy, then... maybe a more volatile world is showing markets doing what they do, which is discover prices based on supply and demand conditions in the economy." — Howard Schneider, Reuters Fed Correspondent
Communication can be a tricky business. Words and phrases can be misconstrued, and context can change. It's a bit of a minefield, particularly if you're a central banker. What you say can move financial markets and affect how businesses and consumers behave, putting billions of dollars on the line. Kevin Warsh, the new chair of the Federal Reserve, wants the U.S. central bank to talk less. He's already made some big changes: shorter statements and no more forward guidance. That's central bank speak for telling people what they plan to do with interest rates. And there's more to come. He's launched a full review of how the Fed communicates. You could call it a silent revolution. But is less talk a good thing for central bankers? What are the risks of keeping quiet, particularly for the world's most important central bank? Let's try and find out. I'm Carmel Crimmins, and this is Reuters Econ World. Every week we go deep on the economic principles and ideas driving the biggest news around the world. On this week's show: Fed talk. Animals don't talk like us humans, but they've got plenty of ways to communicate. Peacocks fan their tails, elephants send low-frequency rumbles, and dogs—they howl. When it comes to human communication, central bankers are a special breed. They use language as an actual policy tool to steer the economy. The idea is to shape expectations about where the economy is going without causing panic. So the language can be technical, jargony, and highly stylized. A missing phrase or word can mean a major policy shift with billions of dollars riding on it, and decoding it is a science all of its own. So I'm happy today to say we've two world experts in interpreting central bank communication: Howard Schneider, our Federal Reserve correspondent, and Balazs Koranyi, our chief correspondent for the European Central Bank. Hey Howard, hey Balazs, how's it going? Carmel, you know, you're brilliant that we've had hawks and doves on inflation for all these decades and now we've got peacocks, elephants, and wolves or dogs to describe communications policy. It's a whole new continuum and way to describe these guys. Thank you, Howard. Howard and Balazs, you are both veterans of plenty of central bank meetings. How many do you think you've sat on? Oh gosh, a hundred or more. Well, last week was my 90th, but it's not like I'm counting. But who's counting? I mean, I kind of set it up there at the start like central bank language communication is super important. It's about setting expectations, isn't it, Balazs? And those expectations really matter. That's right. Central bank speak and what they want to do is set your expectations for the next meeting, and for six weeks from now and two years from now, and essentially steer the economy through talk. Now let me jump right into it and tell you that my experience from 90 ECB meetings is that I think they make it up as they go along. There are lots of rules and beautiful phrases, but in the end, they try to come up with something new every single time on the argument that we are living in extraordinary times. And extraordinary times challenge everything we know about central banking. But if you think about it, we've only lived in extraordinary times: the global financial crisis, then the European debt crisis, then the low-flation after China joined the WTO, then the pandemic, then Russia-Ukraine, now Iran. And essentially, if you think about it, it's never been normal. Or, what you can say is extraordinary is the normal and the good old days either never existed or they were the exception. So before we get into the idea of central banks busking, which I find fascinating and also slightly terrifying, let's take just a step back, right? So, just to explain to people, why are those expectations so important? Why does language matter, Howard, for central bankers? Well, yeah, they didn't really think it used to as much as they do now. Right, they're more important now. Yes, and look, there was sort of a revolution in monetary policy theory and practice beginning in kind of the '80s and '90s really, when expectations theory and the role of public psychology began to be more widely recognized. I mean, people were winning Nobel Prizes for research around this. And that's the idea that people's expectations affect how they behave and therefore affect prices. That's right. You act based on what you think the future's going to look like. So, if you think that the price of the car is going to go up, you know, 5% next year, you run out and buy the car now, which of course increases demand and pushes up prices. If you think the price—this is why deflation is so corrosive—if you think the price is going to fall 5% next year, everybody stops spending and the economy crashes. This is built formally into all of their models now. It's part of the, you know, what they call the New Keynesian Phillips Curve thinking, which has expectations built into it, and they feel that managing that is super important. I'd add one thing too, Balazs: it's not just about managing the future, it's about maintaining the inflation anchor as well. Central bank speak—this is why they so often say we will defend price stability, because they feel that they have to repeat that. They have to be very clear and explicit that that anchor will be defended at all costs. Balazs, even if they're making it up as they go along, which is interesting, let's get into that in a moment. What they say does matter. Like, sometimes isn't it fair to say sometimes markets will react more to what a central banker says than what they do? I think one thing, the key factor we should keep in mind, is that the central bank is going to set the interest rate for the very short term. So it's either going to be an overnight rate or two-week rate, something that's imminent. And the market sets the interest rate towards the long end of the curve. And that long interest rate matters for you when you borrow to finance your house; it matters for the government when they finance their debt. And so if you look at the curve, the longer end of the curve really matters a whole lot more for the people who do the actual borrowing. Yet the central bank only controls the short end. So they try to set expectations for the longer end of the curve and they try to influence the longer interest rate by communication itself. Now there have been instances in the past when they were directly influencing it by purchasing government bonds at the long end of the curve, but we called that an unconventional policy tool. So when it comes to normal operation, the central bank will try to guide you and convince you about the long-term interest rate rather than directly setting it. This kind of trend towards explaining every move and explaining to markets and economists, you know, the direction of travel—it's relatively a new phenomenon, isn't it? I mean, it was only in 1994, I think, that the Fed started actually publicly announcing its interest rate decisions immediately after its meetings. And I think it was a former Bank of England Governor, Norman Montague was his name, who said, "never explain, never excuse," right? So it's a relatively new phenomenon. Yeah, it is, and it has followed along the research and the thinking about expectations theory. And the more the importance of that has been realized, the more central bankers felt like, "Well, here is a really powerful lever we can use to make what we decide more effective." And yeah, the short end feeds into the long end, it doesn't control the long end. And you know, we saw in the various crises that even when interest rates were down at zero, they could talk them down further by making commitments into the future. The financial crisis also played a big role in this, didn't it, Balazs? We saw central bankers sort of, given everything that was happening on markets, feeling the need to actually talk directly to, frankly, the public as well as to traders and economists. And we saw that particularly in Europe with former ECB Governor Mario Draghi and his "whatever it takes" moment. If you think about the Draghi magic and how Draghi is credited with saving the euro, it was essentially all talk and no action. All Draghi said is, "we will do whatever it takes to preserve the euro." Did he actually do it? No, he didn't need to do it. In fact, when he said those words, the tools to carry out that action did not exist. So he was front-running policy in the public eye. He told markets, "believe me, it will be enough," and the market believed it. And only afterwards did Draghi actually go to the ECB and say, "Look, I made a commitment, now let's come up with the actual tool that is going to deliver on this commitment." So the ECB did; they created a facility to make whatever it takes happen. That facility has never been used. Fast forward a few years, there was similar turbulence in the market. Lagarde, as the new ECB president, was faced with kind of a similar situation. Draghi's tool was inappropriate for that, so Lagarde had to come out and make a different type of commitment about maintaining a certain interest rate differential between euro zone government debt. Again, there was no tool for that. They came up with that tool, and fast forward five, six years, that tool has never been used, but it did the job simply by talking about it. So doesn't that disprove then your idea that they're just making it up as they go along, right? Because Draghi was very intentional with that speech, with that "whatever it takes" moment. It's true, but Draghi didn't know how to make it happen. All he knew was that if he was going to be powerful enough in his words, chances are the markets were going to believe him. But he didn't have the actual tool to do it. And the same thing with Lagarde. She was thinking, "if I am very powerful in my commitment, then I probably don't even have to make it work because the market is going to believe me." And both of those, I think, are great experiments, not based on past practice. It's not a standard communication tool; it's not something that the strategy review said "this is what you should do in this situation." In both of those times, a powerful leader was improvising, and it worked, and the market never caught the bluff. But improvising—I've got to just... I think this is a different kettle of fish in a sense, because they are improvising around a very well-documented phenomenon, which is announcement effects. If a central bank has credibility—and the major ones do, they've won it over time—when they say they're going to do something, markets tend to react as if they believe them. This happened throughout the COVID crisis, and after Silicon Valley Bank too. The Fed announced program after program after program, and a lot of them got very little take-up because simply saying it'll be there and we'll do it fixed the problem. I think that's a different phenomenon than sort of the run-of-the-mill day-to-day forward guidance sort of communications, but both are super important. And one thing that always struck me with the Mario Draghi comment was it was really clear. Everyone could understand it, whether you were just an ordinary person on the street in Frankfurt or Berlin, or a trader sitting in New York or London. But traditionally, central bank communication—it's pretty turgid, isn't it? I mean, central bank speak is pretty wonky, Howard. Well, okay, so it depends on who it is, who's doing the speaking and who they're talking to. Yesterday I was thinking it was hot as heck and the sun was high. So if you ask Jay Powell, the former Fed chair, what the summer solstice is, he'd say, "Well, it's the longest day of the year, and interestingly, you know, it's the start of summer, but then we think of summer as long days but actually the amount of daylight's getting shorter day by day by day." Very colloquial, you know, "We'll keep an eye on that for all the American households." Ask Kevin Warsh, the new Fed chair, what the summer solstice is, and he'd say, "Well, it's when the axis of the planet has its northernmost tilt to the sun and the sun is directly overhead at the Tropic of Cancer at 23 degrees north latitude or something like that. Oh, and we've got a task force on that." Much more complex way of speaking. Okay, there is a Flesch-Kincaid score which you'll find in your Microsoft Word. Powell kept it down to about an eighth-grade level. Bernanke and Yellen tended to be late high school, the former Fed chairs. Powell kept it simple. Yeah, very simple. And that was a huge, massive break from the Alan Greenspan years, which were renowned for him speaking very opaquely. I mean, he kind of prided himself on that, right? I think didn't he say once, "If I've made myself too clear, you must have misunderstood me," right? Yes. And so that was the Greenspan era. Bernanke comes in, tries to make it more transparent, but what I'm hearing from you is he still kept it at quite a high level—big words were used—whereas Powell took it down and made it more simplistic. So Bernanke was a great innovator on this front, to the extent that he felt the more people have information, the better they understand what we're doing, the better policy will work. Plus there's a democratic issue as well: we should do this because we're accountable in some way, so people should understand it. So you have him to thank for press conferences after meetings, the summary of economic projections published every quarter—and that includes interest rate projections, the so-called dot plot that people are arguing about now—and then that advanced further under Yellen. Okay, quiz time. Russia's central bank governor, Elvira Nabiullina, is famous for wearing brooches after key policy meetings that are kind of clues to understand the policy decision. For instance, she once wore a pigeon brooch after cutting rates. Now the word for pigeon in Russian also means dove, and in central bank speak, a dove is a policymaker who typically favors lower rates to encourage borrowing and stimulate growth. Now can you name another public person who wears or wore brooches as public statements? Is it A: ECB President Christine Lagarde, B: former UK Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher, or C: former US Secretary of State Madeleine Albright? I'll have the answer for you at the end of the show. Up next: Why exactly does Kevin Warsh want the Fed to talk less? I mean, what Powell introduced in terms of making things more clearly understood—the regular press conference—like, that all sounds like good stuff. Why does Kevin Warsh want to roll that back? Why does he want the Fed to talk less, Howard? Well, I would say in a nutshell, it goes back to Balazs's point about the difference between short and long-term interest rates. Right, there's always a graph. Yeah, he doesn't really believe—he talked about this a little bit at his opening press conference last week—that there is no reason for the Fed to spoon-feed financial markets information and hold their hands. He believes that the rates on long-term bonds, for example, should be set by supply and demand conditions in the economy, not by us telling you to expect X, Y, or Z about the federal funds rate. You all can figure it out. I think he feels too that there's been a lot of both confusion, if you look at the amount of Fed speak there is. Now, the chair is obviously first among equals, so when the chair speaks, people tend to listen more. But all the reserve bank presidents, all the other governors, talk, talk, talk. What's the modal message? Sometimes it is hard to figure out. There's that, and plus I think there's a good dose of "we don't know what's going to happen," particularly right now. You know, it's one thing if the economy has crashed, cratered, interest rates are zero, you can pretty much tell people, "We're going to keep interest rates zero for a long, long time to come"—give them that forward guidance when things are in a crisis. In a non-crisis moment, we don't need to be saying this because we don't know what's going to happen. Tell me now, is inflation going to be 3.5% at the end of the year and we need to raise rates, or is demand going to fall and it's going to be, you know, 2% and we can cut rates? There are reasonable probabilities around each of those things, right? So why muddy the waters by saying you know what's going to happen? It ties your hands, it binds you. You know, worse than not guiding markets is to guide markets to something and then you have to change your mind. Because then that starts to weaken commitment, it starts to weaken credibility, it starts to raise a whole sort of other problems. So he's taking a more, "Look, normal times, we could go either way. Let's just stop talking about it" approach. But isn't it better just to communicate better? Like, doesn't the evidence show, Balazs, that if you kind of communicate clearly, you anchor expectations about inflation into the markets, into the economy, into consumers, and there's less volatility? Like, isn't it about communicating better? I think Howard was making a very important point here, and it goes back to the Draghi argument that we just had. As in, people believe Draghi because the central bank itself is credible. But if the central bank makes lots of forward guidance promises and then it has to break them because the world developed differently, then the credibility starts to erode. So then you're faced with a choice: A, break your own forward guidance, and then maybe your credibility is going to be gone the next time you need to say whatever it takes. Or follow through on the forward guidance and potentially make a policy mistake. And we can go into specific examples, because the ECB certainly had its share of policy mistakes because of forward guidance. Because they got caught out, because basically circumstances overtook them. Like they modeled in one way, thought one thing was going to happen, and the world proved them different. Is that—was that it? So let's look at a very specific example. We were in a super low inflation environment up until the pandemic, and we thought that would—that's never going to be over. In fact, the ECB made lots of commitments about interest rates and asset purchases well into the future, on the premise that we need to make that promise to make sure that financial instruments are priced in a certain way. Then comes inflation. It's coming out of left field. People thought, "No, no, no, this isn't real. You know, we're going to be back to deflation in no time." So the ECB makes a commitment. Fast forward to the end of 2021, when inflation was already rising and people were talking about rate hikes, and the ECB president still goes out into the public and says, "We will almost certainly not raise interest rates in the coming year." Because she thought market expectations were excessive. Now, hindsight is 20/20. We know that the ECB broke that promise and in fact raised interest rates more than in any other year because of what was coming. But at that point, for credibility reasons, the ECB had to maintain that promise, raised interest rates too late, and got itself into quite a bit of a mess. Why does it matter for today? You can say, "You know, we learned from mistakes, then we move on." But there's an argument that a central bank is always fighting the last crisis and not the next one. So here we are in 2026, we're facing inflation again. Is this temporary? Is this permanent? Is this going to pass? This is an oil shock. Oil shocks are not supposed to linger because they weigh on growth. So here's the ECB, raising interest rates, becoming the first central bank among the majors to raise interest rates. Last time it was the last one. And the argument is, people know they made a mistake in 2022, so they're going to jump the gun this time because surely they're not going to make that same mistake again. And along the way, it could turn out—maybe years from now when we do our PhDs on all this—that maybe the ECB made a mistake again, because it was fighting the last crisis and not the next one. But Howard, isn't no guidance worse than forward guidance that might be directing you in the wrong way? I mean, isn't there a risk that in the silence, people will make misguided bets, they will jump to assumptions, right? Well, I could say we're about to find out, because if you look at the market reaction after Warsh's first press conference—and I think this is a risk he will run, especially in this transition period, which will last however long it lasts. The market, globally—U.S., New York, bonds, stocks, whatever—will find a signal. They will decide what to trade on. And the immediate message they took from Kevin Warsh on Wednesday was that they're gonna hike rates probably sooner and more than we expect. Did he mean to communicate that? Some people think that was clearly his intent with the emphasis on inflation. The press conference came off as kind of hawkish. Did they say that in the statement? Eh, not so much. So was this something he meant to say in kind of an elliptical Greenspanian way, or was it just a mistake, that he really was trying to remain neutral on this question and let the data tell the story? I think that's something he's going to have to kind of decide where he wants to make his mistake, in a sense. If you like what you see, why not check out some of Reuters' other podcasts? We've got a special one for the 2026 World Cup called Reuters Pitchside. Check it out wherever you watch your podcasts. In the final part of the show, we look at what we can expect from Kevin Warsh's communications review. What can we expect, do you think, from this communication review, Howard? There is a lot in play. Warsh did a review for the Bank of England back in 2013, I think it was, on communications policy, and he basically said, "Say less, fight more," was the message. And he actually recommended they have some sessions where the tape recorder's shut off and there's no transcript. You know, there'll be minutes, but they'll be minutes of that portion, I think, but there isn't an actual transcript so people can speak freely because their views will never be attributed to them by name at some future point. And you know, he mentioned in his opening press conference this is going to include everything, including transcripts, which in the Fed's case—every word they say is recorded, taken down, transcribed, and released five years later. He thinks that even that, delayed five years down the road, recounting of what people said in the meeting inhibits debate, inhibits the flow of ideas, and makes policymaking worse. So this isn't just about a Fed speaker, you know, in the Midwest talking about interest rates in a way he doesn't like. This is a thoroughgoing look at everything that comes out of the central bank. On the summary of economic projections... Yeah, on the dot plot. So for people who mightn't be familiar, this is like a visual representation of Fed policymakers' expectations of where they think, individually, interest rates might go, right? Here's where the confusion is. That word "expectations"—it's not even that. Okay. It's actually a... each policymaker writes down where they think interest rates will be, if appropriate monetary policy is followed and the economy evolves as they think it will. But the criticism is, isn't it, that the market interprets it as expectations? As a forecast. And that—that's exactly the criticism. And the ECB—the ECB doesn't have a dot plot, right, Balazs? It doesn't. No, it doesn't. It's true. The thing about forward guidance is, it's great to say let the market set the guidance, but if you think about markets, you know, markets have made huge mistakes in the past. Markets are not the most accurate guides. Look at all the bubbles that have been blown and blown up over time. So it's great to say no forward guidance and we go meeting by meeting. It only works as long as you kind of agree with market pricing, because then at some point, you're going to feel a temptation to guide the market in any case. Plus, these are very large bodies. And if the market is not pricing what you individually think is the right setting, then you're going to start talking. And we just saw this with the ECB in the last policy meeting. We go meeting by meeting, no preset course—except when we want to give guidance, as before the last meeting a lot of policymakers came out and said, "No forward guidance, but I think we should raise interest rates." So it was a done deal even before people went into the meeting; it was all set. So I would say that's why I said they make it up as they go along, because it's meeting by meeting, until it's not. Balazs, the ECB communicates in a very different way to the Fed, doesn't it? The ECB is a very unique beast because it's not a single country, and rather it's 21 countries. So they cannot do some of the things that the Fed does. So for example, there are no minutes at the ECB and there can never be minutes. And it's simple: you're an ECB governor, you're representing the euro zone and not your own country. Once you start publishing minutes, then all of a sudden you're the Greek governor, and you're the Italian governor and the Dutch governor. And people start identifying your views with the needs of your own country; then you can no longer represent the euro zone as a whole. So the ECB releases these polished, manicured accounts of policy meetings where you cannot identify people, and a lot of the discussion is not even included in that. So the day before the policy meeting, all these people get together for seminars and dinner, and our understanding is that essentially the policy decision is pretty much a done deal by the end of the first day, when there's no recording and there are no accounts, no minutes, no nothing. So that is quite different. Sounds like the type of world maybe Kevin Warsh might like, Howard, at least in terms of not having any minutes. Well, I don't think he'd go so far as to not have minutes because, you know, there is an assumption in U.S. institutions that you account for what you do. So he'd have a big hurdle there. Second thing is it's a federal system, not a collection of independent states. So even though there are 12 regional reserve districts, the Dallas Fed president is not supposed to represent Dallas and Oklahoma, New Mexico; they're supposed to talk about monetary policy generally. So you would want to know if, for example, somebody said, "Yeah, we in Atlanta, we need rate cuts down in Atlanta, so I'm going to vote for rate cuts." That'd be very far afield from what you're supposed to do as a U.S. central banker. So I don't think he wants no minutes. We might see the end of the regular press conference, though, Howard, after the FOMC meeting, after the interest rate setting meeting. That's a risk, right? Yeah, well, it is. It's something he wants to look at, and he has said very explicitly, "I don't think you should talk unless you've got something to say." I think that's a line from a song, I can't remember what it is, though. But I think he's going to have problems doing away with it altogether, for example, because then if he ever does have a press conference, people are going to say, "Wow, something must have happened." Once you take things out of a regular schedule and kind of make them at your discretion completely, people will attach an awful lot of weight to them. The advantage right now is that even though any given press conference may have more or less value-add in terms of the flow of questions and the understanding derived about the Fed, the meeting, the economy, and monetary policy, they kind of come and go now, and nobody really thinks about it. The problem Bernanke had, for example, even in doing it every quarter, you know, at every other meeting, right? They meet eight times a year. Bernanke did a quarterly press conference around the release of the projections. It set a very firm expectation in markets that they would only move interest rates at meetings where there was a press conference. So even that little differentiation seemed to tie their hands. Powell did away with that by going in the direction of having a press conference every meeting. Many people think that even if he wants to do away with this, it's become such a part of the job right now, and it's so useful in terms of delivering the communication, amplifying what he wants to amplify, dialing back what he wants to dial back, that he probably wouldn't be well-served as a chairman by doing away with it altogether. Balazs, what's the potential impact here for the rest of the world? I mean, we all look to the Fed; it's the world's most important central bank. If it shakes up, if it communicates less, what's the potential implication? It's an interesting question, because if you think about the greatest innovations in modern central banking, they tended to be coming from some of the smaller central banks. Inflation targeting is a kiwi idea that we imported into modern central banking. So I have a hard time imagining that it's going to be the Fed that revolutionizes things, because I think central banks move in small increments. Whether you hold a press conference or not, that's probably not a revolution in central banking. But having said that, the Fed is the largest beast in this business. Whatever the Fed does has an impact on everybody out there. But what I would say is that communication has been in flux ever since I've covered this field. So there will be changes at the Fed, at the ECB—it's going to be changing all the time because the world that we live in is changing. There is something to be said for the fact that the environment is changing far faster than what we were used to. So obviously communication changes, but I just don't see this as drama. I see it as kind of an incremental evolution, and it's natural for a new Fed chair to change how he works. Potential drama on markets, though, Howard? Well, you saw some last week when, you know, the pricing moved real fast even when he said, "I'm not telling you what we're going to do." So yes, I think there's a potential for more volatility. But is that a bad thing? The Warsh view of this might be that listen, if markets are digesting data in the economy and applying that to our policy, then—and this is what we get—maybe a more volatile world is showing markets doing what they do, which is discover prices based on supply and demand conditions in the economy, not based on what some institution that is supposed to be regulating one aspect of one thing says on any given day. A big thank you to Balazs and Howard for their time and insight. You can catch their work on the Reuters app and reuters.com. Now for that quiz: I asked you to name another public person, other than Russia's central bank governor, who wears or wore brooches as public statements. Was it A: ECB President Christine Lagarde, B: former UK Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher, or C: former US Secretary of State Madeleine Albright? And the answer is C: Madeleine Albright. Albright's use of her jewelry as a means of expression was born out of a conflict with Iraq's Saddam Hussein after the Gulf War in '94, when she criticized the Iraqi leader for failing to cooperate with UN weapons inspectors. She was referred to in the government-controlled Iraqi press as an unparalleled serpent. When she returned home, she found a serpent brooch to wear on her next trip to Iraq. And from then on, jewelry became part of her personal diplomatic arsenal. So she wore a wasp pin to deliver a tough message to then-Palestinian leader Yasser Arafat, and a welcoming sun when greeting South Korean President Kim Dae-jung. This is Reuters Econ World. FULL GAGGLE: Rubio Takes Questions From Reporters Amid Meetings With Gulf Leaders Over Iran Deal

Forbes Breaking News(5.6M)·11:53·44.2K views·2026-06-24
Marco RubioSecretary of State, United States
TL;DRSecretary of State Marco Rubio reaffirmed the United States' concrete security commitments to Gulf allies during ongoing nuclear negotiations with Iran, emphasizing that any final deal must be robust, international waterways must remain free of tolls, and the U.S.

TL;DR

Secretary of State Marco Rubio reaffirmed the United States' concrete security commitments to Gulf allies during ongoing nuclear negotiations with Iran, emphasizing that any final deal must be robust, international waterways must remain free of tolls, and the U.S. remains prepared to leverage sanctions if commitments are broken.

Gulf Security and the Iran Negotiations

  • Concrete Security Assurances: Secretary Rubio emphasized that U.S. security guarantees to Gulf allies are backed by active military assets, air defenses, and troop presence on the ground, rather than mere diplomatic promises.
  • Negotiation Alignment: The U.S. is consulting closely with the Gulf Cooperation Council (GCC) to ensure no agreements undermine the security of regional allies.
  • Technical Talks Resuming: The next phase of nuclear negotiations will resume at the staff/technical level in Switzerland around the 30th of the month.
    • Specialized workgroups from the State Department and Department of Energy will handle specific areas, including nuclear energy and sanctions.
  • Temporary Sanctions Relief: The U.S. waived oil sanctions against Iran for a strict 60-day period as part of the negotiation process.
    • Consequences of Non-Compliance: If Iran fails to meet its Switzerland commitments, President Biden retains options to immediately reinstate or expand sanctions.

Freedom of Navigation and Regional Border Security

  • Strait of Hormuz: Rubio flatly rejected an Iranian proposal to charge transit fees in the Strait of Hormuz, asserting that international waterways must remain free and open.
    • Global Consensus: He stated that GCC members and the broader international community are united in opposition to tolling international shipping lanes.
  • Israel-Lebanon Border Talks: The U.S. is mediating negotiations in Washington to transition security control in southern Lebanon from Hezbollah to the Lebanese Armed Forces (LAF).
    • Pilot Zones: Negotiations include establishing specific, defined "pilot zones" where the LAF can progressively secure territory.
    • Israeli Withdrawal: Rubio noted that Israel's military presence is solely a defensive response to Hezbollah drone and rocket attacks, and Israel holds no permanent territorial claims in Lebanon.

Global Oil Waivers and Detainee Policy

  • Russian Oil Waivers: The administration may choose not to renew temporary waivers on Russian oil if global market prices continue to drop and stabilize.
  • Wrongfully Detained Americans: Securing the release of American citizens remains a top priority, coordinated by the Special Presidential Envoy for Hostage Affairs (SPEHA).
    • Rubio declined to comment on specific active cases in Iran to avoid complicating sensitive diplomatic efforts.

Humanitarian Crisis in Sudan

  • Diplomatic Pressure: The U.S. is actively engaging with regional partners—specifically Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates (UAE)—to urge all parties in Sudan to adhere to agreements made in Berlin.
  • Key Objectives: The administration is pushing for a ceasefire and the opening of humanitarian corridors to address a catastrophic humanitarian crisis. Special Envoy Masad Boulos is leading daily engagement on this front.

Notable Quotes

"The security assurances lie in the fact that... they know we've been there with them and they've been there with us. We have people there, we have people on the ground constantly working with them, we have air defenses embedded with them, we have aircraft in these countries... They're not promises, they're actual. They exist." — *Secretary of State Marco Rubio*
"I think the whole world will be against any mechanism that charges money to use an international waterway. It's that simple. The president's already said that's not going to happen... I know of no country on the planet that supports tolling or a fee for the use of the straits." — *Secretary of State Marco Rubio*
"This is a temporary measure, it's for 60 days. Um, and as a result, we expect them to live up to the commitments they made in Switzerland. If they don't live up to those commitments, the president has a lot of options at his disposal, including... reversing these sanctions." — *Secretary of State Marco Rubio*
"We want to see a Lebanon that's in the control of their legitimate government, where there is no foreign government encroaching on their territory... If Hezbollah wasn't launching against [Israel], they wouldn't be there." — *Secretary of State Marco Rubio*

More Content, Less Learning

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·33:21·416 views·2026-06-24
Salman KhanFounder, Khan Academy Hadi PartoviCEO, Code.org Zanny Minton BeddoesEditor-in-Chief, The Economist
TL;DRWhile digital tools and AI have democratized access to information, true education requires active cognitive friction and real-world application rather than passive content consumption.
To improve global learning outcomes, educational systems must shift away from...

TL;DR

While digital tools and AI have democratized access to information, true education requires active cognitive friction and real-world application rather than passive content consumption. To improve global learning outcomes, educational systems must shift away from grade-centric metrics and instead focus on human-mentored, impact-driven environments.

The Gym Metaphor: Information Access vs. Cognitive Friction

  • The Fallacy of the Transmission Model: Merely providing students with access to high-quality lectures does not translate into actual learning.
      • Professor Andrew Maynard noted that the traditional "transmission model"—assuming that stuffing information into a student's head leads to learning—has failed for decades.
  • The "Gym" Metaphor for Learning: Rahul Attuluri compared digital learning platforms to physical fitness.
      • No Pain, No Gain: Just as watching workout videos without exercising yields no physical benefit, merely watching educational content without active problem-solving yields no cognitive growth.
      • Cognitive Gyms: Attuluri suggested that as AI increasingly offloads routine thinking tasks, society may need to establish "cognitive gyms" dedicated purely to keeping human minds active and sharp.
  • The Value of Constructive Friction: Students do not want meaningless "busy work," but they actively crave challenging assignments that have a clear purpose. Learning only occurs when students push against constructive educational friction.

The Failure of Grade-Centric Assessments

  • Disconnect with Real-World Success: Current educational models prioritize grading systems over actual capability.
      • GPA Irrelevance: Professor Vanessa Chan argued that grade point averages (GPAs) have zero correlation with real-world impact or career success. Grade metrics are often artificial assessments based on meeting subjective, randomized goals.
      • Gaming the System: Maynard warned that because students recognize grades are merely a game, they are highly incentivized to use digital tools to take shortcuts, bypassing deeper comprehension.
  • Mastery-Based Learning as an Alternative: Transitioning to mastery-based systems focuses on whether a student understands and can apply a concept, rather than forcing them to progress through fixed, time-bound modules.

The Human Element in the Age of AI

  • Why AI Cannot Replace Teachers: While AI agents can function as highly accessible tutoring assistants, they cannot replace the human educator.
      • The Path of Least Resistance: AI platforms are optimized for engagement and ease, which naturally encourages the "path of least resistance." Conversely, real learning requires a human coach or mentor to push students past their comfort zones.
      • Loss of Knowledge Retention: A cited study indicated that while students using agentic AI performed better on short-term assessments, their long-term knowledge retention was significantly lower than those taught by humans.
  • The Importance of Human Connection: Human-to-human relationships remain critical for networking, professional mentorship, and validation. Maynard shared a student anecdote: while AI can write a technically proficient letter of recommendation, it lacks the human substance and provenance required by employers.

Pedagogical Innovation in Action

  • Boosting Revenue through AI Value Engineering (BRAVE): NextWave introduced the "BRAVE" initiative to shift students out of their academic comfort zones.
      • Real-World Metrics: Rather than testing students on code, the program tasks them with utilizing agentic AI to solve real operational problems for local small and medium enterprises (SMEs). Students are graded on the actual revenue they generate for those businesses.
  • Applying Content at the University of Pennsylvania: Professor Vanessa Chan utilizes unconventional classroom strategies to build essential soft skills and real-world resourcefulness in Penn Engineering freshmen:
      • The Tennis Ball Rule: To balance classroom participation, Chan implements a rule where students must hold a tennis ball to speak, preventing over-talkers from dominating and encouraging quiet students to find their voice.
      • The Penn Campus Scavenger Hunt: In their third week, freshmen are sent across campus to find physical problems and pitch material science solutions. Despite having no formal materials science training yet, students are pushed to use ChatGPT and creative thinking to construct their pitches, learning to embrace failure and feedback.
  • Shifting from Joseph Swan to Thomas Edison: Chan noted that Joseph Swan actually invented the light bulb filament, but Thomas Edison successfully deployed it at scale by understanding manufacturing, utility infrastructure, and consumer economics. Modern education must focus on cultivating "Edisons"—innovators who can navigate complex ecosystems to drive real change.

***

Notable Quotes

"While AI has made it much more easier to offload the thinking task... for our own mental well-being, especially our cognitive well-being, we should still involve ourselves into learning. Maybe in future we'll have cognitive gyms all across [where] people... are harnessing our cognitive ability." — Rahul Attuluri
"If you can combine the accessibility of knowledge with the friction that actually creates value, that's where you begin to get learning." — Professor Andrew Maynard
"We focus on the content, but we don't ask ourselves how do we apply that content to solve problems... AI is able to bring so much information in that we just aren't able to do... where the genius comes from is can you find the connections and the insights for how that information should change things." — Professor Vanessa Chan
